Identifying and Decomposing the Specific Conditional
Defines the specific conditional and explains its composite structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a specific conditional proposition and break it down into its two component propositions.

Core Idea
The specific conditional is a composite proposition: a general conditional plus a denial of permanence.

We already learned about the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma), which affirms a connection between two attributes 'as long as' the subject has a certain description. Now we introduce a more precise version: the specific conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa). It adds the restriction 'not always' (lā dāʾiman), thereby denying that the judgment holds permanently. This makes it a composite proposition, and understanding its structure is essential for correctly interpreting modal statements in Islamic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the marker 'not always'
Look for the phrase 'lā dāʾiman' (لا دائما) added to a general conditional. This is what distinguishes the specific conditional from the general. Without it, the proposition is merely general.
The marker explicitly negates permanence of the subject's description.
2
Identify the two component propositions
The specific conditional is composite. For a positive example, it consists of: (1) a positive general conditional (the first part, or ṣadr), and (2) a negative general absolute (the second part, or ʿajz) which is equivalent to saying 'not always'.
The second part is always a negation of a non-modal absolute proposition.
3
Determine the overall quality
The overall proposition takes its quality (positive or negative) from the first part. If the first part is positive, the whole is positive; if the first part is negative, the whole is negative. The second part is always a negation.
This is because the first part is the main judgment, and the second part only adds a restriction.
4
Construct your own example
Choose a subject and a description, then form a general conditional (e.g., 'Every scribe is writing as long as he is a scribe') and add 'not always' to show the description is not permanent. Verify that the composite matches the structure: positive general conditional + negative absolute.
Make sure the second part negates the same predicate used in the first part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمَشْرُوطَةُ الخَاصَّةُ وَهِيَ : المَشْرُوطَةُ العَامَّةُ لَكِنْ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ قَيْدِ : لَا دَائِماً ٠ » ؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ كَاتِبِ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً لَا دَائِماً ، وَسَالِبَةٌ: «لَا شَيْءٌ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِبَا لَا دَائِماً». وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : مَشْرُوطَةٌ»؛ لِمَا مَرَّ ، وَ خَاصَّةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهَا أَخَصُّ مِنَ المَشْرُوطَةِ العَامَّةِ كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ، وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ : إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ مِنْ : مَشْرُوطَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ؛ أَعْني قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّلُ الأصابع بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْرُ ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : «لَا دَائِماً»، فَإِنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةٍ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «لَا شَيْءٌ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِمُتَحَرِّكِ الأَصَابِعِ
And the specific conditional: it is the general conditional but with the addition of the restriction 'not always'. Its positive example: Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing, not always. And negative: No writer is still in his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing, not always. It is called 'conditional' for the reason mentioned, and 'specific' because it is more specific than the general conditional as understood from what preceded. And it is composite: if it is positive, it is composed of a positive general conditional which is the first part, i.e., your saying for example 'Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing', and a negative general absolute which is the second part, i.e., your saying 'not always', for it is equivalent to saying 'No writer is moving his fingers'.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage defines the specific conditional proposition (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa). The author begins by stating that it is essentially the general conditional with the addition of the restriction 'not always' (lā dāʾiman). Thus, it explicitly negates the permanence of the description (i.e., the description does not persist with the essence of the subject). Examples are given: positive: 'Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing, not always' and negative: 'No writer is still in his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing, not always.' The name 'conditional' is explained as before (due to the condition of the duration of the description), and 'specific' because it is more specific than the general conditional, as it adds a restriction that removes the possibility of permanence. The author then explains its composite nature: a positive specific conditional is composed of two parts: (1) a positive general conditional (the 'first part' or ṣadr), e.g., 'Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing', and (2) a negative general absolute proposition (al-muṭlaqa al-ʿāmma al-sāliba) which is the 'second part' or ʿajz, i.e., the phrase 'not always', which is equivalent to saying 'No writer is moving his fingers' (i.e., a negative absolute). This shows that the specific conditional denies that the judgment holds always, thereby making it a composite proposition. The commentary by al-Anbābī further clarifies that the whole proposition is judged as positive or negative based on its first part, and the second part is opposite in quality but same in quantity.

Key Terms
مشروطة خاصة
لا دائماً
مطلقة عامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two propositions compose a positive specific conditional according to the text?
A) A positive general conditional and a negative general absolute.
B) A positive general conditional and a positive general absolute.
C) A negative general conditional and a negative general absolute.
D) A positive specific conditional and a negative specific conditional.

Key Takeaway
The specific conditional is a composite modal proposition that adds 'not always' to the general conditional, making it more precise by denying permanence of the subject's description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Given the negative specific conditional 'No animal is not moving necessarily as long as it is alive, not always', identify its two component propositions. Write them out clearly, labeling which is the first part (ṣadr) and which is the second part (ʿajz).
?
Task 2: Construct a positive specific conditional using a different subject-predicate pair from Islamic sciences (e.g., 'Every scholar is knowledgeable necessarily as long as he studies, not always'). Then decompose it into its two parts and verify that the second part is indeed a negative absolute proposition.

The Particular Conditional (al-Mashrutah al-Khassah)
Defines the particular conditional and explains its composition for affirmative and negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a particular conditional proposition and decompose it into its constituent parts: an affirmative general conditional and a negative general absolute (for affirmative) or a negative general conditional and an affirmative general absolute (for negative).

Core Idea
The particular conditional adds 'not always' to the general conditional, making it more restrictive.

The particular conditional is a key type of conditional proposition in Islamic logic. It is formed by adding the qualifier 'not always' to a general conditional. This lesson will show you how to recognize and analyze such propositions by breaking them into their two components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qualifier
Look for the phrase 'not always' (la da'iman) at the end of the proposition. This indicates it is a particular conditional.
2
Determine the quality (affirmative or negative)
Check the judgment in the first part: if it affirms the predicate, it's affirmative; if it negates, it's negative.
3
Extract the general conditional part
Separate the part before 'not always' – this is a general conditional (e.g., 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer').
4
Translate 'not always' into a general absolute
For an affirmative particular, 'not always' becomes a negative general absolute (e.g., 'No writer is moving fingers with general absolute'). For a negative particular, it becomes an affirmative general absolute.
The general absolute is a universal proposition that holds 'in general' (bi-al-ijmal al-'amm), not necessarily always.
5
Verify the composition rule
The first part (sadr) and the second part (ʿajz) always have opposite quality but the same quantity (both universal).
This ensures that the composite proposition correctly captures the non-permanence of the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمَشْرُوطَةُ الخَاصَّةُ وَهِيَ : المَشْرُوطَةُ العَامَّةُ لَكِنْ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ قَيْدِ : لَا دَائِماً ٠ » ؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ كَاتِبِ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً لَا دَائِماً ، وَسَالِبَةٌ: «لَا شَيْءٌ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِبَا لَا دَائِماً». وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : مَشْرُوطَةٌ»؛ لِمَا مَرَّ ، وَ خَاصَّةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهَا أَخَصُّ مِنَ المَشْرُوطَةِ العَامَّةِ كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ، وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ (۲) : إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ مِنْ : مَشْرُوطَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ؛ أَعْني قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّلُ الأصابع بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْرُ ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : «لَا دَائِماً»، فَإِنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةٍ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «لَا شَيْءٌ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِمُتَحَرِّكِ الأَصَابِعِ الأنبابي مدة دوام وصفه، وهو الكتابة؛ إذ سلب سكون الأصابع عن ذات الكاتب من غير اعتبار وصفه ليس بضروري. قوله : «لَا دَائِماً) أي : ليس الوصف دائماً بدوام ذات الموضوع. قوله : (كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ) أي: قيدت بما يرفع احتمال دوام الوصف للذَّات، بخلاف العامة، فإنَّها تحتمل الدوام وعدمه . قوله : (وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ ... إلخ) من هنا تبيَّن أنَّ الاعتبار في إيجاب القضية المركبة وسلبها بإيجاب جزئها الأوّل وسلبه، فإن كان موجباً كانتِ القضيَّة موجبةٌ ، وإن كان سالباً كانت سالبة، وأنَّ الجزء الثاني مخالف للأوَّل في الإيجاب والسَّلب موافق في الكلية والجزئية . اهـ (صبَّان» (۳) قوله : (فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ ... إلخ) هي الأولى مِنَ المطلقات الثلاثة الآتية. قوله : (فَإِنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ... إلخ) هذه القضيَّة سالبة كليَّةٌ مطلقة؛ لأنَّ سلب الحكم عن جميع أفراد الكاتب حاصل بالفعل في بعض الأوقات، لا كلّها ؛ لأنَّه المحقق . (۱) الشرشيمي : قوله : (لَا دَائِماً ) أي : ليس ذلك الحكم دائماً به دوام الذات، بل هو دائم بدوام الوصف؛ الذي قد ينقطع، فكذلك الحكم، وكذا يقال فيما بعد. (۲) الشرشيمي : قوله : (وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ ... إلخ) اعلم : أنَّ العبرة في كون القضيَّة موجبة أو سالبة بصدرها، والمطلقة العامة تخالفها في الكيف، لا في الكم. (۳) انظر : حاشية الصبان على الشرح الصغير للملوي ) ص: (٣٦٩ (٥٦) وَالأَوَّلُ: «المَوضُوعُ بِالحَمْلِيَّة وَالآخِرُ المَحْمُولُ بِالسَّوِيَّة الباجوري .(١) بالإطلاقِ العَامُ ) ; أَيْ : بِالفِعْلِ ; لأنَّ إِيجَابَ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوعِ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ دَائِماً , كَانَ السَّلْبُ في الجُمْلَةِ, وَهَذَا هُوَ مَعْنَى المُطْلَقَةِ العَامَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ . مُتَحَقِّقاً (۲) - وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ : مِنْ مَشْرُوطَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ (٢) بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً, فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْزُ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : «لا دَائِماً»); لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: كُلُّ كَاتِبِ سَاكِنُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالإِطْلَاقِ العام»; لِأَنَّ سَلْبَ المَحْمُولِ عَنِ المَوْضُوعِ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ دَائِماً كَانَ الإِيجَابُ مُتَحَقِّقاً فِي الجُمْلَةِ, وَهَذَا هُوَ مَعْنَى المُطْلَقَةِ العَامَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ .
And the particular conditional: it is the general conditional but with the addition of the qualifier 'not always'. Its example for affirmative: 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always.' And for negative: 'No writer is necessarily still of fingers as long as he is a writer, not always.' And it is called 'conditional' for what has passed, and 'particular' because it is more specific than the general conditional as known from what has passed. And it is composite: if it is affirmative, it consists of an affirmative general conditional as the first part – I mean your saying for example 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' – and a negative general absolute as the last part – I mean your saying 'not always', for it is in the power of saying 'No writer is moving fingers with general absolute'; because if the affirmation of the predicate for the subject is not permanent, then the negation obtains in general, and this is the meaning of the negative general absolute. And if it is negative, it consists of a negative general conditional as the first part – I mean your saying for example 'No writer is necessarily still of fingers as long as he is a writer' – and an affirmative general absolute as the last part – I mean your saying 'not always', for it is in the power of saying 'Every writer is still of fingers with general absolute'; because if the negation of the predicate from the subject is not permanent, then the affirmation obtains in general, and this is the meaning of the affirmative general absolute.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage defines the particular conditional (al-mashrutah al-khassah). It begins by stating that it is the same as the general conditional but with the addition of the qualifier 'not always' (la da'iman). The author provides examples: for affirmative, 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always.' For negative, 'No writer is necessarily still of fingers as long as he is a writer, not always.' The name 'particular' is explained as being more specific than the general conditional because the added qualifier eliminates the possibility of the permanence of the attribute for the subject. The passage then explains the composite nature of this proposition. For an affirmative particular conditional, it consists of two parts: the first part (sadr) is an affirmative general conditional, and the second part (ʿajz) is a negative general absolute. The 'not always' is interpreted as a negative general absolute: 'No writer is moving fingers with general absolute.' The reasoning is that if the affirmative judgment is not permanent, then the negation holds in general. For a negative particular conditional, the first part is a negative general conditional, and the second part is an affirmative general absolute: 'Every writer is still of fingers with general absolute.' The reasoning is that if the negative judgment is not permanent, then the affirmative holds in general. The passage also includes various marginal notes clarifying points: the qualification 'not always' refers to the lack of permanence of the attribute with the subject, not to the duration of the subject itself; the composition shows that the truth value of the composite proposition is determined by its first part, and the second part always has the opposite quality but same quantity. This is a key definitional passage in the study of conditional propositions.

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصة
المشروطة العامة
قضية مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A particular conditional affirmative 'Every writer is moving fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, not always' is composed of which two parts?
A) An affirmative general conditional and a negative general absolute
B) A negative general conditional and an affirmative general absolute
C) An affirmative general conditional and an affirmative general absolute
D) A negative general conditional and a negative general absolute

Key Takeaway
The particular conditional is built by adding 'not always' to a general conditional, and its composition always pairs a general conditional with a general absolute of opposite quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an affirmative particular conditional on a fiqh-related topic (e.g., 'Every payer of zakat is necessarily fulfilling an obligation as long as he is a payer, not always') and decompose it into its two parts: the general conditional and the general absolute.
?
Construct a negative particular conditional (e.g., 'No scholar is necessarily ignorant as long as he is a scholar, not always') and decompose it, identifying the quality of each part and verifying the composition rule.

Absolute Temporal Propositions (al-waqtiyyah al-mutlaqah)
Defines the absolute temporal proposition with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute temporal proposition and distinguish it from a non-absolute temporal proposition by checking for the qualifier 'not always'.

Core Idea
The absolute temporal judges necessity at a specific time but leaves out 'not always'.

In this passage, the author defines the absolute temporal proposition. It is a judgment of necessity restricted to a specific time, but without any further qualification that would indicate it does not hold at all times. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing temporal modalities in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time restriction
Look for a phrase specifying a particular time (e.g., 'at the time of writing' or 'at the time of prayer'). This makes the proposition temporal. For instance, 'Every worshiper is necessarily facing the qibla at the time of prayer' has a clear time restriction.
The passage uses 'at the time of writing' as an example.
2
Check for the qualifier 'not always'
See if the proposition includes the phrase 'not always' (la da'iman). If it does not, it is absolute temporal. In the example above, there is no 'not always', so it is absolute.
3
Recognize its simple composition
The absolute temporal is simple (basitah); it does not contain an additional proposition. It is a single judgment of necessity at that time.
The passage states it is simple as will come.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٤) - وَالوَقْتِيَّةُ المُطْلَقَةُ وَهِيَ : الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ فِي وَقْتٍ مُعَيَّنِ؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوْجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتَ الكِتَابَةِ، وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِسَاكِنِ الْأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتَ الكِتَابَةِ» . وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : وَقْتِيَّةٌ» ; لِلتَّقْيِيدِ فِيهَا بِالوَقْتِ، وَ مُطْلَقَةٌ»; لإطلاقِهَا عَنِ التَّقْيِيدِ (۳) بِقَوْلِنَا : لَا دَائِماً»، وَهِيَ بَسِيطةٌ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي. (٥) - وَالوَقْتِيَّةُ غَيْرُ المُطْلَقَةِ وَهِيَ : «الوَقْتِيَّةُ المُطْلَقَةُ لَكِنْ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ قَيْدِ لَا دَائِماً»; مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتَ الكِتَابَةِ لا دَائِماً»، وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءٌ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتَ الكِتَابَةِ لَا دَائِماً » . وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : وَقْتِيَّةٌ» ; لِمَا مَرَّ ، وَ غَيْرَ مُطْلَقَةٍ»; لِأَنَّهَا مُقَيَّدَةٌ بِقَوْلِنَا : لَا دَائِماً»، وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ : : إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ مِنْ : وَقْتِيَّةٍ مُطْلَقَةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأصابع بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقتَ الكِتَابَةِ، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْرُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : «لَا دَائِماً»; لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَحَرِّكِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالإِطْلَاقِ العَامِّ»; لِمَا مَرَّ مِنْ أَنَّ إِيجَابَ
(4) The absolute temporal: it is that in which is judged the necessity of the relation in a specific time. Its example for affirmative: 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing'; and for negative: 'No human is necessarily still of fingers at the time of writing.' And it is called 'temporal' because of the restriction to time, and 'absolute' because of its freedom from the restriction (3) by our saying 'not always', and it is simple as will come. (5) The non-absolute temporal: it is the absolute temporal but with the addition of the qualifier 'not always'. Its example for affirmative: 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing, not always'; and for negative: 'No human is necessarily still of fingers at the time of writing, not always.' And it is called 'temporal' for what has passed, and 'non-absolute' because it is restricted by our saying 'not always', and it is composite: if it is affirmative, it consists of an affirmative absolute temporal as the first part – I mean your saying for example 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing' – and a negative general absolute as the last part – I mean your saying 'not always', for it is in the power of saying 'No human is moving fingers with general absolute'; because, as has passed, the affirmation of the predicate for the subject if it is not permanent, then the negation obtains in general.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines two types of temporal propositions. First, the absolute temporal (al-waqtiyyah al-mutlaqah) is a proposition that judges the necessity of the relation at a specific time. Examples: affirmative 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing' and negative 'No human is necessarily still of fingers at the time of writing.' It is called 'temporal' because it is restricted to a time, and 'absolute' because it is not further restricted by adding 'not always'; it is simple in composition. Second, the non-absolute temporal (al-waqtiyyah ghayr al-mutlaqah) is the same absolute temporal but with the addition of 'not always'. Examples: affirmative 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing, not always' and negative 'No human is necessarily still of fingers at the time of writing, not always.' It is called 'non-absolute' because it is indeed restricted by the qualifier 'not always', making it composite. The passage then explains the composition of the affirmative non-absolute temporal: it consists of an affirmative absolute temporal as the first part (sadr) and a negative general absolute as the second part (ʿajz), derived from 'not always' which is equivalent to 'No human is moving fingers with general absolute.' The reasoning, as before, is that if the affirmative judgment is not permanent, its negation holds in general. The composition for the negative would follow analogously. This passage is a key expansion on temporal modalities.

Key Terms
الوقتية المطلقة
الوقتية غير المطلقة

Key Takeaway
Absolute temporal propositions are simple judgments of necessity at a specific time, free from the qualifier 'not always'.

Non-Absolute Temporal Propositions (al-waqtiyyah ghayr al-mutlaqah)
Defines the non-absolute temporal proposition and explains its composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decompose a non-absolute temporal proposition into its two components: an absolute temporal and a negative general absolute.

Core Idea
The non-absolute temporal adds 'not always' to the absolute, making it composite.

The passage extends the concept of temporal propositions by introducing the non-absolute temporal. This type adds the qualifier 'not always' to the absolute temporal, indicating that the necessity holds at the specified time but not permanently. The author then analyzes its composition, showing it is made of an affirmative absolute temporal and a negative general absolute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the non-absolute temporal
Look for the presence of 'not always' (la da'iman) added to an absolute temporal statement. For example, 'Every worshiper is necessarily facing the qibla at the time of prayer, not always.' This indicates non-absolute.
The passage gives the example: 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing, not always.'
2
Separate the two parts
Split the proposition into the absolute temporal part (the 'sadr') and the negation of permanence (the 'ʿajz'). The sadr is 'Every worshiper is necessarily facing the qibla at the time of prayer'. The ʿajz is 'not always'.
The passage calls the absolute part the 'sadr' and the negative part the 'ʿajz'.
3
Understand the equivalence
Recognize that 'not always' is equivalent to a general negative: 'No worshiper is facing the qibla with general absolute' (meaning not at all times). The passage explains this equivalence.
The passage states: 'it is in the power of saying: No human is moving fingers with general absolute'.

Defining the General Absolute Proposition (al-Mutlaqa al-Amma)
Author defines the general absolute proposition with its positive and negative forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general absolute proposition and distinguish it from permanent propositions.

Core Idea
The general absolute means the predicate holds at least once, not necessarily always.

In this passage, al-Bajuri explains the meaning of 'general absolute' (al-mutlaqa al-amma), a key type of modal proposition in Islamic logic. He defines both its affirmative and negative forms using the concept of 'actuality' (bi al-fi'l). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting propositions in kalam, fiqh, and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Find the phrase 'بالإطلاق العام؛ أي: بالفعل'. This tells you that 'general absolute' means the proposition is true in actuality, not necessarily at all times.
2
Analyze the negative form
The author explains: if the predicate is not affirmed permanently, then it is denied in a general sense. The example 'No writer is still-fingered necessarily as long as he is writing' means that during the act of writing, it is not necessary that the writer is still-fingered. So the general absolute negative asserts that the predicate is not permanently true.
The negative form is equivalent to 'not always'.
3
Analyze the affirmative form
Similarly, if the denial of the predicate is not permanent, then affirmation holds in general. The author gives the equivalent: 'Every writer is still-fingered with the general absolute' means that at some times (e.g., when not writing), the writer is still-fingered. So the affirmative general absolute asserts that the predicate holds at least once.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider the proposition 'Zakat is due on wealth'. It is not due at all times, only when wealth reaches nisab, so it is a general absolute affirmative. This helps internalize the concept.
Contrast with permanent propositions like 'Allah is eternal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥٦) وَالأَوَّلُ: «المَوضُوعُ بِالحَمْلِيَّة وَالآخِرُ المَحْمُولُ بِالسَّوِيَّة". الباجوري: (بالإطلاقِ العَامُ)؛ أَيْ: بِالفِعْلِ؛ لأنَّ إِيجَابَ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوعِ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ دَائِماً، كَانَ السَّلْبُ في الجُمْلَةِ، وَهَذَا هُوَ مَعْنَى المُطْلَقَةِ العَامَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ. - وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ: مِنْ مَشْرُوطَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْزُ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ: «لا دَائِماً»؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: كُلُّ كَاتِبِ سَاكِنُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالإِطْلَاقِ العام»؛ لِأَنَّ سَلْبَ المَحْمُولِ عَنِ المَوْضُوعِ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ دَائِماً كَانَ الإِيجَابُ مُتَحَقِّقاً فِي الجُمْلَةِ، وَهَذَا هُوَ مَعْنَى المُطْلَقَةِ العَامَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ.
(56) The first: the subject in the categorical and the latter the predicate equally. al-Bajuri: (with the general absolute) i.e., in actuality; because the affirmation of the predicate for the subject when it is not permanent, the negation is in general, and this is the meaning of the general absolute negative. And if it is negative: from a general conditional negative and it is the preface; I mean your saying for example: 'No writer is still-fingered necessarily as long as he is writing,' then a general affirmative absolute and it is the conclusion; I mean your saying: 'Not always'; because it is in the power of saying: 'Every writer is still-fingered with the general absolute'; because the negation of the predicate from the subject when it is not permanent, the affirmation is realized in general, and this is the meaning of the general affirmative absolute.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the general absolute proposition (al-mutlaqa al-amma) in both its negative and affirmative forms. The author begins by referencing the first part of a proposition (the subject and predicate). He then explains that the general absolute means 'in actuality' (bi al-fi'l), not necessarily always. For the negative form: if the affirmation of the predicate for the subject is not permanent, then the negation holds in general. For example, 'Not every writer is necessarily still-fingered as long as he is writing' is composed of a general conditional negative (the preface) and a general absolute affirmative (the conclusion 'not always'), which is equivalent to 'Every writer is still-fingered in a general absolute sense' because if the negation is not permanent, affirmation occurs at some time. The same reasoning applies for the affirmative form.

Key Terms
المُطْلَقَة العَامَّة (al-mutlaqa al-amma): A proposition that affirms or denies the predicate of the subject in actuality, not permanently, i.e., it is true at some time. [Note: In some later texts, 'mutlaqa' can refer to non-modal propositions; here it is a specific modal type.]
بالإطلاق العام (bi al-itlaq al-amm): Means 'in actuality' (bi al-fi'l), i.e., the relation holds at least once, but not necessarily always.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It affirms the predicate of the subject necessarily.
D) It denies the predicate of the subject at all times.

Key Takeaway
The general absolute proposition asserts a predicate that holds in actuality at least once, not permanently.

The Absolute Temporal Proposition: Definition and Examples
Author defines the absolute temporal proposition with examples and notes its simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct absolute temporal propositions (waqtiyya mutlaqa) and distinguish them from other temporal propositions in Islamic modal logic.

Core Idea
The absolute temporal proposition asserts a necessary relation at a specific time, not at all times.

In this passage, the author defines the absolute temporal proposition, a key type of modal proposition in Islamic logic. You will learn its structure, see examples from human action, and understand why it is called 'absolute temporal'. This tool is essential for analyzing modal propositions in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of the definition
The proposition must assert necessity (ضرورة) of the relation (affirmative or negative) and restrict it to a specific وقت (time). Look for these in the Arabic.
2
Distinguish affirmative and negative examples
The author gives both: 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing' (affirmative) and 'No human is necessarily still-fingered at the time of writing' (negative). Note the structure: subject + predicate + necessity + time.
3
Understand the naming
'Temporal' (وقتية) because it is restricted by time; 'absolute' (مطلقة) because it is not further restricted by the phrase 'not always' (لا دائماً). This contrasts with the 'absolute' in other senses.
4
Recognize its simplicity
The author notes it is 'simple' (بسيطة), meaning it is not compound. It expresses one necessary relation at one time.
5
Compare with other temporal propositions
The author hints that it is simple, implying there are compound temporals. For now, focus on the definition and examples.

(٤) - وَالوَقْتِيَّةُ المُطْلَقَةُ وَهِيَ: الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ فِي وَقْتٍ مُعَيَّنِ؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوْجَبَةٌ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتَ الكِتَابَةِ، وَسَالِبَةٌ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِسَاكِنِ الْأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتَ الكِتَابَةِ». وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ: وَقْتِيَّةٌ»؛ لِلتَّقْيِيدِ فِيهَا بِالوَقْتِ، وَ «مُطْلَقَةٌ»؛ لإطلاقِهَا عَنِ التَّقْيِيدِ بِقَوْلِنَا: لَا دَائِماً»، وَهِيَ بَسِيطةٌ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي.
(4) And the absolute temporal: it is that in which is judged with necessity of the relation in a specific time; its example affirmative: 'Every human is moving-fingers necessarily at the time of writing,' and negative: 'No human is still-fingered necessarily at the time of writing.' And it is called 'temporal' because of the restriction by time, and 'absolute' because of its being free from restriction by our saying 'not always', and it is simple as will come.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the absolute temporal proposition (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa). It is a proposition that asserts the necessity of the relation (affirmative or negative) at a specific time. Two examples are given: the affirmative 'Every human is necessarily moving his fingers at the time of writing' and the negative 'No human is necessarily still-fingered at the time of writing'. The term 'temporal' (waqtiyya) comes from the restriction to a particular time, and 'absolute' (mutlaqa) because it is not further restricted by the phrase 'not always' (la da'iman). The author notes that this type is simple (basita), meaning it is not composed of two simpler propositions.

Decomposing the Non-Absolute Temporal Proposition
Author defines and dissects the non-absolute temporal proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-absolute temporal proposition and decompose it into its constituent absolute temporal and general absolute propositions.

Core Idea
A non-absolute temporal is a compound proposition: temporal claim plus a ‘not always’ qualifier.

The non-absolute temporal proposition may seem complex, but it is built from two simpler propositions. Understanding its composition helps you see how logicians analyze statements that are true at a specific time but not permanently. In this lesson, we will break down the definition and examples given by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
The non-absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya ghayr al-mutlaqa) is defined as an absolute temporal plus the restriction 'not always' (lā dā’iman). Locate this in the Arabic: al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa lākin maʿa ziyādati qaydi lā dā’iman.
2
Examine the affirmative example
The affirmative example: 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing not always.' Note the two parts: the temporal claim (at writing time, all humans move fingers) and the qualifier 'not always' (meaning it is not a permanent necessity).
3
Parse the compound structure (affirmative case)
For the affirmative, the author says it is composed of an absolute temporal affirmative (the preface) and a general absolute negative (the conclusion, expressed as 'not always'). Confirm that 'not always' is equivalent to 'No human is moving fingers with the general absolute' – i.e., the negation of permanence.
The general absolute negative is a proposition that negates the predicate for all times in a general sense.
4
Parse the compound structure (negative case)
For the negative example, 'No human is necessarily still-fingered at the time of writing not always', the composition is an absolute temporal negative (preface) and a general absolute affirmative (conclusion). The 'not always' here is equivalent to 'Every human is still-fingered with the general absolute' – i.e., the negation of permanence yields an affirmative general absolute.
The logic mirrors the affirmative: when the temporal condition is not permanent, the opposite general absolute holds.
5
Generalize the pattern
Any non-absolute temporal proposition is compound: one part is an absolute temporal (affirmative or negative), the other part is a general absolute (opposite polarity) expressing the 'not always' condition. Recognize that the compound nature is essential for analyzing such propositions in logical discourse.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥) - وَالوَقْتِيَّةُ غَيْرُ المُطْلَقَةِ وَهِيَ: «الوَقْتِيَّةُ المُطْلَقَةُ لَكِنْ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ قَيْدِ لَا دَائِماً»؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتَ الكِتَابَةِ لا دَائِماً»، وَسَالِبَةٌ: لَا شَيْءٌ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتَ الكِتَابَةِ لَا دَائِماً». وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ: وَقْتِيَّةٌ»؛ لِمَا مَرَّ، وَ «غَيْرَ مُطْلَقَةٍ»؛ لِأَنَّهَا مُقَيَّدَةٌ بِقَوْلِنَا: لَا دَائِماً»، وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ: - إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ مِنْ: وَقْتِيَّةٍ مُطْلَقَةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً: «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأصابع بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقتَ الكِتَابَةِ، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْرُ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ: «لَا دَائِماً»؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَحَرِّكِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالإِطْلَاقِ العَامِّ»؛ لِمَا مَرَّ مِنْ أَنَّ إِيجَابَ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوعِ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ دَائِماً كَانَ السَّلْبُ مُتَحَقِّقاً فِي الجُمْلَةِ، وَهَذَا هُوَ مَعْنَى المُطْلَقَةِ العَامَّةِ الصَّالِيَة. - وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ مِنْ: وَقْتِيَّةٍ مُطْلَقَةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتَ الكِتَابَةِ، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْزُ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ: «لَا دَائِماً»؛ لأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةٍ أَنْ يُقَالَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ سَاكِنُ الأَصابع بِالإِطْلَاقِ العَامِّ؛ لِمَا مَرَّ مِنْ أَنَّ سَلْبَ المَحْمُولِ عَنِ المَوْضُوعِ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ دَائِماً كَانَ الإِيجَابُ مُتَحَقِّقاً فِي الجُمْلَةِ، وَهَذَا هُوَ مَعْنَى المُطْلَقَةِ العَامَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ.
(5) And the non-absolute temporal: it is the absolute temporal but with the addition of the restriction 'not always'; its example affirmative: 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing not always,' and negative: 'No human is still-fingered necessarily at the time of writing not always.' And it is called 'temporal' for what preceded, and 'non-absolute' because it is restricted by our saying 'not always', and it is compound: if it is affirmative, it is composed of an absolute temporal affirmative which is the preface; I mean your saying for example: 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing,' then a general absolute negative which is the conclusion; I mean your saying: 'Not always'; because it is in the power of saying: 'No human is moving fingers with the general absolute'; because the affirmation of the predicate for the subject when it is not permanent, the negation is realized in general, and this is the meaning of the general absolute negative. And if it is negative, it is composed of an absolute temporal negative which is the preface; I mean your saying: 'No human is still-fingered necessarily at the time of writing,' then a general absolute affirmative which is the conclusion; I mean your saying: 'Not always'; because it is in the power of saying: 'Every human is still-fingered with the general absolute'; because the negation of the predicate from the subject when it is not permanent, the affirmation is realized in general, and this is the meaning of the general affirmative absolute.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the non-absolute temporal proposition (al-waqtiyya ghayr al-mutlaqa). It is an absolute temporal proposition with the additional restriction 'not always' (la da'iman). Examples are given: affirmative 'Every human is necessarily moving fingers at the time of writing not always' and negative 'No human is necessarily still-fingered at the time of writing not always'. The author then explains that this type is compound (murakkaba), composed of two simpler propositions: an absolute temporal (the preface) and a general absolute (the conclusion). For the affirmative, the absolute temporal affirmative is the preface, and the negation of the general absolute (i.e., 'not always') is equivalent to a general absolute negative ('No human is moving fingers in the general absolute sense'), forming the conclusion. The same applies for the negative: the absolute temporal negative is the preface, and the 'not always' is equivalent to a general absolute affirmative ('Every human is still-fingered in the general absolute sense').

مُطْلَقَة عَامَّة

Understanding the Absolute Distributed Proposition
Author defines the absolute distributed proposition with examples and notes its simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the absolute distributed proposition, state its defining conditions, and distinguish it from other modal propositions.

Core Idea
The absolute distributed proposition asserts a necessary relation at an unspecified time, not bound by 'not always'.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified not only by quantity and quality but also by modality. The absolute distributed proposition is one such modal type. It is widely used in theological and legal arguments where a necessity is affirmed only at some indeterminate time, without the restriction of 'not always'. This lesson unpacks its definition and rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term is 'absolute distributed proposition' (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa). Locate this term in the Arabic text and note that it is a type of modal proposition.
2
Extract the defining conditions
The definition states it is a judgment with necessity of the relation at an unspecified time. There are two key conditions: (1) the relation is necessary, and (2) the time is unspecified. Also, note the absence of the restriction 'not always' (lā dā'imā).
The phrase 'free from restriction by our saying "not always"' clarifies that this proposition does not negate perpetuity, it simply doesn't include that condition.
3
Understand the naming rationale
It is called 'distributed' (muntashira) because the time is spread (distributed) over an indefinite period. It is called 'absolute' (mutlaqa) because it is unrestricted by the 'not always' condition. This contrasts with the perpetual proposition which includes 'always' or 'not always'.
4
Note the simplicity claim
The author states that this proposition is simple (basīṭa), meaning it is not composed of two propositions. This distinguishes it from composite modal propositions that combine mutlaqa and wujūdiyya.
The simplicity is important for later syllogistic figures where compound modals may cause issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٦) - وَالمُنْتَشِرَةُ المُطْلَقَةُ وَهِيَ: الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ فِي وَقْتٍ غَيْرِ مُعَيَّنِ»؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ مُتَنَفِّسُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتاً مَّا وَسَالِبَةٌ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتاً مَّا. وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ مُنْتَشِرَةٌ»؛ لِانْتِشَارِ وَقْتِهَا، وَ: «مُطْلَقَةٌ»؛ لِإِطْلَاقِهَا عَنِ التَّقْبِيدِ بِقَوْلِنَا: «لَا دَائِماً»، وَهِيَ بَسِيطَةٌ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا يَأْتِي.
(6) And the absolute distributed: it is that in which is judged with necessity of the relation at an unspecified time; its example affirmative: 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time,' and negative: 'No human is necessarily breathing at some time.' And it is called 'distributed' because of the distribution of its time, and 'absolute' because of its being free from restriction by our saying 'not always', and it is simple as will be known from what comes.

Commentary
This passage defines the absolute distributed proposition (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa). It asserts the necessity of the relation at an unspecified time. Examples: affirmative 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time' and negative 'No human is necessarily breathing at some time'. The term 'distributed' (muntashira) refers to the distribution of time over an unspecified period, and 'absolute' (mutlaqa) because it is not restricted by 'not always'. The author notes that this type is simple (basita), similar to the absolute temporal, meaning it is not composed of two propositions.

Definition of the Non-Absolute Distributed Proposition
Author defines the non-absolute distributed proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-absolute distributed proposition and explain how it is compounded from an absolute distributed proposition and a negation of perpetuity.

Core Idea
A non-absolute distributed proposition combines 'at some time' with 'not always'.

In this lesson, we explore the non-absolute distributed proposition (al-muntashira ghayr al-mutlaqa). This type of proposition is essential for precise statements in Islamic logic, particularly when making universal claims that are true only at some unspecified time. We will see how it differs from the absolute distributed and how it is composed of two simpler propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the definition
The author defines it as the absolute distributed proposition but with the addition of the restriction 'not always'. Note the two parts: the distributed part (at some time) and the restriction (not always).
Distributed means the predicate applies to every individual of the subject at some (possibly different) time.
2
Examine the affirmative example
The affirmative example is: 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time not always.' This means for each human, there is a time (unspecified) when they are breathing, but it is not true that they are breathing at all times.
The 'not always' negates perpetual truth.
3
Examine the negative example
The negative example is: 'No human is necessarily breathing at some time not always.' This denies that there is any human for whom it is necessary that they breathe at some time and not always.
This is the contradictory of the affirmative.
4
Understand the compound nature
The author states it is compound (murakkaba). It is composed of an absolute distributed proposition (e.g., 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time') and a general absolute proposition that negates perpetuity.
This composition is analogous to the non-absolute temporal proposition seen earlier.

Like the non-absolute temporal, this type is compound, meaning it is composed of two propositions: an absolute distributed and a general absolute. The author notes that it is called 'distributed' for the same reason (unspecified time) and 'non-absolute' because it is restricted by 'not always'. The full composition is likely analogous to the temporal case, though the detailed analysis is given in the marginal note.

Propositions
intermediate
7 min
Absolute Spread Proposition (Muntashira Mutlaqa)
Definition of absolute spread proposition with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute spread proposition and explain why it is called 'simple'.

Core Idea
Absolute spread propositions judge necessity at an unspecified time without extra restrictions.

In our study of modal propositions in Islamic logic, we encounter the 'spread' (muntashira) category. The absolute spread proposition is the simplest form: it asserts that a relation holds necessarily at some unspecified time, without any further temporal qualification. Understanding this foundational type is essential for grasping more complex modal propositions used in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic
The absolute spread proposition judges the necessity of the relation (either affirmative or negative) at an unspecified time. The time is not fixed; it can be any time.
Compare with propositions that specify a time (e.g., 'at the time of the creation').
2
Recognize the examples
The text gives two examples: affirmative 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time' and negative 'No human is necessarily breathing at some time'. Note that both are about the same subject and predicate but differ in quality.
These examples show that muntashira mutlaqa can be both affirmative and negative.
3
Understand the terminology
It is called 'spread' (muntashira) because the time is spread out (not fixed). It is called 'absolute' (mutlaqa) because it is free from any additional restriction such as 'not always'.
The term 'absolute' here means no extra temporal qualifier.
4
Note the simplicity
The author states that this type is 'simple' (basita). This means it contains only one modal condition: necessity at some time, without any further condition.
Simplicity here refers to the absence of an added restriction.

(٦) - المُنتَشِرَةُ المُطْلَقَةُ وَهِيَ : الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ فِي وَقْتٍ غَيْرِ مُعَيَّنِ»؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ مُتَنَفِّسُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتاً مَّا وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتاً وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ مُنْتَشِرَةٌ»؛ لِانْتِشَارِ وَقْتِهَا ، وَ : «مُطْلَقَةٌ»؛ لِإِطْلَاقِهَا عَنِ التَّقْبِيدِ بِقَوْلِنَا : «لَا دَائِماً»، وَهِيَ بَسِيطَةٌ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا يَأْتِي. (۷) - وَالمُنتَشِرَةُ غَيْرُ المُطْلَقَةِ وَهِيَ : المُنْتَشِرَةُ المُطْلَقَةُ لَكِنْ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ قَيْدِ : لَا دَائِماً »؛ مِثَالُهَا " مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ مُتَنَفِّسٌ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتاً مَّا لَا دَائِماً»، وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتاً مَّا لَا دَائِماً». وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : مُنْتَشِرَةٌ»؛ لِمَا مَرَّ ، وَ : «غَيْرَ مُطْلَقَةٍ» ؛ لِأَنَّهَا مُقَيَّدَةٌ بِقَوْلِنَا : «لَا دَائِماً»، وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ : الأنبابي قوله : (كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَنَفِّسُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَقْتاً مَّا ) قد حكم في هذا المثال بضرورة ثبوت التنفس للإنسان في وقت غير معين. وقوله : (لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ... إلخ) قد حكم في هذا المثال بضرورة سلب التنفس عنِ الإنسان في وقت غير معين، ولا تنافي بينهما كما هو ظاهر؛ إذ كلّ في وقتٍ غير وقت الآخر؛ إلَّا أنَّ وقتهما غير معيَّن .
(6) The absolute spread: it is that in which necessity of the relation is judged at an unspecified time. Its affirmative example: Every human is necessarily breathing at some time. Its negative example: No human is necessarily breathing at some time. It is called 'spread' because its time is spread, and 'absolute' because it is free from restriction by our saying 'not always', and it is simple as will be known from what follows. (7) The non-absolute spread: it is the absolute spread but with the addition of the restriction 'not always'. Its affirmative example: Every human is necessarily breathing at some time not always. Its negative example: No human is necessarily breathing at some time not always. It is called 'spread' for the reason mentioned, and 'non-absolute' because it is restricted by our saying 'not always', and it is composite. al-Anbabi says: His statement 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time' — in this example judgment was made of the necessity of affirming breathing for the human at an unspecified time. And his statement 'No human... etc.' — in this example judgment was made of the necessity of negating breathing from the human at an unspecified time. There is no contradiction between them, as is clear, since each is at a time other than the other's time, except that their times are unspecified.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines two types of modal propositions known as 'spread' (muntashira). The author first defines the absolute spread (muntashira mutlaqa): it judges that the relation (affirmative or negative) holds necessarily at some unspecified time. Examples are given: 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time' (affirmative) and 'No human is necessarily breathing at some time' (negative). The name 'spread' refers to the time being spread out (not fixed), and 'absolute' means it is not further restricted by the phrase 'not always'. This type is called simple (basita) because it does not contain an additional restriction. Next, the non-absolute spread (muntashira ghayr mutlaqa) is defined as the same but with the added restriction 'not always'. Examples include the same propositions but with 'la da'iman' appended. This type is called composite (murakkaba) because it contains two conditions: necessity at some time and not always. The commentary by al-Anbabi clarifies that the affirmative and negative examples are not contradictory because they apply to different unspecified times. This distinction is crucial for understanding modal logic in the Islamic tradition, where temporal conditions affect the validity of arguments.

Key Terms
مُنتَشِرَة (muntashira): A proposition that judges necessity of the relation at an unspecified time; time is spread out. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
مُطْلَقَة (mutlaqa): Free from additional temporal restrictions like 'not always'. [Note: Often contrasted with muqayyada; context determines the specific restriction.]
بَسِيطَة (basīṭa): Simple proposition with only one modal condition (necessity at some time). [Note: In logic, 'simple' can also refer to non-composite terms; here it means no additional temporal qualifier.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an absolute spread (muntashira mutlaqa) affirmative proposition?
A) Every human is necessarily breathing at some time not always.
B) Every human is necessarily breathing at some time.
C) No human is necessarily breathing at some time.
D) Every human is an animal always.

Key Takeaway
The absolute spread proposition is a simple modal proposition that judges necessity at an unspecified time without additional restrictions.

Non-Absolute Spread Proposition (Muntashira Ghayr Mutlaqa)
Definition of non-absolute spread proposition with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-absolute spread proposition and explain why it is called 'composite'.

Core Idea
Non-absolute spread propositions add 'not always', making them composite and temporally restricted.

Building on the absolute spread, the non-absolute spread introduces an additional restriction: 'not always'. This subtle addition transforms a simple proposition into a composite one, with two temporal conditions. This distinction is critical for precise logical analysis in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the additional restriction
The non-absolute spread is exactly like the absolute spread but with the added phrase 'not always' (لا دائما). This restricts the necessity to a specific time frame that is not perpetual.
The restriction makes the proposition composite (murakkaba) because it contains two conditions: necessity at some time and not at all times.
2
Compare the examples
Compare the affirmative absolute example 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time' with the non-absolute 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time not always'. The latter explicitly denies that this necessity holds for all times.
The negative example also includes 'not always', negating perpetual negation.
3
Understand the terminology
It is still called 'spread' because the time is unspecified, but 'non-absolute' (ghayr mutlaqa) because it is restricted by the phrase 'not always'.
The term 'non-absolute' here means it is qualified with a temporal restriction.
4
Note the compositeness
The author states that this type is 'composite' (murakkaba). It has two elements: the absolute spread condition plus the restriction 'not always'. This compositeness is what distinguishes it from the simple absolute spread.
Understanding compositeness helps in analyzing complex modal statements in arguments.

It is called 'spread' because its time is spread, and 'absolute' because it is free from restriction by our saying 'not always', and it is simple as will be known from what follows. The Three Perpetual Propositions
Definition and classification of three perpetual propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish among the three types of perpetual propositions (absolute, general conventional, specific conventional) based on their definitions, examples, and underlying rationale.

Core Idea
Perpetual propositions assert 'always'—the key is whether the permanence is unconditional or conditional on a description, and whether the possibility of permanence is negated.

In this passage from al-Bajuri's commentary, the author defines three types of perpetual propositions—modal propositions that judge a relation to hold always. Understanding these distinctions is essential for analyzing syllogisms in logic and for evaluating claims in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where permanence of attributes or rulings matters. Let's break down each type's definition, example, and the rationale behind the threefold classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criteria for absolute perpetual
Look at the definition: it judges permanence without any restriction by a description or the like. Note its positive and negative examples: 'Every human is an animal always' and 'No human is a stone always'.
This is the simplest type; it is called 'absolute' because it is unrestricted.
2
Understand the general conventional
It judges permanence on condition of the subject's description persisting. The restriction is understood by convention even if not explicitly stated. Note the examples: 'Every writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is writing' and its negative.
It is called 'general' because it does not include the negation of permanence ('not always').
3
Grasp the specific conventional
It is the general conventional plus the restriction 'not always', indicating that the relation does not hold perpetually outside the described condition. The affirmative example is missing in the text, but the concept is clear.
The 'not always' part distinguishes it from the general conventional.
4
Analyze the rationale for three types
The commentary by al-Anbabi explains: the cause of perpetuity is either the essence (absolute) or a description; if a description, either with or without negating the possibility of permanence. This yields exactly three types.
Al-Sharshimi also clarifies that the term 'urfiya applies only when the description is explicitly mentioned, though the condition is understood conventionally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالدَّوَائِمُ الثَّلَاثُ، وَهِيَ : (۸) - الدَّائِمَةُ المُطْلَقَةُ وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِدَوَامِ النِّسْبَةِ مَعَ الإِطْلَاقِ عَنِ التَّفْسِيدِ بِوَصْفٍ أَوْ نَحْوِهِ» ; مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ دَائِماً»، وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ دَائِماً». وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ: «دَائِمَةٌ» ; لِأَنَّ كَيْفِيَّةَ نِسْبَتِهَا الدَّوَامُ، وَ : «مُطْلَقَةٌ»؛ لِإِطْلَاقِهَا عَنِ التَّفْيِيدِ بِوَصْفٍ أَوْ نَحْوِهِ، وَهِيَ بَسِيطَةٌ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا يَأْتِي. (۹) - وَالعُرْفِيَّةُ العَامَّةُ وَهِيَ : «الَّتي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِدَوَامِ النِّسْبَةِ بِشَرْطِ دَوَامِ وَصْفِ المَوْضُوعِ»; مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، وَسَالِبَةٌ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً ما دَامَ كَاتِباً . وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : «عُرْفِيَّةٌ» ; لِانْفِهَامِ التَّقْيِيدِ فِيهَا بِدَوَامِ الوَصْفِ مِنَ العُرْفِ، وَلَوْ لَمْ يُصَرَّحْ بِهِ و: «عَامَّةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهَا أَعَمُّ مِنَ العُرْفِيَّةِ الخَاصَّةِ، فَإِنَّهَا لَمْ تُقَيَّدْ بِمَا يَنْفِي احْتِمَالَ الدَّوَامِ وَهُوَ قَوْلُنَا : لَا دَائِماً ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ نَظِيرُهُ ، وَهِيَ بَسِيطَةٌ كَالَّتِي قَبْلَهَا . (١٠) - وَالعُرْفِيَّةُ الخَاصَّةُ وَهِيَ : «العُرْفِيَّةُ العَامَّةُ لَكِنْ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ قَيْدِ : لَا دَائِماً » ; مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : [؟] الأنبابي قوله : (وَالدَّوَائِمُ الثَّلَاثُ وجه كونها ثلاثة : أنَّ علَّة الدَّوامِ إِمَّا الذَّات أو الوصف، وعلى الثاني إمَّا مقيَّدٌ بما ينفي احتمال دوام الوصف، أو لا . الشرشيمي: قوله : وَلَوْ لَمْ يُصَرَّ بِهِ ألا ترى أنه لو قيل : كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأصابع دَائِماً»، فهو في العرف أنه ما دام كاتباً، لكنَّها لا تسمَّى : «عرفيَّةٌ» ; إلا عند التصريح بالوصف.
And the three perpetuals are: (8) The absolute perpetual: it is that in which judgment is made of the permanence of the relation with absoluteness from restriction by a description or the like. Its affirmative example: Every human is an animal always. Its negative example: No human is a stone always. It is called 'perpetual' because the quality of its relation is permanence, and 'absolute' because of its absolution from restriction by a description or the like, and it is simple as will be known from what follows. (9) The general conventional: it is that in which judgment is made of the permanence of the relation on condition of the permanence of the description of the subject. Its affirmative example: Every writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is writing. Its negative example: No writer is still-fingered always as long as he is writing. It is called 'conventional' because the restriction by the permanence of the description is understood from convention even if not explicitly stated, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific conventional, for it is not restricted by what negates the possibility of permanence, which is our saying 'not always', as its counterpart preceded, and it is simple like the one before it. (10) The specific conventional: it is the general conventional but with the addition of the restriction 'not always'. Its affirmative example: [missing]. al-Anbabi says: His statement 'the three perpetuals' — the reason they are three is that the cause of perpetuity is either the essence or the description; on the second, it is either restricted by what negates the possibility of permanence of the description or not. al-Sharshimi says: His statement 'even if not explicitly stated' — do you not see that if it were said 'Every writer is moving his fingers always', it is understood conventionally that it is as long as he is writing, but it is not called 'conventional' except when the description is explicitly stated.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage classifies three types of perpetual (da'ima) propositions, which assert the relation holds always. The first is the absolute perpetual (da'ima mutlaqa), which judges the relation always without any condition related to a description. Examples: 'Every human is an animal always' and 'No human is a stone always.' It is called 'absolute' because it is not restricted by a description, and it is simple. The second is the general conventional ('urfiya 'amma), which judges the relation always on the condition that the subject's description (e.g., 'writer') persists. Examples: 'Every writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is writing' and its negative. The restriction is understood by convention ('urf) even if not explicitly stated. It is called 'general' because it is broader than the specific conventional, as it does not include the negation of permanence ('not always'). It is also simple. The third is the specific conventional ('urfiya khassa), which is the same as the general conventional but with the added restriction 'not always', indicating that the relation does not hold perpetually outside the described condition. The commentary by al-Anbabi explains why there are three: the cause of perpetuity is either the essence (absolute) or a description; if description, it is either with or without negating the possibility of permanence. Al-Sharshimi clarifies that a proposition like 'Every writer is moving his fingers always' is understood conventionally as conditional on writing, but it is not technically called 'conventional' unless the description is explicitly mentioned in the proposition.

Key Terms
دَائِمَة (dā'ima): A proposition that judges the relation holds always (perpetually). [Note: In earlier texts, 'da'ima' may be used interchangeably with 'mutlaqa'? Here it is a specific type with permanence.]
عُرْفِيَّة ('urfiya): A proposition whose restriction to a description is understood by convention, even if not explicitly stated. [Note: The term 'urfiya' is used in later commentaries to distinguish from absolute propositions; stable in the tradition.]
بَسِيطَة (basīṭa): Here it means the proposition does not contain an additional restriction like 'not always'. [Note: Same as in previous lesson; context-specific.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a 'general conventional' ('urfiya 'amma) affirmative proposition?
A) Every human is an animal always.
B) Every writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is writing.
C) Every writer is moving his fingers always but not always.
D) No writer is still-fingered always.

Key Takeaway
The three perpetual propositions differ in whether the permanence is unconditional (absolute), conditional on a description (general conventional), or conditional with a negation of permanence outside the description (specific conventional).

The Special Customary Proposition: Definition and Composition
Bajuri defines the special customary proposition and analyzes its composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a special customary proposition and differentiate it from general customary and general absolute propositions.

Core Idea
The special customary proposition combines a condition with a negation of perpetuity.

In this lesson, we will examine Bajuri's definition and analysis of the special customary proposition. You will learn its structure and how it is compounded from two simpler propositions. This will sharpen your ability to parse compound modal propositions in logic, especially those used in kalam and usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Note whether the example given is affirmative or negative. 'Every writer is moving fingers...' is affirmative; 'No writer is still fingers...' is negative.
2
Separate antecedent and consequent
The special customary is composed of two parts: the antecedent (a general customary) and the consequent (a general absolute). In the affirmative example, the antecedent is 'Every writer is moving fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer' and the consequent is 'not perpetually'.
The consequent 'lā dā’imā' is shorthand for a general absolute negation.
3
Interpret the consequent
The phrase 'lā dā’imā' (not perpetually) does not negate the entire judgment; it negates the unqualified perpetuity. It is equivalent to 'No writer is moving fingers with the general absolute' – meaning it is not true for all time unconditionally.
This is the key difference from the general customary, which lacks this negation.
4
Compare with general customary
The general customary only has the conditional part ('dā’imā mā dām'); the special customary adds the negation of perpetuity. This makes it more specific, as Bajuri states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً لَا دَائِماً، وَسَالِبَةٌ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً لَا دَائِماً. وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ: عُرْفِيَّةٌ لِمَا مَرَّ، وَخَاصَّةٌ لِأَنَّهَا أَخَصُّ مِنَ العُرْفِيَّةِ العَامَّةِ كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ، وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ: إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ مِنْ عُرْفِيَّةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ، أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً: كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْزُ، أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ: لَا دَائِماً؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِمُتَحَرِّكِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالإِطْلَاقِ العَامِّ، لِمَا مَرَّ. وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ مِنْ عُرْفِيَّةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ، أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْزُ، أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ: لَا دَائِماً؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ سَاكِنُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالإِطْلَاقِ العَامِّ، لِمَا مَرَّ.
Every writer is moving fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer, not perpetually; and negative: No writer is still fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer, not perpetually. And it was named 'customary' for what preceded, and 'special' because it is more specific than the general customary, as known from what preceded. And it is compound: if affirmative, from an affirmative general customary which is the antecedent, meaning your saying for example: 'Every writer is moving fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer', then a negative general absolute which is the consequent, meaning your saying: 'not perpetually'; because it is in the strength of saying: 'No writer is moving fingers with the general absolute', for what preceded. And if negative, from a negative general customary which is the antecedent, meaning your saying for example: 'No writer is still fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer', then an affirmative general absolute which is the consequent, meaning your saying: 'not perpetually'; because it is in the strength of saying: 'Every writer is still fingers with the general absolute', for what preceded.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines the special customary proposition (العرفية الخاصة) and explains its composition. The author first gives examples: an affirmative example, 'Every writer is moving fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer, not perpetually', and a negative example, 'No writer is still fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer, not perpetually'. The proposition is called 'customary' because it involves a condition (دائماً ما دام), and 'special' because it is more specific than the general customary proposition, which does not include the negation of perpetuity. The composition is then analyzed: if the proposition is affirmative, it consists of an affirmative general customary as the antecedent (e.g., 'Every writer is moving fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer') and a negative general absolute as the consequent (e.g., 'not perpetually'), which is equivalent to saying 'No writer is moving fingers with the general absolute'. If the proposition is negative, it consists of a negative general customary as the antecedent and an affirmative general absolute as the consequent, equivalent to 'Every writer is still fingers with the general absolute'. This shows that the special customary proposition is a compound of a general customary and a general absolute, with the latter indicating that the judgment is not perpetual in an unqualified sense.

Key Terms
العرفية الخاصة
العرفية العامة
المطلقة العامة

Key Takeaway
The special customary proposition is composed of a general customary (antecedent) and a general absolute (consequent) that negates unqualified perpetuity.

Definition and Characteristics of the General Possible Proposition
Bajuri defines the general possible proposition and explains its naming and scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general possible proposition by checking whether necessity is negated from the opposite side, and distinguish it from the special possible.

Core Idea
The general possible proposition includes both necessary and contingent truths.

In this lesson, we analyze the definition of the general possible proposition (الممكنة العامة) as presented by al-Bājūrī. This proposition is fundamental in modal logic because it covers a wider range than the special possible. By understanding its defining characteristic—negating necessity from the opposite side—you will be able to correctly classify modal propositions in Islamic theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic text: الممكنة العامة.
2
Extract the definition
The definition is: 'the proposition in which it is judged by the negation of necessity from the opposite side.' Break this down: what is the opposite side? What does it mean to negate necessity from it?
3
Examine the examples
The text gives an affirmative example (كل إنسان حيوان بالإمكان العام) and a negative example (لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالإمكان العام). Check how these satisfy the definition.
4
Understand the naming
Why is it called 'possible' (ممكنة)? Because its modality is possibility. Why 'general' (عامة)? Because it is broader than the special possible and includes necessary propositions.
5
Grasp the scope
The general possible includes both necessary and contingent propositions, whereas the special possible excludes necessary ones. Verify this with examples from Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمُمْكِنَتَانِ، وَهُمَا: (١١) المُمْكِنَةُ العَامَّةُ، وَهِيَ: الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِسَلْبِ الضَّرُورَةِ عَنِ الطَّرَفِ المُخَالِفِ؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ بِالإِمْكَانِ العَامِّ، وَسَالِبَةٌ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ بِالإِمْكَانِ العَامِّ. وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ مُمْكِنَةً لِأَنَّ كَيْفِيَّةَ نِسْبَتِهَا الإِمْكَانُ، وَعَامَّةً لِأَنَّهَا أَعَمُّ مِنَ المُمْكِنَةِ الخَاصَّةِ، فَإِنَّهَا كَمَا تَصْدُقُ بِهَا تَصْدُقُ بِالضَّرُورِيَّةِ، وَهِيَ بَسِيطَةٌ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي.
And the two possible propositions, and they are: (11) The general possible, and it is: that in which it is judged by the negation of necessity from the opposite side; its example in affirmative: 'Every human is an animal with general possibility', and negative: 'No human is a stone with general possibility'. And it was named 'possible' because the modality of its relation is possibility, and 'general' because it is more general than the special possible, for it is verified by it just as it is verified by the necessary, and it is simple as will come.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines the general possible proposition (الممكنة العامة) as one where necessity is negated from the opposite side. For example, in 'Every human is an animal with general possibility', the necessity of 'human not being animal' is negated; similarly in the negative example. It is called 'possible' because its modality is possibility (imkān), and 'general' because it is broader than the special possible proposition: it holds true even for necessary truths (like 'God exists') as well as contingent ones. The text notes that it is simple (basiṭa), not compound, as will be explained later. This proposition is foundational for understanding modal logic: it includes both the possible and the necessary, whereas the special possible excludes necessity.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
سلب الضرورة
الطرف المخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

The general possible proposition is defined by negating necessity from the opposite side, making it inclusive of both necessary and contingent judgments.
