Defining the Special Possible Proposition
Bajuri defines the special possible and its composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the special possible proposition and identify its composition from two general possibilities.

Core Idea
The special possible negates necessity on both sides, making it compound, not simple.

In modal logic, propositions can be necessary or possible. The special possible is a refined category that excludes any necessity, indicating genuine contingency. In this passage, Bajuri defines it and reveals its compound nature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term
Locate the Arabic term 'الممكنة الخاصة' (special possible) in the source. This is the subject being defined.
2
Extract the definition
The definition is: 'that in which it is judged by the negation of necessity from the two sides, i.e., the agreeing and the opposing'. So the special possible negates necessity on both sides.
The agreeing side is the judgment as stated; the opposing side is its negation.
3
Understand why 'special'
It is called 'special' because it is more specific than the general possible: the general possible includes necessary propositions, but the special possible excludes them.
The general possible only negates necessity on one side, while the special possible negates on both.
4
Recognize composition
The special possible is compound, composed of two general possible propositions: one affirmative and one negative. This reflects that both the judgment and its opposite are possible in the general sense.
For example, 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' implies both 'Every human is a writer is possible' (general possible affirmative) and 'No human is a writer is possible' (general possible negative).
5
Apply to an example
Take the example given: 'Every human is a writer with special possibility'. This means it is not necessary that every human is a writer, nor is it necessary that no human is a writer. The proposition is genuinely contingent.
The negative example: 'No human is a writer with special possibility' similarly negates necessity on both sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٢) وَالمُمْكِنَةُ الخَاصَّةُ، وَهِيَ: الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِسَلْبِ الضَّرُورَةِ عَنِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ أَعْنِي المُوَافِقَ وَالمُخَالِفَ؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ كَاتِبٌ بِالإِمْكَانِ الخَاصِّ، وَسَالِبَةٌ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِكَاتِبٍ بِالإِمْكَانِ الخَاصِّ. وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ مُمْكِنَةً لِمَا مَرَّ، وَخَاصَّةً لِأَنَّهَا أَخَصُّ مِنَ المُمْكِنَةِ العَامَّةِ كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا تَقَدَّمَ، وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ سَوَاءٌ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةً أَوْ سَالِبَةً مِنْ مُمْكِنَتَيْنِ عَامَّتَيْنِ إِحْدَاهُمَا مُوجَبَةٌ وَالأُخْرَى سَالِبَةٌ.
(12) And the special possible, and it is: that in which it is judged by the negation of necessity from the two sides, i.e., the agreeing and the opposing; its example in affirmative: 'Every human is a writer with special possibility', and negative: 'No human is a writer with special possibility'. And it was named 'possible' for what preceded, and 'special' because it is more specific than the general possible as known from what preceded, and it is compound, whether affirmative or negative, from two general possibilities, one affirmative and the other negative.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines the special possible proposition (الممكنة الخاصة) as one where necessity is negated from both the agreeing side (the judgment itself) and the opposing side. For example, 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' means that it is not necessary that every human is a writer, nor is it necessary that no human is a writer; the proposition is truly contingent. It is called 'special' because it is more restricted than the general possible: the general possible includes necessary propositions, but the special possible excludes them. Importantly, the special possible is compound, composed of two general possible propositions: one affirmative and one negative. This composition reflects that both the judgment and its opposite are possible in the general sense.

Key Terms
الممكنة الخاصة
الطرف الموافق
مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The General Possible Proposition
Definition and characteristics of the general possible proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general possible proposition and distinguish it from a specific possible proposition.

Core Idea
The general possible proposition includes both the possible and the necessary.

In Islamic logic, modal propositions are crucial for expressing degrees of certainty. The general possible proposition is the simplest modal form, covering anything that is not judged as necessary or perpetual. Understanding it helps you grasp how possibility is a broader category that includes necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's modality
Look at the proposition: does it include a qualifier like 'necessarily', 'perpetually', or 'possibly'? If it says 'possible' in the general sense, it is a general possible.
The absence of necessity or perpetuity is key.
2
Check if necessity or perpetuity is negated
A general possible does not explicitly negate necessity; it simply does not assert it. It is neutral regarding necessity.
3
Recognize its simplicity
The author states it is 'simple' (basīṭa), meaning it does not decompose into two propositions. It is a single judgment.
This contrasts with the specific possible.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the statement 'Every human is capable of writing (with general possibility)'. This includes both those who actually write (necessary writers) and those who could write but don't (possible non-writers).
This example is from the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والممكنة العامة وهي: التي لم يُحكم فيها بضرورة ولا دوام. وإنما سميت: ممكنة؛ لأن كيفية نسبتها الإمكان، وعامة؛ لأنها أعم من الممكنة الخاصة، فإنها كما تصدق بها تصدق بالضرورية، وهي بسيطة كما سيأتي.
والممكنة الخاصة وهي: التي حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة عن الطرفين - أعني: الموافق والمخالف؛ مثالها موجبة: كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص، وسالبة: لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان الخاص.
وإنما سميت: ممكنة لما مر، وخاصة؛ لأنها أخص من الممكنة العامة كما علم مما تقدم، وهي مركبة سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة من ممكنتين عامتين؛ إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة؛ لأن قولك مثلا: كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص في قوة أن يقال: كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان العام، وأن يقال: لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان العام، وكذا يقال في مثال السالبة، وبذلك تعلم أنه ليس المراد أنها مركبة لفظا، بل المراد أنها في قوة قضيتين.
And the general possible proposition: it is that in which no necessity or perpetuity is judged. It is called 'possible' because the modality of its relation is possibility, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific possible proposition; for just as it is true of it, it is true of the necessary, and it is simple as will come. And the specific possible proposition: it is that in which the negation of necessity is judged concerning the two sides – I mean: the affirmative and the negative; its example affirmative: every human is a writer with specific possibility, and negative: no human is a writer with specific possibility. And it is called 'possible' for what passed, and 'specific' because it is more specific than the general possible proposition as known from what preceded, and it is composite whether affirmative or negative from two general possible propositions; one affirmative and the other negative; because your saying for example: 'every human is a writer with specific possibility' is equivalent to saying: 'every human is a writer with general possibility', and to saying: 'no human is a writer with general possibility', and likewise is said in the example of the negative. And by that you know that what is meant is not that it is composite in wording, but rather that it is equivalent to two propositions.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines two types of modal propositions: the general possible and the specific possible. The author begins with the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-ʿāmma), which is a proposition in which the judgment is not qualified by necessity or perpetuity. It is called 'possible' because its modality (kayfiyya) is possibility, and 'general' because it encompasses both the specific possible and the necessary (it applies to what is possible and also to what is necessary, since the necessary is also possible in the general sense). The author notes that this proposition is simple (basīṭa), meaning it does not imply another proposition, as will be explained later. Next, the specific possible proposition (al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa) is defined: it is a proposition in which necessity is explicitly negated from both the affirmative and negative sides. The author gives examples: 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility' (affirmative) and 'No human is a writer with specific possibility' (negative). The specific possible is called 'specific' because it is more specific than the general possible, as it excludes the necessary. The key point is that the specific possible is composite: it is equivalent to two general possible propositions, one affirmative and one negative. For instance, 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility' is equivalent to both 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'No human is a writer with general possibility'. The author stresses that this composition is not in wording but in meaning: the proposition has the force (quwwa) of two separate propositions.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
الإمكان العام
بسيطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It negates necessity from both sides.
B) It is free from judgment of necessity and perpetuity.
C) It is equivalent to two possible propositions.
D) It applies only to necessary existents.

Key Takeaway
The general possible proposition is the simplest modal form, not ruling out necessity or perpetuity, and is true of both possible and necessary things.

The Specific Possible Proposition and Its Composition
Definition and composition of the specific possible proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the specific possible proposition and explain why it is equivalent to two general possible propositions.

Core Idea
The specific possible proposition is not a single statement but has the force of two distinct claims.

While the general possible is simple, the specific possible is more restrictive: it explicitly denies necessity on both sides. This makes it logically equivalent to two general possible propositions. This concept is essential for precise modal reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
A specific possible proposition negates necessity from both the affirmative and negative sides. For example, 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility' means it is possible that every human is a writer, and it is also possible that not every human is a writer (i.e., it is not necessary that every human is a writer, nor is it necessary that no human is a writer).
The negation is of necessity, not of the proposition itself.
2
Analyze the examples
The author gives two examples: affirmative ('every human is a writer with specific possibility') and negative ('no human is a writer with specific possibility'). Notice that both negate necessity on both sides.
3
Decompose into two general possibles
Take the affirmative specific possible: 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility.' It is equivalent to both (1) 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' and (2) 'No human is a writer with general possibility.' The specific possible asserts both of these at once.
This decomposition is in meaning, not wording.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider 'It is possible (specific) that the world is originated.' This implies both that the world is originated (general possible) and that the world is not originated (general possible) – but of course, only one of these is actually true; the specific possible merely denies necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والممكنة العامة وهي: التي لم يُحكم فيها بضرورة ولا دوام. وإنما سميت: ممكنة؛ لأن كيفية نسبتها الإمكان، وعامة؛ لأنها أعم من الممكنة الخاصة، فإنها كما تصدق بها تصدق بالضرورية، وهي بسيطة كما سيأتي.
والممكنة الخاصة وهي: التي حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة عن الطرفين - أعني: الموافق والمخالف؛ مثالها موجبة: كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص، وسالبة: لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان الخاص.
وإنما سميت: ممكنة لما مر، وخاصة؛ لأنها أخص من الممكنة العامة كما علم مما تقدم، وهي مركبة سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة من ممكنتين عامتين؛ إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة؛ لأن قولك مثلا: كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص في قوة أن يقال: كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان العام، وأن يقال: لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان العام، وكذا يقال في مثال السالبة، وبذلك تعلم أنه ليس المراد أنها مركبة لفظا، بل المراد أنها في قوة قضيتين.
And the general possible proposition: it is that in which no necessity or perpetuity is judged. It is called 'possible' because the modality of its relation is possibility, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific possible proposition; for just as it is true of it, it is true of the necessary, and it is simple as will come. And the specific possible proposition: it is that in which the negation of necessity is judged concerning the two sides – I mean: the affirmative and the negative; its example affirmative: every human is a writer with specific possibility, and negative: no human is a writer with specific possibility. And it is called 'possible' for what passed, and 'specific' because it is more specific than the general possible proposition as known from what preceded, and it is composite whether affirmative or negative from two general possible propositions; one affirmative and the other negative; because your saying for example: 'every human is a writer with specific possibility' is equivalent to saying: 'every human is a writer with general possibility', and to saying: 'no human is a writer with general possibility', and likewise is said in the example of the negative. And by that you know that what is meant is not that it is composite in wording, but rather that it is equivalent to two propositions.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines two types of modal propositions: the general possible and the specific possible. The author begins with the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-ʿāmma), which is a proposition in which the judgment is not qualified by necessity or perpetuity. It is called 'possible' because its modality (kayfiyya) is possibility, and 'general' because it encompasses both the specific possible and the necessary (it applies to what is possible and also to what is necessary, since the necessary is also possible in the general sense). The author notes that this proposition is simple (basīṭa), meaning it does not imply another proposition, as will be explained later. Next, the specific possible proposition (al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa) is defined: it is a proposition in which necessity is explicitly negated from both the affirmative and negative sides. The author gives examples: 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility' (affirmative) and 'No human is a writer with specific possibility' (negative). The specific possible is called 'specific' because it is more specific than the general possible, as it excludes the necessary. The key point is that the specific possible is composite: it is equivalent to two general possible propositions, one affirmative and one negative. For instance, 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility' is equivalent to both 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'No human is a writer with general possibility'. The author stresses that this composition is not in wording but in meaning: the proposition has the force (quwwa) of two separate propositions.

Key Terms

الإمكان الخاص
مركبة

Key Takeaway
The specific possible proposition logically implies both the affirmative and negative general possible propositions, making it logically composite.

The General Absolute Proposition (al-Muṭlaqa al-ʿĀmma)
Defines the general absolute proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general absolute proposition, explain its simplicity, and distinguish it from the existential propositions.

Core Idea
The general absolute judges actual existence without any temporal or modal restriction.

Modal logic in the Islamic tradition classifies propositions by the manner in which the relation between subject and predicate holds. The general absolute is the simplest modal type: it affirms that the relation actually exists, without any qualification of time, necessity, or perpetuity. This lesson unpacks its definition and significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term 'المطلقة العامة' (general absolute) in the Arabic passage. Notice it is introduced as one of the three absolute propositions.
2
Extract the defining condition
The definition states that it judges the relation 'with absoluteness' (بالإطلاق), meaning the relation actually exists (حاصلة بالفعل). There is no additional condition like negation of perpetuity or necessity.
The condition is simply actual existence.
3
Understand the name
It is called 'absolute' (مطلقة) because the attribute of its relation is absoluteness, and 'general' (عامة) because it is more general than the two existential types that follow, as it is not restricted by 'not perpetually' or 'not necessarily'.
Generality here means it includes more possibilities.
4
Analyze the example
Consider the affirmative example: 'كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق' (Every human is breathing absolutely). This means that, disregarding time, the relation of breathing holds actually for every human. The negative example is the opposite.
The example shows that 'absolutely' signals the absence of additional modal conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمطلقات الثلاث، وهي:
المطلقة العامة وهي: التي حكم فيها بإطلاق النسبة؛ أي: كونها حاصلة بالفعل؛ مثالها موجبة: كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق، وسالبة: لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالإطلاق. وإنما سميت: مطلقة؛ لأن صفة نسبتها الإطلاق، وعامة؛ لأنها أعم من الوجوديتين المذكورتين بعد، فإنها لم تقيد بنفي الدوام أو الضرورة بخلافهما، وهي بسيطة كما يعلم مما يأتي.
والوجودية اللادائمة وهي: المطلقة العامة لكن مع زيادة قيد: ولا دائما؛ مثالها موجبة: كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق لا دائما، وسالبة: لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالإطلاق لا دائما. وإنما سميت: وجودية؛ لوجود نسبتها بالفعل، واللادائمة؛ لأنها مقيدة بقولنا: لا دائما، وهي مركبة.
And the three absolutes, and they are:
The general absolute: it is that in which the relation is judged absolutely; i.e., its being actual; its example affirmative: every human is breathing absolutely, and negative: no human is breathing absolutely. It is called 'absolute' because the attribute of its relation is absoluteness, and 'general' because it is more general than the two existential propositions mentioned later, for it is not restricted by negation of perpetuity or necessity unlike them, and it is simple as will be known from what comes.
And the non-perpetual existential: it is the general absolute but with the addition of the condition: 'and not perpetually'; its example affirmative: every human is breathing absolutely not perpetually, and negative: no human is breathing absolutely not perpetually. It is called 'existential' because its relation exists actually, and 'non-perpetual' because it is restricted by our saying: 'not perpetually', and it is composite.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage defines two of the three absolute propositions (al-muṭlaqāt al-thalāth). The author first defines the general absolute (al-muṭlaqa al-ʿāmma): it is a proposition in which the relation is judged as actual existence (bi-al-fiʿl). It is called 'absolute' because its modality is absoluteness (iṭlāq), and 'general' because it is more general than the two existential types that follow, as it is not qualified by negation of perpetuity or necessity. The general absolute is simple (basīṭa). The example given is 'Every human is breathing absolutely' and its negative counterpart. Next, the non-perpetual existential (al-wujūdiyya al-lā dāʾima) is defined: it is the general absolute but with an added condition 'not perpetually'. For example, 'Every human is breathing absolutely, not perpetually'. It is called 'existential' because its relation actually exists, and 'non-perpetual' because it is restricted by the negation of perpetuity. This proposition is composite (murakkaba), meaning it implies a qualification. Note that the third absolute proposition (likely the 'specific absolute' or 'necessary absolute') is not presented in this window.

الإطلاق

Key Takeaway
The general absolute is the simplest modal proposition: it affirms actual existence without any additional temporal or modal condition.

The Non-Perpetual Existential Proposition (al-Wujūdiyya al-Lā Dāʾima)
Defines the non-perpetual existential proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the non-perpetual existential proposition, explain its composite nature, and contrast it with the general absolute.

Core Idea
The non-perpetual existential is the general absolute restricted by the condition that the relation does not hold forever.

After understanding the general absolute, we now examine a specific restriction: the non-perpetual existential. This proposition still judges the relation as actual, but adds the condition that this actuality is not perpetual. It is a composite modal type used to describe temporary states. This lesson clarifies its definition and logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term 'الوجودية اللادائمة' (non-perpetual existential) in the Arabic passage. It is introduced as the second absolute proposition.
2
Extract the defining conditions
The definition states it is the general absolute but with an added condition: 'ولا دائما' (and not perpetually). This means the relation actually exists, but not for all time.
It combines actual existence with a negation of perpetuity.
3
Understand the name
It is called 'existential' (وجودية) because the relation exists actually, and 'non-perpetual' (لا دائمة) because it is restricted by the negation of perpetuity (لا دائما).
The name clearly indicates its two components.
4
Analyze the example
Consider the example: 'كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق لا دائما' (Every human is breathing absolutely, not perpetually). This means at some point, every human breathes, but not continuously forever. The negative example is the opposite.
The condition 'not perpetually' qualifies the absoluteness.

Defining the General Absolute Proposition
Author defines the first of the three absolute propositions: the general absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general absolute proposition and distinguish it from other modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The general absolute states the relation actually occurs, with no condition of permanence or necessity.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by modality. The general absolute is a foundational type where the relation is asserted as actually happening, without any extra condition of permanence or necessity. Understanding this is key to grasping more complex modal propositions used in kalam and fiqh arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum
Find the term being defined: المطلقة العامة (the general absolute). Note that it is one of three 'absolute' propositions.
The Arabic term is explicitly introduced in the text.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'proposition with a relation' (قضية فيها نسبة). The differentia is that the relation is judged as 'absolutely occurring' (بإطلاق النسبة أي كونها حاصلة بالفعل).
This differentia distinguishes it from modal propositions that add conditions like necessity or permanence.
3
Analyze the examples
Affirmative: 'Every human is a breather absolutely' – the relation 'is a breather' is actually true for every human. Negative: 'No human is a breather absolutely' – the relation is actually false for every human.
The examples show how the absolute is instantiated in both affirmative and negative forms.
4
Understand the naming
It is called 'absolute' because the relation itself is unqualified (iṭlāq). It is called 'general' because it is broader than the two existential types that will be introduced later – it does not include any negation of permanence or necessity.
The name reflects its logical breadth compared to other absolute propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمُطْلَقَاتُ الثَّلَاثُ، وَهِيَ : (١٣) - المُطْلَقَةُ العَامَّةُ وَهِيَ : الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِإِطْلَاقِ النِّسْبَةِ ; أَيْ: كَوْنَهَا حَاصِلَةٌ بِالفِعْلِ ; مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَنَفِّسٌ بِالإطْلَاقِ، وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسِ بِالإِطْلَاقِ». وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : مُطْلَقَةٌ»; لِأَنَّ صِفَةَ نِسْبَتِهَا الإِطْلَاقُ، وَ: «عَامَّةٌ»; لِأَنَّهَا أَعَمُّ مِنَ الوُجُودِيَّتَيْنِ المَذْكُورَتَيْنِ بَعْدُ , فَإِنَّهَا لَمْ تُقَيَّدُ بِنَفْيِ الدَّوَامِ أَوِ الضَّرُورَةِ بِخِلَافِهِمَا , وَهِيَ بَسِيطَةٌ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا يَأْتِي.

It is called 'absolute' because the quality of its relation is absoluteness, and 'general' because it is more general than the two existential types mentioned later, for it is not restricted by negation of permanence or necessity unlike them, and it is simple as will be known from what follows.

Commentary
This passage defines the first of the three absolute propositions: al-muṭlaqa al-‘āmma (the general absolute). The definition states that it is a proposition in which the relation (nisba) is judged with absolute iṭlāq (absoluteness), meaning that the relation is actually occurring (ḥāṣila bi-l-fi‘l). Examples are given: for affirmative, 'Every human is a breather absolutely'; for negative, 'No human is a breather absolutely'. The term 'absolute' indicates that the relation is not qualified with any modality. The term 'general' contrasts it with the two 'existential' types that will follow: it is more general because it does not include any negation of permanence or necessity. The text further notes that this proposition is simple (basīṭa), i.e., not composed of multiple propositions, as will be clarified later. The explanation follows traditional order: definition, example, reason for naming, and characterization.

Analyzing the Non-Permanent Existential Proposition
Definition of the second absolute proposition and its composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the non-permanent existential proposition, state its composition, and convert its examples into the two constituent general absolute propositions.

Core Idea
The non-permanent existential is not simple—it is a composite of two general absolute propositions.

In this passage, al-Bajūrī defines the second of the three absolute propositions: the non-permanent existential (al-wujūdiyya al-lā dā'ima). He explains that it is the general absolute proposition with an added condition of non-permanence, and he shows that it is actually composite (murakkaba), consisting of two simpler propositions. Understanding this composite structure is crucial for correctly handling modal propositions in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Notice that the passage defines the non-permanent existential as an absolute proposition (قضية مطلقة) with the extra condition 'not permanent' (لَا دَائِمًا). Recognize it as one of the three absolute propositions alongside the general absolute and the permanent existential.
The term 'existential' (وجودية) refers to the actual existence of the relation.
2
Extract the two examples
Find the affirmative example: 'Every human is a breather absolutely, not permanently.' Then find the negative example: 'No human is a breather absolutely, not permanently.' Write them down and note the structure.
3
Break down the composite structure
For the affirmative case, identify the antecedent (الصدر): 'Every human is a breather absolutely' (general absolute affirmative). Identify the consequent (العجز): 'not permanently' (which is equivalent to a general absolute negative). Do the reverse for the negative case.
The passage explicitly says that 'not permanent' is in the force of a general absolute negative/affirmative.
4
Convert the consequent into its full propositional form
Realize that the consequent 'not permanent' is a shorthand. Expand it: for the affirmative example, 'not permanent' means 'No human is a breather absolutely in the general sense.' For the negative example, 'not permanent' means 'Every human is a breather absolutely in the general sense.' Verify that this matches the author's explanation.
This conversion shows why the proposition is composite: it expresses two separate claims.

(١٤) - وَالوُجُودِيَّةُ اللَّا دَائِمَةُ وَهِيَ : المُطْلَقَةُ العَامَّةُ لَكِنْ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ قَيْدِ: وَلَا دَائِماً»; مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَنَفِّسُ بِالإطْلَاقِ لَا دَائِماً», وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسٍ بِالإِطْلَاقِ لَا دَائِماً . وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : وُجُودِيَّةٌ»; لِوُجُودِ نِسْبَتِهَا بِالفِعْلِ، وَ «اللَّا دَائِمَةً»; لِأَنَّهَا مُقَيَّدَةٌ بِقَوْلِنَا : «لَا دَائِماً»، وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ : إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ : مِنْ مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَنَفِّسٌ بِالإِطْلَاقِ، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجُزُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : «لَا دَائِماً»; لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةٍ أَنْ يُقَالَ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسٍ بِالإِطْلَاقِ العَامِّ»; لِمَا سَبَقَ. - وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ : مِنْ مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسٍ بِالإِطْلَاقِ , فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجُزُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : لَا دَائِماً»; لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ مُتَنَفِّسٌ بالإطلاقِ العام»; لِمَا مَرَّ .
(14) The non-permanent existential: it is the general absolute but with the addition of the condition 'not permanent'; its affirmative example: 'Every human is a breather absolutely, not permanently', and negative: 'No human is a breather absolutely, not permanently'. It is called 'existential' because of the actual existence of its relation, and 'non-permanent' because it is restricted by our saying 'not permanent', and it is composite: if affirmative, it is composed of a general absolute affirmative (the antecedent) — I mean your saying for example 'Every human is a breather absolutely' — and a general absolute negative (the consequent) — I mean your saying 'not permanent' — because it is equivalent to saying 'No human is a breather absolutely in the general sense', as previously. And if negative, it is composed of a general absolute negative (the antecedent) — I mean your saying for example 'No human is a breather absolutely' — and a general absolute affirmative (the consequent) — I mean your saying 'not permanent' — because it is equivalent to saying 'Every human is a breather absolutely in the general sense', as previously.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage defines the second of the three absolute propositions: al-wujūdiyya al-lā dā'ima (the non-permanent existential). It is essentially the general absolute proposition with an added condition: 'not permanent'. Examples are given for both affirmative and negative forms. The name 'existential' reflects that the relation actually exists (bi-l-fi‘l), while 'non-permanent' indicates the restriction to non-permanence. Importantly, this proposition is composite (murakkaba): it is not a simple proposition but is equivalent to a conjunction of two general absolute propositions. The author explains the composition for the affirmative case: it consists of a general absolute affirmative (the 'antecedent' — actually the first part) and a general absolute negative (the 'consequent' — the condition 'not permanent'), because 'not permanent' is equivalent to the negative general absolute 'No human is a breather absolutely in the general sense'. Similarly, for the negative case, it is composed of a general absolute negative and a general absolute affirmative. This analysis shows how modal propositions can be reduced to simpler ones.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Composition of the Existential Non-Necessary Proposition
Author explains the composition of the existential non-necessary proposition from absolute and possible universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decompose an existential non-necessary proposition into its component propositions: an absolute universal and a possible universal.

Core Idea
The existential non-necessary proposition is a compound of an absolute universal and a possible universal.

In modal logic, propositions are classified by necessity, actuality, and possibility. The existential non-necessary proposition (الوجودية لا الضرورية) occupies a middle ground: it asserts existence without necessity. In this passage, the author breaks down its composition from two simpler propositions: an absolute universal and a possible universal. Understanding this decomposition is key to handling modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the existential non-necessary proposition is affirmative or negative. The composition differs slightly between the two.
2
Locate the sadr (first part)
For an affirmative proposition, the sadr is an absolute universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is a breather absolutely'). For a negative, it is an absolute universal negative.
3
Locate the ajz (last part)
The ajz is 'not by necessity'. Recognize that this is equivalent to a possible universal: for affirmative, a possible universal negative; for negative, a possible universal affirmative.
4
Verify equivalence of ajz
Understand that 'not by necessity' negates necessity from the contradictory of the sadr. This is the definition of general possibility (الإمكان العام).
5
Apply to both cases
Practice by decomposing both an affirmative and a negative existential non-necessary proposition into their sadr and ajz components.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها

(٥٦) وَالأَوَّلُ: «المَوضُوعُ بِالحَمْلِيَّة وَالآخِرُ المَحْمُولُ بِالسَّوِيَّة

إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ : مِنْ مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَنَفِّسٌ بِالإِطْلَاقِ، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْزُ ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : «لَا دَائِماً»؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةٍ أَنْ يُقَالَ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسٍ بِالإِطْلَاقِ العَامِّ»؛ لِمَا سَبَقَ.

- وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ : مِنْ مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسٍ بِالإِطْلَاقِ ، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْزُ ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : لَا دَائِماً»؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ مُتَنَفِّسٌ بالإطلاقِ العام»؛ لِمَا مَرَّ .

(١٥) - وَالوُجُودِيَّةُ اللَّا ضَرُورِيَّةُ وَهِيَ : المُطْلَقَةُ العَامَّةُ لَكِنْ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ قَيْدِ : اللَّا ضَرُورَةَ » ؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَنَفِّسٌ بِالإِطْلَاقِ لَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ»، وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفْسٍ بِالإطْلَاقِ لَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ .

وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : وُجُودِيَّةٌ» ؛ لِمَا مَرَّ ، وَ : «اللَّا ضَرُورِيَّةَ»؛ لِأَنَّهَا مُقَيَّدَةٌ بِقَوْلِنَا : «لَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ»،

وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ :
- إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ : مِنْ مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَنَفِّسٌ بِالإِطْلَاقِ، فَمُمْكِنَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْزُ ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : «لَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفْسٍ بِالإِمْكَانِ العَامِّ ؛ لِمَا عَلِمْتَ مِنْ أَنَّ الإِمْكَانَ العَامَّ هُوَ سَلْبُ الضَّرُورَةِ عَنِ الطَّرَفِ المُخَالِفِ.
- وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ : مِنْ مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسٍ بِالإطْلَاقِ، فَمُمْكِنَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْزُ ؛ أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : «لَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ» ؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ مُتَنَفِّسُ بِالإِمْكَانِ العَامِّ»؛ لِمَا ذُكِرَ .

(1) الشرشيمي : قوله : (لِمَا عَلِمْتَ مِنْ أَنَّ الإِمْكَانَ هذا تعليل لكون قوله : «لَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ» في قوَّة الممكنة العامة؛ لأنَّك إذا قلت: «لَا شَيْء مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِمُتَنَفِّسِ بِالإِمْكَان العام، دلَّ على سلب الضرورة عن قولك : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ مُتَنَفِّسٌ بِالإِطْلَاقِ»، وهذا هو صدر القضيَّة الَّذي قيَّدته بقولك : لَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ»، فهذه القضيَّة المفسّرة مفيدة سلب الضرورة عن صدر الجملة، كما أفاده المفسّر، وهو قولك: «لَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ».
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings.

(56) The first [part] is the subject in a categorical [proposition] and the last is the predicate uniformly.

If it is affirmative: it is composed of an absolute universal affirmative, which is the first part; I mean your statement for example: 'Every human is a breather absolutely.' Then an absolute universal negative, which is the last part; I mean your statement: 'Not always' because it is in the power of saying: 'No human is a breather with general absoluteness' due to what preceded.

- And if it is negative: it is composed of an absolute universal negative, which is the first part; I mean your statement for example: 'No human is a breather absolutely.' Then an absolute universal affirmative, which is the last part; I mean your statement: 'Not always' because it is in the power of saying: 'Every human is a breather with general absoluteness' due to what preceded.

(15) The existential non-necessary [proposition] is: the absolute universal but with the addition of the condition: 'not necessary'. Its example affirmative: 'Every human is a breather absolutely, not by necessity.' And negative: 'No human is a breather absolutely, not by necessity.'

And indeed it is called 'existential' due to what preceded, and 'non-necessary' because it is restricted by our saying: 'not by necessity'.

And it is compound:
- If it is affirmative: from an absolute universal affirmative, which is the first part; I mean your statement for example: 'Every human is a breather absolutely.' Then a possible universal negative, which is the last part; I mean your statement: 'not by necessity' because it is in the power of saying: 'No human is a breather with general possibility' because you know that general possibility is the negation of necessity from the opposite side.
- And if it is negative: from an absolute universal negative, which is the first part; I mean your statement for example: 'No human is a breather absolutely.' Then a possible universal affirmative, which is the last part; I mean your statement: 'not by necessity' because it is in the power of saying: 'Every human is a breather with general possibility' due to what was mentioned.

Al-Shirshimi: His saying: 'Because you know that possibility...' is an explanation for why his saying 'not by necessity' is in the power of the general possible; because if you say: 'No human is a breather with general possibility', it indicates the negation of necessity from your saying: 'Every human is a breather absolutely', and this is the first part of the proposition which you restricted with your saying: 'not by necessity'. So this interpreted proposition is useful for negating necessity from the first part of the sentence, as the interpreter indicated, and it is your saying: 'not by necessity'.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage defines the composition of the 'existential non-necessary' proposition, a type of modal proposition. The author first explains how a proposition like 'Not always' (لا دائماً) is composed of an absolute universal affirmative and a negative for the affirmative case, and vice versa for the negative case. Then he introduces the existential non-necessary proposition, which is an absolute universal proposition with the added condition 'not by necessity'. He gives examples: 'Every human is a breather absolutely, not by necessity' (affirmative) and 'No human is a breather absolutely, not by necessity' (negative). The name 'existential' refers to its affirmative mode, and 'non-necessary' to the restriction. The author then breaks down the compound structure: for the affirmative, the first part (صدر) is an absolute universal affirmative, and the last part (عجز) is a possible universal negative, because 'not by necessity' is equivalent to the negation of necessity from the opposite side, which is general possibility. For the negative, the first part is an absolute universal negative and the last part is a possible universal affirmative. The gloss from al-Shirshimi reinforces this by explaining that 'not by necessity' functions like a possible universal, negating necessity from the first part.

Key Terms
الوجودية لا الضرورية (al-wujūdiyya lā al-ḍarūriyya): A proposition that is absolute universal but with the added condition 'not by necessity', indicating existence without necessity. [Note: Stable term in modal logic; no significant shift.]
مطلقة عامة (muṭlaqa ʻāmma): A proposition with no modal qualification, asserting universally without temporal or modal restriction. [Note: Some logicians divide absolute into general and special; this text uses it as the basic non-modal.]
ممكنة عامة (mumkina ʻāmma): A proposition affirming possibility in a general sense, equated here with negation of necessity from the opposite side. [Note: General possibility is standardly defined as negation of necessity; no shift.]
صدر (ṣadr): The first part of a compound proposition, from which the compound is formed. [Note: Used in logic as the 'beginning' or 'forepart' of a proposition; stable.]
عجز (ʻajz): The last part of a compound proposition, the part that adds qualification. [Note: Stable; also means 'weakness' but here technical.]
الإمكان العام (al-imkān al-ʻāmm): General possibility, defined as negation of necessity from the opposite side (i.e., the proposition's contradictory). [Note: Standard definition in logic; no shift.]

Key Takeaway
The existential non-necessary proposition is a compound of an absolute universal and a possible universal, where the ajz 'not by necessity' is equivalent to a possible universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Propositions

Subject and Predicate: Logical Rank vs. Word Order
Author explains the reason for naming the first part 'subject' and the second 'predicate' with grammatical analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject and predicate in any proposition based on logical rank, not word order, and explain the reasoning behind their naming.

Core Idea
The subject is logically prior, but may appear last in speech.

In this passage, the author explains why the first part of a proposition is called the 'subject' (al-mawḍūʻ) and the second the 'predicate' (al-maḥmūl). The key insight is that these terms refer to logical rank (al-rutba) in meaning, not necessarily the order of words. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly analyzing propositions in Islamic logic and related sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of a proposition
Every categorical proposition has a subject (what is being talked about) and a predicate (what is said about the subject). For example, in 'The world is temporally originated', 'the world' is the subject and 'temporally originated' is the predicate.
2
Understand the naming reason
The subject is called 'موضوع' because it is imagined as something placed (وضع) for another thing to be carried upon it. The predicate is 'محمول' meaning that which is carried. The subject is the foundation, and the predicate is the description.
This metaphorical naming helps remember their logical roles.
3
Distinguish logical rank from word order
The subject is first in rank (meaning-wise) even if mentioned last in the sentence; the predicate is last in rank even if mentioned first. Logical priority belongs to the described (al-mawṣūf) over the description (al-ṣifa).
Do not be fooled by Arabic sentences where the predicate comes first.
4
Apply the grammatical analogy
Grammarians place the mubtada' before the khabar in nominal sentences, reflecting logical order. However, the agent (fāʻil) comes after the verb because the verb governs it – an exception due to verbal governance, not logical precedence. This shows that word order can vary while logical order remains fixed.
The exception confirms the rule: logical precedence is based on meaning, not grammar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (المَوضُوعُ خَبَرٌ عَنِ الأَوَّلِ)) .

وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يُتَخَيَّلُ أَنَّهُ كَشَيْءٍ وُضِعَ لِيُحْمَلَ عَلَيْهِ غَيْرُهُ؛ كَمَا قَالَهُ ابْنُ يَعْقُوبَ (۲) . وَالمُسَمَّى بِذَلِكَ : الأَوَّلُ فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَإِنْ ذُكِرَ آخِراً، كَمَا أَنَّ المُسَمَّى بـ «المَحْمُولِ»: الآخِرُ فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَإِنْ ذُكِرَ أَوَّلاً .

وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ المَوضُوعُ أَوَّلاً فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَالمَحْمُولُ آخِراً فِيهَا ؛ لأنَّ المَوْضُوعَ مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهِ بِالمَحْمُولِ، وَالمَحْكُومُ بِهِ وَصْفٌ لِلمَحْكُوم عَلَيْهِ فِي المَعْنَى ، وَالمَوْصُوفُ سَابِقٌ عَلَى صِفَتِهِ، وَلِهَذَا جَعَلَ النُّحاةُ رُتْبَةَ المُبْتَدَأَ التَّقَدُّمُ، وَرُتْبَةَ الخَبَرِ التَّأْخُرُ ، وَإِنَّمَا جَعَلُوا رُتْبَةَ الفَاعِلِ التَّأْخُرُ عَنِ الفِعْلِ مَعَ أَنَّهُ مَوْصُوفٌ فِي المَعْنَى لِأَمْرٍ لَفْظِيٍّ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الفِعْلَ عَامِلٌ فِيهِ، وَرُتُبةُ العَامِلِ التَّقَدُّمُ عَلَى مَعْمُولِهِ، فَلْيُتَامَّلْ .
His saying: 'The subject is a predicate' about the first. [Commentary:] It is only named thus because it is imagined as something placed for another to be predicated upon it, as Ibn Ya'qub said. And what is named thus is the first in rank even if mentioned last, just as what is named 'the predicate' is the last in rank even if mentioned first.

And the subject is only first in rank and the predicate last in rank because the subject is that which is judged by the predicate, and that which is judged is a description of the judged object in meaning, and the described precedes its description. For this reason, the grammarians made the rank of the subject (mubtada') to be preceding and the rank of the predicate (khabar) to be following. They only made the rank of the agent (fa'il) to follow the verb despite it being described in meaning due to a verbal matter, namely that the verb is an 'amil (governing word) in it, and the rank of the 'amil is to precede its object of governance. So let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage explains why the first part of a proposition is called 'subject' (موضوع) and the second 'predicate' (محمول). The author begins with a quotation from the matn that the subject is 'a predicate about the first' — i.e., the term 'subject' is used for the first part. The reason for naming it 'subject' is that it is imagined as something placed (وضع) for something else to be predicated upon it. The subject is first in rank (الرتبة) even if it is mentioned later in the utterance, similarly, the predicate is last in rank even if mentioned first. The justification is that the subject is what is being judged (محكوم عليه) by the predicate, and the predicate is a description (وصف) of the subject in meaning. The described (الموصوف) is logically prior to its description. The author then draws an analogy with Arabic grammar: the mubtada' (subject of a nominal sentence) is placed before the khabar (predicate) because it is the topic. However, the agent (fa'il) comes after the verb despite being the subject in meaning, because the verb is an 'amil (governing word) and the 'amil naturally precedes its object. This exception is due to a verbal rule, not logical order. The passage thus clarifies that the terms 'subject' and 'predicate' are based on logical precedence in meaning, not necessarily word order.

الرتبة

A) Because it is always mentioned first in a sentence
B) Because it is imagined as something placed for another to be predicated upon it
C) Because it is derived from the verb 'to place'
D) Because it is the known part of the proposition

Key Takeaway
The subject is logically first in meaning as the described, even if it appears later in speech; the predicate is logically last as the description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the Quranic verse 'Hast thou not seen how thy Lord dealt with the people of the elephant?' (105:1). Identify the subject and predicate in the implied proposition 'Thy Lord dealt with the people of the elephant'. Explain which part is first in rank.
?

Definition of Conditional Proposition
Defines conditional proposition and its differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a conditional proposition and distinguish it from a categorical proposition based on the mode of judgment.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition is not about what is, but about what depends on something else.

In logic, we classify propositions by the nature of their judgment. This lesson focuses on the definition of a conditional proposition, which is the foundation for analyzing conditional arguments used in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition in the matn
Find the phrase in the matn that defines conditional: 'وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: شَرْطِيَّةٌ'. This is the core definition.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'proposition' (implied). The differentia is 'judgment made on the mode of conditional (ta'liq)'. Unlike categoricals, which attribute a predicate directly, conditionals involve a connection between two parts.
The differentia is what distinguishes a conditional from a categorical.
3
Understand the scope of ta'liq
The commentary clarifies that ta'liq is not limited to explicit 'if-then' but includes implicit opposition. This broadening is necessary to include disjunctive conditionals.
See the objection and answer: ta'liq = connection between two parts, even if opposition.
4
Distinguish from categorical propositions
Categorical propositions make a direct affirmation or negation without condition. For example, 'Zayd is standing' is categorical. 'If Zayd is standing, then he is alive' is conditional. The mode of judgment is what sets them apart.
This distinction is crucial for identifying argument forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(متن) وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَتَنْقَسِمْ (شرح الباجوري) قَوْلُهُ : (وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ (١) ... إلخ) أَيْ: وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا حُكْماً كَايْناً عَلَى وَجْهِ التَّعْلِيقِ(٢) لَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الحَمْلِ، فَإِنَّهَا إلخ، وَعَلَى هَذَا التَّقْدِيرِ فَ عَلَى» بَاقِيَةٌ عَلَى بَابِهَا . وَيُحْتَمَلُ - وَهُوَ الَّذِي اقْتَصَرَ عَلَيْهِ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ وَتَبِعَهُ غَيْرُهُ - أَنَّهَا بِمَعْنَى : «البَاءِ»؛ وَالمَعْنَى : وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّعْلِيقِ، فَإِنَّهَا ... إلخ (۳). فَإِنْ قِيلَ : لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ التَّعْلِيقَ تَوْقِيفُ شَيْءٍ عَلَى شَيْءٍ آخَرَ، وَهَذَا خَاصٌ بِالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ، مَعَ أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيَقْسُمُ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ : إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَإِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ. أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ «التَّعْلِيقِ فِي كَلَامِهِ : الرَّبْطُ بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ وَلَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ العِنَادِ، (حاشية الأنبابي) قوله : الرَّبْطُ بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ وَلَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ العِنَادِ) أي : سواء كان على وجه التَّوقف والتَّرتُب، أو على وجه التّنافي، والمراد : ربط مخصوص يشمل القسمين، لا مطلق ربط يشملهما وغيرهما، وإلا لم يحصل التمييز عن الحملية، ولم يكن فائدةً لقوله : عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ». (شرح الباجوري تابع) أَوْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ : مَا يَشْمَلُ التَّعْلِيقَ صَرِيحاً كَمَا فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ، أَوِ اسْتِلْزاماً كَمَا فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا تَسْتَلْزِمُ تَوَقُفَ تُبُوتِ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى انْتِفَاءِ الآخَرِ، أَوْ تَوَقُفَ انْتِفَاءِ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى ثُبُوتِ الْآخَرِ، فَكَأَنَّهُ قِيلَ : إِنِ انْتَفَى هَذَا ثَبَتَ هَذَا، أَوْ إِنْ ثَبَتَ هَذَا انْتَفَى هَذَا ، فَلْيُتَامَّلْ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَتَنْقَسِمْ... إلخ) قَسَّمَهَا المُصَنِّفُ إِلَى : مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ ;
(Matn) And if in it a judgment is made on the mode of conditional, then it is conditional, and it divides. (Bajuri commentary) His statement: 'And if on the mode of conditional' i.e., if in it a judgment is made as a judgment occurring on the face of conditional, not on the face of categorical, then it is... etc. On this estimation, 'on' remains on its [literal] meaning. And it is possible—and this is what al-Mallawi confined himself to and others followed—that it is in the sense of 'with'; the meaning: and if in it there is a judgment with conditional, then it is... etc. For it is said: it is not hidden that conditional is the suspending of one thing upon another, and this is specific to the conditional connected, although the author will divide the conditional into connected conditional and disjunctive conditional. It is answered: that what is intended by 'conditional' in his speech is the connection between the two parts even if on the face of opposition. (Anbabbi gloss) His statement: 'the connection between the two parts even if on the face of opposition' i.e., whether it is on the face of dependency and ordering, or on the face of mutual exclusion. And what is intended is a specific connection that includes the two divisions, not an absolute connection that includes them and others; otherwise no distinction from the categorical would be obtained, and there would be no benefit in his statement 'on the mode of conditional'. (Bajuri continued) Or that what is intended includes conditional explicitly as in the connected, or implicitly as in the disjunctive; because it entails the dependency of the affirmation of one on the negation of the other, or the dependency of the negation of one on the affirmation of the other, as if it is said: if this is negated, this is affirmed, or if this is affirmed, this is negated. So let it be pondered. His statement: 'and it divides'... etc. The author divided it into connected and disjunctive.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage defines a conditional proposition and gives its primary division. The matn states that if a judgment is made in the mode of conditional (ta'liq), the proposition is called conditional (shartiyya). The commentator al-Bajuri explains that 'on the mode of conditional' means the judgment is made with conditional dependency, not as a simple categorical attribution. There is a discussion about the preposition 'ala: whether it retains its literal meaning or means 'with'. An objection is raised: conditional (ta'liq) specifically means suspending one thing on another, which only applies to the connected conditional (muttasila), yet the author later divides conditional into both connected and disjunctive. The answer is that the intended meaning of ta'liq here is a general connection between the two parts, even if it is opposition (in the disjunctive). Al-Anbabbi adds that this connection must be specific enough to exclude categoricals. Al-Bajuri gives another answer: ta'liq can be explicit (as in connected) or implicit (as in disjunctive, where the link is a dependency of affirmation/negation). The passage concludes by stating that the author divides the conditional into connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila).

شرطية
حملية

B) The judgment being made on the mode of conditional (ta'liq)

D) The truth or falsity of the proposition

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition is defined by its mode of judgment (ta'liq), which is a connection between two parts that may be explicit dependency or implicit opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following proposition is conditional or categorical: 'The world is created.' Explain your answer using the differentia of ta'liq.
?
Take a disjunctive proposition from fiqh: 'Either the sale is valid or it is invalid.' Rewrite it as an implied conditional following the logic of the text (e.g., 'If it is not valid, then it is invalid').

Primary Division of Conditionals: Connected and Disjunctive
Divides conditional into connected and disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) conditionals and explain how disjunctives imply a conditional relationship.

Core Idea
Disjunctive propositions are conditional because they imply a dependency between affirmation and negation.

After defining conditional propositions, we now examine their primary division. Understanding the two types is essential for analyzing conditional arguments in Islamic logic, where both connected and disjunctive conditionals are used in reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types from the text
The matn states that the conditional divides into connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). Locate this division in the passage.
The division is at the end: 'قسّمها المصنف إلى: متصلة، ومنفصلة'.
2
Define each type
Connected conditional: judgment that if one thing is, then another is (explicit dependency). Disjunctive conditional: judgment that one of two things is the case, with opposition between them.
Use the definitions from the commentary: muttasila has explicit ta'liq; munfasila has implicit ta'liq through opposition.
3
Understand how disjunctives are implicitly conditional
The text says the disjunctive entails that affirmation of one depends on negation of the other, and vice versa. So it can be rephrased as 'If this is not, then that is' or 'If this is, then that is not'.
This is key to seeing why munfasila falls under conditional.
4
Apply the division to examples
Practice classifying propositions from Islamic sciences as muttasila or munfasila. For example, 'If it rains, then the roof leaks' is muttasila. 'Either the world is created or it is eternal' is munfasila.
Ensure the examples fit the definition of conditional (not categorical).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(متن) وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَتَنْقَسِمْ (شرح الباجوري) قَوْلُهُ : (وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ (١) ... إلخ) أَيْ: وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا حُكْماً كَايْناً عَلَى وَجْهِ التَّعْلِيقِ(٢) لَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الحَمْلِ، فَإِنَّهَا إلخ، وَعَلَى هَذَا التَّقْدِيرِ فَ عَلَى» بَاقِيَةٌ عَلَى بَابِهَا . وَيُحْتَمَلُ - وَهُوَ الَّذِي اقْتَصَرَ عَلَيْهِ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ وَتَبِعَهُ غَيْرُهُ - أَنَّهَا بِمَعْنَى : «البَاءِ»؛ وَالمَعْنَى : وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّعْلِيقِ، فَإِنَّهَا ... إلخ (۳). فَإِنْ قِيلَ : لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ التَّعْلِيقَ تَوْقِيفُ شَيْءٍ عَلَى شَيْءٍ آخَرَ، وَهَذَا خَاصٌ بِالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ، مَعَ أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيَقْسُمُ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ : إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَإِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ. أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ «التَّعْلِيقِ فِي كَلَامِهِ : الرَّبْطُ بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ وَلَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ العِنَادِ، (حاشية الأنبابي) قوله : الرَّبْطُ بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ وَلَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ العِنَادِ) أي : سواء كان على وجه التَّوقف والتَّرتُب، أو على وجه التّنافي، والمراد : ربط مخصوص يشمل القسمين، لا مطلق ربط يشملهما وغيرهما، وإلا لم يحصل التمييز عن الحملية، ولم يكن فائدةً لقوله : عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ». (شرح الباجوري تابع) أَوْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ : مَا يَشْمَلُ التَّعْلِيقَ صَرِيحاً كَمَا فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ، أَوِ اسْتِلْزاماً كَمَا فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا تَسْتَلْزِمُ تَوَقُفَ تُبُوتِ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى انْتِفَاءِ الآخَرِ، أَوْ تَوَقُفَ انْتِفَاءِ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى ثُبُوتِ الْآخَرِ، فَكَأَنَّهُ قِيلَ : إِنِ انْتَفَى هَذَا ثَبَتَ هَذَا، أَوْ إِنْ ثَبَتَ هَذَا انْتَفَى هَذَا ، فَلْيُتَامَّلْ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَتَنْقَسِمْ... إلخ) قَسَّمَهَا المُصَنِّفُ إِلَى : مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ ;
(Matn) And if in it a judgment is made on the mode of conditional, then it is conditional, and it divides. (Bajuri commentary) His statement: 'And if on the mode of conditional' i.e., if in it a judgment is made as a judgment occurring on the face of conditional, not on the face of categorical, then it is... etc. On this estimation, 'on' remains on its [literal] meaning. And it is possible—and this is what al-Mallawi confined himself to and others followed—that it is in the sense of 'with'; the meaning: and if in it there is a judgment with conditional, then it is... etc. For it is said: it is not hidden that conditional is the suspending of one thing upon another, and this is specific to the conditional connected, although the author will divide the conditional into connected conditional and disjunctive conditional. It is answered: that what is intended by 'conditional' in his speech is the connection between the two parts even if on the face of opposition. (Anbabbi gloss) His statement: 'the connection between the two parts even if on the face of opposition' i.e., whether it is on the face of dependency and ordering, or on the face of mutual exclusion. And what is intended is a specific connection that includes the two divisions, not an absolute connection that includes them and others; otherwise no distinction from the categorical would be obtained, and there would be no benefit in his statement 'on the mode of conditional'. (Bajuri continued) Or that what is intended includes conditional explicitly as in the connected, or implicitly as in the disjunctive; because it entails the dependency of the affirmation of one on the negation of the other, or the dependency of the negation of one on the affirmation of the other, as if it is said: if this is negated, this is affirmed, or if this is affirmed, this is negated. So let it be pondered. His statement: 'and it divides'... etc. The author divided it into connected and disjunctive.

Commentary
This passage defines a conditional proposition and gives its primary division. The matn states that if a judgment is made in the mode of conditional (ta'liq), the proposition is called conditional (shartiyya). The commentator al-Bajuri explains that 'on the mode of conditional' means the judgment is made with conditional dependency, not as a simple categorical attribution. There is a discussion about the preposition 'ala: whether it retains its literal meaning or means 'with'. An objection is raised: conditional (ta'liq) specifically means suspending one thing on another, which only applies to the connected conditional (muttasila), yet the author later divides conditional into both connected and disjunctive. The answer is that the intended meaning of ta'liq here is a general connection between the two parts, even if it is opposition (in the disjunctive). Al-Anbabbi adds that this connection must be specific enough to exclude categoricals. Al-Bajuri gives another answer: ta'liq can be explicit (as in connected) or implicit (as in disjunctive, where the link is a dependency of affirmation/negation). The passage concludes by stating that the author divides the conditional into connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila).

Propositions
beginner
5 min
The Four Quantification Types of Conditional Propositions
Enumerates the four kinds of conditionals with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any conditional proposition as makhsusa, kulliyya, juz'iyya, or muhmala based on its quantifier indicators, in both connected and disjunctive forms.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions are quantified just like categorical ones: specific, universal, particular, or indeterminate.

In the previous lesson you learned about connected and disjunctive conditionals. Now we see that each type can be further divided by the scope of the judgment: does it apply to one situation, all situations, some, or is it unspecified? This classification mirrors the quantification of categorical propositions and is essential for evaluating logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the connective type
First note whether the conditional is connected (muttasila, using 'if' or 'when' e.g. 'إن') or disjunctive (munfasila, using 'either...or' e.g. 'إما'). This is the first division of the text.
2
Detect the quantifier indicator
Look for words that specify the scope: 'now' (الآن) indicates specific (makhsusa); 'whenever' (كلما) or 'always' (دائماً) indicate universal (kulliyya); 'it may be' (قد يكون) indicates particular (juz'iyya); no indicator means indeterminate (muhmala).
The indicator may appear at the beginning or within the proposition.
3
Classify the proposition
Based on the indicator, assign the proposition to one of the four kinds. Remember: makhsusa is restricted to a specific situation, kulliyya covers all possible situations, juz'iyya covers some, and muhmala leaves the scope open.
4
Verify with examples from the text
Test your classification against the examples given: e.g. 'If you come now, I will honor you' is makhsusa; 'Always, the number is either even or odd' is kulliyya; 'It may be that if this is an animal, it is a human' is juz'iyya; 'If this is a human, it is an animal' is muhmala.
The same quantifier can apply to both connected and disjunctive conditionals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَتَنْقَسِمْ... إلخ) قَسَّمَهَا المُصَنِّفُ إِلَى : مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ ; (ثم قال) (١) - فَالأُوْلَى: «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَضْعِ مُعَيَّنٍ مِنَ الأَوْضَاعِ المُمْكِنَةِ»؛ أَيْ: حَالٍ مُعَيَّنٍ مِنَ الأَحْوَالِ المُمْكِنَةِ ; مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ نَحْوُ : إِنْ جِئْتَنِي الآنَ، أَكْرَمْتُكَ» (١) ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ نَحْوُ : «زَيْدُ الآنَ: إِمَّا كَاتِبٌ، أَوْ غَيْرُ كَاتِبِ» (٢). (۲) - وَالثَّانِيَةُ : مَا ذُكِرَ فِيهَا مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمِ جَمِيعِ الأَوْضَاعِ» ; مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ : كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ ، فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ (۳) ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ : دَائِماً : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً ، أَوْ فَرْداً». (۳) - وَالثَّالِثَةُ : مَا ذُكِرَ فِيهَا مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمِ بَعْضِ الأَوْضَاع» ; مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ هَذَا حَيَوَاناً ، كَانَ إِنْسَاناً » (٤) ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ : قَدْ يَكُونُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ حَيَوَاناً ، أَوْ فَرَساً». (٤) - وَالرَّابِعَةُ: مَا لَمْ يُذْكَرْ فِيهَا شَيْءٌ مِنْ ذَلِكَ» ; مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ: «إِنْ كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً ، كَانَ حَيَوَاناً ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، أَوْ فَرْداً»، فَتَنَبَّه . (حاشية الأنبابي) قوله : (إِلَى مَخْصُوصَةٍ، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ) ظاهر هذا : أنَّ الكلية والجزئية والإهمال لا تجري في المخصوصة، وهو طريقة، وهناك طريقة أخرى مشى عليها السنوسي تجعل هذه الأقسام الثلاثة في المخصوصة أيضاً.
His statement: 'and it divides'... etc. The author divided it into connected and disjunctive. Then he said: (1) The first: that in which a judgment is made on a specific situation from the possible situations; i.e., a specific state from the possible states; its example in connected: 'If you come to me now, I will honor you'; and in disjunctive: 'Zayd now: either is a writer or is not a writer'. (2) The second: that in which there is mentioned what indicates the generalization of all situations; its example in connected: 'Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime is present'; and in disjunctive: 'Always: either the number is even or odd'. (3) The third: that in which there is mentioned what indicates the generalization of some situations; its example in connected: 'It may be that if this is an animal, it is a human'; and in disjunctive: 'It may be: either the thing is an animal or a horse'. (4) The fourth: that in which nothing of that is mentioned; its example in connected: 'If this is a human, it is an animal'; and in disjunctive: 'Either the number is even or odd'. So be alert. (Anbabbi gloss) His statement: 'to specific, universal...' The apparent meaning is that universality, particularity, and indeterminateness do not occur in the specific [type]; this is one method. There is another method followed by al-Sanusi that makes these three divisions also in the specific.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four kinds of conditional propositions (shartiyya), applicable to both connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) types. The four kinds are: (1) Makhsusa (specific): the judgment is made with respect to a specific situation or time, e.g., 'If you come now, I will honor you' (connected), or 'Zayd now is either a writer or not' (disjunctive). (2) Kulliyya (universal): the judgment covers all possible situations, indicated by words like 'whenever' or 'always', e.g., 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists' (connected), or 'Always, the number is either even or odd' (disjunctive). (3) Juz'iyya (particular): the judgment covers some situations, indicated by 'it may be that', e.g., 'It may be that if this is an animal, it is a human' (connected), or 'It may be that the thing is either an animal or a horse' (disjunctive). (4) Muhmala (indeterminate): no quantification is indicated, e.g., 'If this is a human, it is an animal' (connected), or 'The number is either even or odd' (disjunctive). Al-Anbabbi notes a methodological point: in one view, the makhsusa type does not admit further quantification (kulliyya, juz'iyya, muhmala), while al-Sanusi's method applies these to makhsusa as well.

Key Terms
مخصوصة
كلية
جزئية
مهملة

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are classified into four types based on quantification: specific, universal, particular, and indeterminate.
