Conditional Propositions: Connected and Disjoined
Definition of conditional and its two main types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected and disjoined conditional propositions and identify their defining features.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition ties one claim to another, either as a necessary connection or an exclusive separation.

In Islamic logic, conditional propositions (shartiyya) form the basis for reasoning about cause and effect, obligations, and theological arguments. This lesson introduces the two fundamental types: connected (muttaṣila) and disjoined (munfaṣila), explaining how each expresses a different kind of conditional relationship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of a conditional proposition
Read the passage: 'If on the condition in it a judgment was made, then it is a conditional.' Understand that a conditional is a proposition that links a judgment to a condition.
The definition includes both explicit and implicit conditionality.
2
Distinguish connected from disjoined
Connected conditionals explicitly state a condition and its result (e.g., 'If you come, I honor you'). Disjoined conditionals imply a condition through mutual exclusivity (e.g., 'Either Zayd is a writer or not'). The passage explains that the disjoined entails dependence of affirmation on negation or vice versa.
The disjoined can be rephrased as a conditional: 'If not A then B' or 'If A then not B'.
3
Analyze the logical structure
For connected: 'If A then B' (A implies B). For disjoined: 'Either A or B' where A and B cannot both be true (exclusive). The passage shows how the disjoined can be rephrased as a conditional: 'If not A then B' or 'If A then not B'.
Al-Bajuri clarifies that the disjoined implicitly contains a conditional dependency.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Note that the examples given (sun rising implying daytime, number being even or odd) are from natural and mathematical domains, but the same structure applies in fiqh and kalam (e.g., 'If the prayer is performed correctly, it is valid' is connected; 'Either this act is wajib or mubah' is disjoined).
Recognizing the type helps in evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥٧) وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَتَنْقَسِمْ. قَالَ الباجوري: أَوْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ : مَا يَشْمَلُ التَّعْلِيقَ صَرِيحاً كَمَا فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ، أَوِ اسْتِلْزاماً كَمَا فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا تَسْتَلْزِمُ تَوَقُفَ تُبُوتِ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى انْتِفَاءِ الآخَرِ، أَوْ تَوَقُفَ انْتِفَاءِ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى ثُبُوتِ الْآخَرِ، فَكَأَنَّهُ قِيلَ : إِنِ انْتَفَى هَذَا ثَبَتَ هَذَا، أَوْ إِنْ ثَبَتَ هَذَا انْتَفَى هَذَا ، فَلْيُتَامَّلْ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَتَنْقَسِمْ... إلخ) قَسَّمَهَا المُصَنِّفُ إِلَى : مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ ; وَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى : مَخصُوصَةٍ، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ، وَجُزْئِيَّةٍ، وَمُهْمَلَةٍ. (۱) - فَالأُوْلَى: «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَضْعِ مُعَيَّنٍ مِنَ الأَوْضَاعِ المُمْكِنَةِ»؛ أَيْ: حَالٍ مُعَيَّنٍ مِنَ الأَحْوَالِ المُمْكِنَةِ ; مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ نَحْوُ : إِنْ جِئْتَنِي الآنَ، أَكْرَمْتُكَ» (١) ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ نَحْوُ : «زَيْدُ الآنَ: إِمَّا كَاتِبٌ، أَوْ غَيْرُ كَاتِبِ» (٢). (۲) - وَالثَّانِيَةُ : مَا ذُكِرَ فِيهَا مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمِ جَمِيعِ الأَوْضَاعِ»؛ مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ : كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ ، فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ (۳) ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ : دَائِماً : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً ، أَوْ فَرْداً». (۳) - وَالثَّالِثَةُ : مَا ذُكِرَ فِيهَا مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمِ بَعْضِ الأَوْضَاع» ; مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ هَذَا حَيَوَاناً ، كَانَ إِنْسَاناً » (٤) ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ : قَدْ يَكُونُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ حَيَوَاناً ، أَوْ فَرَساً». (٤) - وَالرَّابِعَةُ: مَا لَمْ يُذْكَرْ فِيهَا شَيْءٌ مِنْ ذَلِكَ» ; مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ: «إِنْ كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً ، كَانَ حَيَوَاناً ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، أَوْ فَرْداً»، فَتَنَبَّه. قَالَ الأنبابي: قوله : (إِلَى مَخْصُوصَةٍ، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ) ظاهر هذا : أنَّ الكلية والجزئية والإهمال لا تجري في المخصوصة، وهو طريقة، وهناك طريقة أخرى مشى عليها السنوسي تجعل هذه الأقسام الثلاثة في المخصوصة أيضاً. (1) الشرشيمي: قوله : (إِنْ جِئْتَنِي الآن) أي : فالمجيء الآن حالٌ مِنَ الأحوال التي للمخاطب قد حكم عليه بالإكرام؛ أي: ربط به الإكرام. (2) الشرشيمي: قوله : (زَيْدُ (الآن) أي: كون زيد في هذا الزَّمان حال من أحواله، بخلاف كونه في صغره، فإنَّه لا يمكن كتابته. (3) الشرشيمي: قوله : كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ) أي: سواء كانت حارَّةٌ، أو متوسطة، أو ضعيفة، منكسفة أو لا ؛ مِنَ الأحوال الممكنة، ولا يقال سواء كانت أكلة أو شاربة؛ لأنَّه لا يمكن. (4) الشرشيمي: قوله : (قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ ... إلخ) أي : قد يكون في بعض الأوقات ترتب الإنسانيَّة على الحيوانية؛ بأن يكون هناك غير الإنسان؛ أي: وقد لا يكون بأن يكون الحيوان غير الإنسان كـ: «الحمار».
(57) And if on the condition in it a judgment was made, then it is a conditional, and it divides. Al-Bajuri said: Or that the intended [meaning] is what includes the condition explicitly as in the connected, or implicitly as in the disjoined; because it entails the dependence of the affirmation of one of them on the negation of the other, or the dependence of the negation of one of them on the affirmation of the other, so it is as if it was said: If this is negated, this is affirmed, or if this is affirmed, this is negated, so let it be pondered. His statement: (and it divides... etc.) The author divided it into: connected and disjoined; and each of them divides into: particular, universal, partial, and unspecified. (1) The first: 'That in which a judgment is made on a specific situation from among possible situations'; i.e., a specific state from among possible states; its connected example is like: 'If you come to me now, I will honor you' (1), and its disjoined example is like: 'Zayd now: either a writer or not a writer' (2). (2) The second: 'That in which is mentioned what indicates the generalization of all situations'; its connected example: 'Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists' (3), and its disjoined example: 'Always: either the number is even or odd'. (3) The third: 'That in which is mentioned what indicates the generalization of some situations'; its connected example: 'It may be that if this is an animal, it is a human' (4), and its disjoined example: 'It may be that: either the thing is an animal or a horse'. (4) The fourth: 'That in which nothing of that is mentioned'; its connected example: 'If this is a human, it is an animal', and its disjoined example: 'Either the number is even or odd', so be aware. Al-Anbabiy said: His statement: (to particular and universal) The apparent meaning of this is that universal, partial, and unspecified do not apply to the particular, and this is one method, and there is another method followed by al-Sanusi that makes these three divisions apply to the particular as well. (1) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (If you come to me now) i.e., the coming now is a state from among the states of the addressee upon which honor was judged; i.e., honor was attached to it. (2) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (Zayd now) i.e., Zayd's being in this time is a state from among his states, unlike his being in his childhood, for then his being a writer is not possible. (3) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (Whenever the sun is rising) i.e., whether it is hot, moderate, weak, eclipsed or not; from the possible states, and it is not said whether it is eating or drinking because that is impossible. (4) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (It may be that if... etc.) i.e., it may be in some times the dependency of humanity on animality; such that there is something other than human; i.e., and it may not be such that the animal is other than human like 'the donkey'.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage defines conditional propositions (shartiyya) and divides them into connected (muttaasila) and disjoined (munfasila). Then each type is further divided into four subtypes based on the scope of the condition: particular (makhsusa), universal (kulliyya), partial (juz'iyya), and unspecified (muhmala). The passage provides definitions and examples for each subtype. For the particular, the condition is specific to one situation (e.g., 'If you come to me now'). For the universal, the condition applies to all situations (e.g., 'Whenever the sun is rising'). For the partial, the condition applies to some situations (e.g., 'It may be that if this is an animal, it is a human'). For the unspecified, no quantifier is mentioned (e.g., 'If this is a human, it is an animal'). The commentary by al-Anbabiy notes a disagreement: whether universal, partial, and unspecified apply to the particular. Al-Shirshimi's footnotes clarify the examples. From the Islamic sciences, the connected universal example 'Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists' is a common natural example; the disjoined universal 'Always: either the number is even or odd' is a mathematical example. These examples are used to illustrate the logical forms, not secular content per se, but they are acceptable as they are from the tradition.

Key Terms
شرطية
متَّصلة
منفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes a connected conditional proposition?
A) It links two independent statements.
B) It asserts that one statement's truth depends on another's truth.
C) It presents two mutually exclusive options.
D) It is always false.

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are either connected (explicit condition-result) or disjoined (mutual exclusion implying conditionality).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following propositions from fiqh as connected or disjoined conditional: (1) 'If the prayer is invalid due to impurity, it must be repeated.' (2) 'Either this contract is valid or it is void.' Provide the logical form in each case.

The Four Subtypes of Conditional Propositions
Division of each conditional type into four subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each subtype (particular, universal, partial, unspecified) in both connected and disjoined conditionals and explain their quantificational scope.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions vary by the scope of the condition, from a single situation to all, some, or unspecified.

After establishing the two main types of conditional propositions, the author divides each into four subtypes based on the quantifier of the condition: particular (specific situation), universal (all situations), partial (some situations), and unspecified (no quantifier). This lesson examines these subtypes with examples and notes a scholarly disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four subtypes
The passage lists: makhsusa (particular), kulliyya (universal), juz'iyya (partial), muhmala (unspecified). Note that they apply to both connected and disjoined.
The names are derived from the quantifier or lack thereof.
2
Understand each subtype's quantifier
Particular: condition is a specific state (e.g., 'now'). Universal: quantifier like 'whenever' or 'always'. Partial: 'it may be that' indicates some situations. Unspecified: no quantifier.
The quantifier affects the validity of inferences drawn from the conditional.
3
Examine the examples
For connected: particular: 'If you come now'; universal: 'Whenever the sun rises'; partial: 'It may be that if this is an animal, it is a human'; unspecified: 'If this is a human, it is an animal'. For disjoined: particular: 'Zayd now either writer or not'; universal: 'Always: either even or odd'; partial: 'It may be that either animal or horse'; unspecified: 'Either even or odd'.
Examples are from everyday and mathematical domains, but the same structure applies in Islamic sciences.
4
Note the scholarly disagreement
Al-Anbabiy mentions that some scholars (like al-Sanusi) include universal, partial, and unspecified within the particular category, while others treat them as separate. This affects how one classifies conditionals.
Be aware that terminology can vary across authors.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥٧) وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَتَنْقَسِمْ. قَالَ الباجوري: أَوْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ : مَا يَشْمَلُ التَّعْلِيقَ صَرِيحاً كَمَا فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ، أَوِ اسْتِلْزاماً كَمَا فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا تَسْتَلْزِمُ تَوَقُفَ تُبُوتِ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى انْتِفَاءِ الآخَرِ، أَوْ تَوَقُفَ انْتِفَاءِ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى ثُبُوتِ الْآخَرِ، فَكَأَنَّهُ قِيلَ : إِنِ انْتَفَى هَذَا ثَبَتَ هَذَا، أَوْ إِنْ ثَبَتَ هَذَا انْتَفَى هَذَا ، فَلْيُتَامَّلْ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَتَنْقَسِمْ... إلخ) قَسَّمَهَا المُصَنِّفُ إِلَى : مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ ; وَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى : مَخصُوصَةٍ، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ، وَجُزْئِيَّةٍ، وَمُهْمَلَةٍ. (۱) - فَالأُوْلَى: «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَضْعِ مُعَيَّنٍ مِنَ الأَوْضَاعِ المُمْكِنَةِ»؛ أَيْ: حَالٍ مُعَيَّنٍ مِنَ الأَحْوَالِ المُمْكِنَةِ ; مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ نَحْوُ : إِنْ جِئْتَنِي الآنَ، أَكْرَمْتُكَ» (١) ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ نَحْوُ : «زَيْدُ الآنَ: إِمَّا كَاتِبٌ، أَوْ غَيْرُ كَاتِبِ» (٢). (۲) - وَالثَّانِيَةُ : مَا ذُكِرَ فِيهَا مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمِ جَمِيعِ الأَوْضَاعِ»؛ مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ : كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ ، فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ (۳) ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ : دَائِماً : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً ، أَوْ فَرْداً». (۳) - وَالثَّالِثَةُ : مَا ذُكِرَ فِيهَا مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمِ بَعْضِ الأَوْضَاع» ; مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ هَذَا حَيَوَاناً ، كَانَ إِنْسَاناً » (٤) ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ : قَدْ يَكُونُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ حَيَوَاناً ، أَوْ فَرَساً». (٤) - وَالرَّابِعَةُ: مَا لَمْ يُذْكَرْ فِيهَا شَيْءٌ مِنْ ذَلِكَ» ; مِثَالُهَا مُتَّصِلَةٌ: «إِنْ كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً ، كَانَ حَيَوَاناً ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، أَوْ فَرْداً»، فَتَنَبَّه. قَالَ الأنبابي: قوله : (إِلَى مَخْصُوصَةٍ، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ) ظاهر هذا : أنَّ الكلية والجزئية والإهمال لا تجري في المخصوصة، وهو طريقة، وهناك طريقة أخرى مشى عليها السنوسي تجعل هذه الأقسام الثلاثة في المخصوصة أيضاً. (1) الشرشيمي: قوله : (إِنْ جِئْتَنِي الآن) أي : فالمجيء الآن حالٌ مِنَ الأحوال التي للمخاطب قد حكم عليه بالإكرام؛ أي: ربط به الإكرام. (2) الشرشيمي: قوله : (زَيْدُ (الآن) أي: كون زيد في هذا الزَّمان حال من أحواله، بخلاف كونه في صغره، فإنَّه لا يمكن كتابته. (3) الشرشيمي: قوله : كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ) أي: سواء كانت حارَّةٌ، أو متوسطة، أو ضعيفة، منكسفة أو لا ؛ مِنَ الأحوال الممكنة، ولا يقال سواء كانت أكلة أو شاربة؛ لأنَّه لا يمكن. (4) الشرشيمي: قوله : (قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ ... إلخ) أي : قد يكون في بعض الأوقات ترتب الإنسانيَّة على الحيوانية؛ بأن يكون هناك غير الإنسان؛ أي: وقد لا يكون بأن يكون الحيوان غير الإنسان كـ: «الحمار».
(57) And if on the condition in it a judgment was made, then it is a conditional, and it divides. Al-Bajuri said: Or that the intended [meaning] is what includes the condition explicitly as in the connected, or implicitly as in the disjoined; because it entails the dependence of the affirmation of one of them on the negation of the other, or the dependence of the negation of one of them on the affirmation of the other, so it is as if it was said: If this is negated, this is affirmed, or if this is affirmed, this is negated, so let it be pondered. His statement: (and it divides... etc.) The author divided it into: connected and disjoined; and each of them divides into: particular, universal, partial, and unspecified. (1) The first: 'That in which a judgment is made on a specific situation from among possible situations'; i.e., a specific state from among possible states; its connected example is like: 'If you come to me now, I will honor you' (1), and its disjoined example is like: 'Zayd now: either a writer or not a writer' (2). (2) The second: 'That in which is mentioned what indicates the generalization of all situations'; its connected example: 'Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists' (3), and its disjoined example: 'Always: either the number is even or odd'. (3) The third: 'That in which is mentioned what indicates the generalization of some situations'; its connected example: 'It may be that if this is an animal, it is a human' (4), and its disjoined example: 'It may be that: either the thing is an animal or a horse'. (4) The fourth: 'That in which nothing of that is mentioned'; its connected example: 'If this is a human, it is an animal', and its disjoined example: 'Either the number is even or odd', so be aware. Al-Anbabiy said: His statement: (to particular and universal) The apparent meaning of this is that universal, partial, and unspecified do not apply to the particular, and this is one method, and there is another method followed by al-Sanusi that makes these three divisions apply to the particular as well. (1) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (If you come to me now) i.e., the coming now is a state from among the states of the addressee upon which honor was judged; i.e., honor was attached to it. (2) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (Zayd now) i.e., Zayd's being in this time is a state from among his states, unlike his being in his childhood, for then his being a writer is not possible. (3) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (Whenever the sun is rising) i.e., whether it is hot, moderate, weak, eclipsed or not; from the possible states, and it is not said whether it is eating or drinking because that is impossible. (4) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (It may be that if... etc.) i.e., it may be in some times the dependency of humanity on animality; such that there is something other than human; i.e., and it may not be such that the animal is other than human like 'the donkey'.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage defines conditional propositions (shartiyya) and divides them into connected (muttaasila) and disjoined (munfasila). Then each type is further divided into four subtypes based on the scope of the condition: particular (makhsusa), universal (kulliyya), partial (juz'iyya), and unspecified (muhmala). The passage provides definitions and examples for each subtype. For the particular, the condition is specific to one situation (e.g., 'If you come to me now'). For the universal, the condition applies to all situations (e.g., 'Whenever the sun is rising'). For the partial, the condition applies to some situations (e.g., 'It may be that if this is an animal, it is a human'). For the unspecified, no quantifier is mentioned (e.g., 'If this is a human, it is an animal'). The commentary by al-Anbabiy notes a disagreement: whether universal, partial, and unspecified apply to the particular. Al-Shirshimi's footnotes clarify the examples. From the Islamic sciences, the connected universal example 'Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists' is a common natural example; the disjoined universal 'Always: either the number is even or odd' is a mathematical example. These examples are used to illustrate the logical forms, not secular content per se, but they are acceptable as they are from the tradition.

(Assume emphasis on 'now'.)

Propositions
beginner
5 min
Parts of a Conditional: Muqaddam and Tali
Author states that both connected and disjoined conditionals have two parts called muqaddam and tali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in both connected and disjoined conditional propositions and distinguish between the two types.

Core Idea
Every conditional has two parts: antecedent and consequent, regardless of the relationship.

In this passage, al-Bajuri explains that conditional propositions, whether connected (muttaṣila) or disjoined (munfaṣila), are composed of two parts: an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (tali). Understanding these parts is essential for analyzing conditional arguments in Islamic logic, which are widely used in legal and theological reasoning. The passage also clarifies the nature of the connection—concomitance for connected conditionals and opposition for disjoined ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Read the proposition and determine whether the relationship between the two parts is one of concomitance (if one, then the other) or opposition (either one or the other, but not both or one must be). This tells you if it is connected (muttaṣila) or disjoined (munfaṣila).
Connected: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists.' Disjoined: 'The number is either even or odd.'
2
Locate the two parts
Find the first part (muqaddam) and the second part (tali) in the proposition. In a connected conditional, the muqaddam is the condition and the tali is the result. In a disjoined conditional, both are alternatives.
In 'If the crescent is sighted, then Ramadan begins', the muqaddam is 'the crescent is sighted' and the tali is 'Ramadan begins'.
3
Label the logical relationship
For connected: note that truth of muqaddam implies truth of tali (concomitance). For disjoined: note that truth of one implies falsehood of the other (opposition), and falsehood of one implies truth of the other (in exhaustive disjunction).
The text specifies that in disjoined, 'whenever one is realized, the other is negated, or whenever one is negated, the other is realized.'
4
Verify the author's affirmation
Check the passage's claim that both types have a muqaddam and tali. The commentary confirms this, noting that some scholars like al-Sanusi initially differed but later agreed.
Al-Bajuri states: 'the explicit statement of the author is that the two parts of the disjoined are called antecedent and consequent.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها (٥٨) أَيْضاً : إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَهُ، وَمِثْلِهَا شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَهُ (٥٩) جُزْآهُمَا : مُقَدَّمٌ، وَتَالٍ. قَالَ الباجوري: قَوْلُهُ : (أَيْضاً) هُوَ فِي الأَصْلِ مَصْدَرُ آضَ ، يَبْيضُ : إِذَا رَجَعَ ، وَالمَعْنَى : رُجُوعاً إِلَى الانْقِسَامِ السَّابِقِ فِي الحَمْلِيَّةِ (١). قَوْلُهُ : (إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَهُ) أَيْ نَحْوَ : إِنْ كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ، فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ . وَسُمِّيَتْ : شَرْطِيَّةٌ ; لأَنَّهُ حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الشَّرْطِ وَالتَّعْلِيقِ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ، وَ مُتَّصِلَةٌ»؛ لاتِّصَالِ طَرَفِيهَا ، فَإِنَّهُ كُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ أَحَدُهُمَا تَحَقَّقَ الآخَرُ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَمِثْلِهَا أَيْ: وَإِلَى مِثْلِهَا ، فَهُوَ بِالجَرِّ عَطْفاً عَلَى مَدْخُولِ إِلَى، وَالمُرَادُ : أَنَّهَا مِثْلُهَا أَصْلِ الرَّبْطِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ الرَّبْطُ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ عَلَى وَجْهِ التَّلَازُم ، وَفِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ عَلَى وَجْهِ التَّعَانُدِ . هَذَا، وَلَا حَاجَةَ لِزِيَادَةِ قَوْلِهِ : مِثْلِهَا مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى؛ لِأَنَّ المُمَاثَلَةَ فِيمَا ذَكَرَ مُتَحَقِّقَةٌ مِنْ جَعْ المُنْفَصِلَةِ قِسْماً مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ. قَوْلُهُ : (شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَهُ أَيْ نَحْوَ : العَدَدُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ ، أَوْ فَرْدُه . وَسُمِّيَتْ : شَرْطِيَّةٌ ; لِأَنَّهُ حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الشَّرْطِ وَالتَّعْلِيقِ عَلَى مَا مَرَّ، وَ: «مُنْفَصِلَةٌ»؛ لانْفِصَالِ طَرَفِيهَا ; لِأَنَّهُ كُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ أَحَدُهُمَا انْتَفَى الآخَرُ ، أَوْ كُلَّمَا انْتَفَى أَحَدُهُمَا تَحَقَّقَ الآخَرُ (٢). قَوْلُهُ : (جُزَاهُمَا ... إلخ) الصَّمِيرُ عَائِدٌ لِلشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُنْفَصِلَةِ، فَصَرِيحُ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ أَنَّ جُزْأَي المُنْفَصِلَةِ يُقَالُ لَهُمَا: «مُقَدَّمٌ ، وَتَالٍ ، وَهُوَ مَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ السَّيِّدُ الشَّرِيفُ فِي شَرْحِ الخَوْنَجِي»، وَبَعْضُ شُرَّاحِ إِيْسَاغُوجِي، وَالقُطْبُ، لَكِنْ ظَاهِرُ كَلَامِ السَّنُوسِيِّ فِي شَرْحِ مُخْتَصَرِهِ» خِلَافَهُ ، بَلْ صَرَّحَ بِذَلِكَ فِي شَرْحِ إِيْسَاغُوجِي». قَالَ الأنبابي: قوله : (وَالمَعْنَى : رُجُوعاً إِلَى الانْقِسَامِ السَّابِقِ فِي الحَمْلِيَّةِ كذا في بعض النسخ، والصَّوابُ حذف له : «السَّابق في الحمليّة، كما في بعض آخر ; لأنَّ مقصوده الرُّجوع إلى مطلق الانقسام، كما هو ظاهر. قوله : الاتِّصَالِ طَرَفِيهَا ) أي : اقترانهما صدقاً ; أي: تحققاً; لأنَّ الصدق في القضايا بمعنى : التَّحقق، وفي المفردات بمعنى: الحمل.
Chapter on propositions and their rulings (58) Also: to a connected conditional, and like it a disjoined conditional (59) The two parts of them are: antecedent and consequent. Al-Bajuri said: His statement: (Also) it is originally the verbal noun of āḍa, yaḍuḍu: when it returns, and the meaning is: a return to the previous division in the categorical (1). His statement: (to a connected conditional) i.e., like: If the sun is rising, then daytime exists. And it is called conditional because a judgment is made in it in the manner of condition and suspension as previously, and 'connected' due to the connection of its two parts, for whenever one of them is realized, the other is realized. His statement: (and like it) i.e., and to its like, so it is in the genitive as a conjunction to the object of 'to', and the intended meaning is that it is like it in the basic connection, although the connection in the connected is in the manner of concomitance, and in the disjoined in the manner of opposition. This, and there is no need for the addition of his statement: 'like it' in terms of meaning, because the similarity in what was mentioned is realized from making the disjoined a part of the conditional. His statement: (disjoined conditional) i.e., like: The number: either even or odd. And it is called conditional because a judgment is made in it in the manner of condition and suspension as passed, and 'disjoined' due to the separation of its two parts; because whenever one of them is realized, the other is negated, or whenever one of them is negated, the other is realized (2). His statement: (The two parts of them... etc.) The pronoun refers to the connected conditional and the disjoined conditional, so the explicit statement of the author is that the two parts of the disjoined are called 'antecedent' and 'consequent', and this is what al-Sayyid al-Sharif stated in his commentary on al-Khunaji, and some commentators of Isaghuji, and al-Qutb, but the apparent statement of al-Sanusi in his commentary on his Mukhtasar is contrary to it, rather he explicitly stated that in his commentary on Isaghuji. Al-Anbabiy said: His statement: (and the meaning: a return to the previous division in the categorical) thus in some copies, and the correct [reading] is to delete the words 'the previous in the categorical', as in some others; because his intention is the return to the absolute division, as is apparent. His statement: (the connection of its two parts) i.e., their conjunction in truth; i.e., realization; because truth (sidq) in propositions means realization, and in singular terms it means predication.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage explains that both connected and disjoined conditionals consist of two parts: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tali). The author clarifies the term 'aydan' (also) indicating a return to the previous division in categorical propositions. The connected conditional is exemplified by 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists', and the disjoined by 'The number: either even or odd'. The commentary highlights that the connection in the connected is of concomitance (talazum), while in the disjoined it is of opposition (ta'anud). A note mentions disagreement: al-Sanusi's view on the terminology for the parts of a disjoined conditional differs from others, but he later confirmed the standard usage. Al-Anbabiy's marginal note corrects a textual variant regarding the phrase 'previous division in the categorical'. Using Islamic logic examples: a connected conditional could be 'If the crescent is sighted, then Ramadan begins'; a disjoined conditional could be 'Either this act is obligatory or prohibited'.

According to the text, what are the two parts of a disjoined conditional called?
A) Subject and predicate

D) Condition and result

Key Takeaway
Both connected and disjoined conditionals consist of an antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali), but the relationship differs: concomitance vs opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example of a connected conditional proposition in a fiqh text (e.g., 'If the sale is valid, then the ownership transfers'). Identify its muqaddam and tali.
?
Construct a disjoined conditional proposition related to a ruling in usul al-fiqh (e.g., 'Either the act is obligatory or it is prohibited'). Label the parts and explain the relationship.

Conditional Propositions: Muttasila and Munfasila
Defines conditional propositions, explaining the two types: muttasila and munfasila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a connected conditional (muttasila) from a disconnected conditional (munfasila) and identify the relationship between their parts.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition is a judgment made on condition; its parts are linked either by correlation or by opposition.

After covering categorical propositions, Islamic logicians turn to conditionals. A conditional proposition (shartiyya) is a judgment that is suspended on a condition. The matn divides it into two types: connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila). The commentary clarifies the difference: in muttasila, the truth of one part ensures the truth of the other; in munfasila, the truth of one part ensures the falsity of the other. Understanding this distinction is essential for constructing valid conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the overarching category
Identify that a conditional proposition (shartiyya) is a judgment made conditionally, suspended on a condition. This is the common genus for both muttasila and munfasila.
The word 'also' in the matn signals a return to the previous division of categoricals.
2
Analyze the connected conditional (muttasila)
In muttasila, the two parts are linked by correlation (talazum). Whenever one part is realized, the other is realized. Example: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists.'
The parts are called muqaddam (antecedent) and tālī (consequent).
3
Analyze the disconnected conditional (munfasila)
In munfasila, the parts are linked by opposition (ta'anud). Whenever one part is realized, the other is negated, and vice versa. Example: 'The number is either even or odd.'
The commentary notes that the link is opposite to correlation.
4
Compare the two types using the examples
Contrast the examples: muttasila uses 'if...then...' (correlation), while munfasila uses 'either...or...' (mutual exclusivity). The relationship in muttasila is one-way or two-way? Actually it's one-way: from antecedent to consequent. In munfasila it's two-way: each part implies the negation of the other.
The commentary emphasizes that both are subdivisions of shartiyya, so the phrase 'similar to it' is redundant in meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها (٥٨) أَيْضاً : إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَهُ، وَمِثْلِهَا شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَهُ (٥٩) . قَوْلُهُ : (أَيْضاً) هُوَ فِي الأَصْلِ مَصْدَرُ آضَ ، يَئيضُ : إِذَا رَجَعَ ، وَالمَعْنَى : رُجُوعاً إِلَى الانْقِسَامِ السَّابِقِ فِي الحَمْلِيَّةِ (١) . قَوْلُهُ : (إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَهُ) أَيْ نَحْوَ : إِنْ كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ، فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ . وَسُمِّيَتْ : شَرْطِيَّةٌ ؛ لأَنَّهُ حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الشَّرْطِ وَالتَّعْلِيقِ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ، وَمُتَّصِلَةٌ لاتِّصَالِ طَرَفِيهَا ، فَإِنَّهُ كُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ أَحَدُهُمَا تَحَقَّقَ الآخَرُ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَمِثْلِهَا) أَيْ: وَإِلَى مِثْلِهَا ، فَهُوَ بِالجَرِّ عَطْفاً عَلَى مَدْخُولِ إِلَى، وَالمُرَادُ : أَنَّهَا مِثْلُهَا أَصْلِ الرَّبْطِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ الرَّبْطُ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ عَلَى وَجْهِ التَّلَازُم ، وَفِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ عَلَى وَجْهِ التَّعَانُدِ . هَذَا، وَلَا حَاجَةَ لِزِيَادَةِ قَوْلِهِ : مِثْلِهَا مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى؛ لِأَنَّ المُمَاثَلَةَ فِيمَا ذَكَرَ مُتَحَقِّقَةٌ مِنْ جَعْلِ المُنْفَصِلَةِ قِسْماً مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ. قَوْلُهُ : (شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَهُ) أَيْ نَحْوَ : العَدَدُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ ، أَوْ فَرْدُه . وَسُمِّيَتْ : شَرْطِيَّةٌ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الشَّرْطِ وَالتَّعْلِيقِ عَلَى مَا مَرَّ، وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ لانْفِصَالِ طَرَفِيهَا ، لِأَنَّهُ كُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ أَحَدُهُمَا انْتَفَى الآخَرُ ، أَوْ كُلَّمَا انْتَفَى أَحَدُهُمَا تَحَقَّقَ الآخَرُ
Chapter on propositions and their judgments (58) Also: to a connected conditional, and similar to it a disconnected conditional (59). His statement: '(also)' is originally the verbal noun of āḍa, meaning 'to return', and the meaning is: a return to the previous division in the categorical. His statement: '(to a connected conditional)' i.e., like: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists.' It is called 'conditional' because judgment is made in it on the basis of a condition and suspension, as previously, and 'connected' because of the connection of its two parts; for whenever one of them is realized, the other is realized. His statement: '(and similar to it)' i.e., and to its like, so it is in the genitive as a conjunction to the object of 'to', and the intended meaning is that it is like it in the essence of the link, although the link in the connected is on the basis of correlation, and in the disconnected on the basis of opposition. This, and there is no need for the addition of his statement 'similar to it' from the point of view of meaning; because the similarity in what he mentioned is actualized by making the disconnected a division of the conditional. His statement: '(a disconnected conditional)' i.e., like: 'The number: either even or odd.' It is called 'conditional' because judgment is made in it conditionally and suspended, as previously, and 'disconnected' because of the disconnection of its two parts; because whenever one of them is realized, the other is negated, or whenever one of them is negated, the other is realized.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya). It begins with the matn stating that after categorical propositions, the author turns to conditional ones, dividing them into two types: muttasila (connected) and munfasila (disconnected). The commentary clarifies the word 'also' as indicating a return to the previous division among categoricals. For muttasila, an example is given: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists.' It is called shartiyya because it involves a condition and suspension, and muttasila because the two parts are connected such that whenever one is true, the other is true. For munfasila, the commentary notes that it is similar in being conditional but differs in the type of link: it involves opposition (ta'ānud) rather than correlation. An example is: 'The number is either even or odd.' It is called munfasila because the parts are disconnected, meaning that whenever one is true, the other is false, and vice versa. The passage emphasizes that both types are subdivisions of conditional propositions, and the addition of 'similar to it' is redundant in meaning since the similarity is already established by including munfasila as a type of shartiyya.

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are either connected by correlation (muttasila) or disconnected by opposition (munfasila), and both are valid forms of shartiyya.

The Parts of Conditionals and the Muqaddam-Tālī Debate
Debate on applying muqaddam/tālī to munfasila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two parts of a conditional, explain why some scholars deny muqaddam and tālī for munfasila, and articulate the resolution using hasr iḍāfī.

Core Idea
The meaning-order in conditionals determines whether we call a part muqaddam or tālī.

In logic, every conditional proposition has two parts, but do we always call them muqaddam (antecedent) and tālī (consequent)? This lesson explores a debate about whether these terms apply to disconnected conditionals (munfasila) and how a refined understanding of 'restriction' resolves it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of any conditional
Recognize that both muttasila (connected) and munfasila (disconnected) consist of two component propositions.
The source explicitly states 'their two parts' referring to both types.
2
Understand the standard definition of muqaddam and tālī
In muttasila, muqaddam is the part entered by the conditional particle (e.g., 'in'), and tālī is the part suspended on it. This is the well-known terminological convention.
Ibn Yaʿqūb's quote clarifies this definition.
3
Find the objection regarding munfasila
Ibn Yaʿqūb and some others argue that in munfasila, the order of parts does not change the meaning, so applying muqaddam and tālī is inappropriate.
The meaning does not differ by advancing or delaying the parts.
4
Examine a counterexample
Consider a disjunction where one part affirms something and the other negates it; affirmation seems conceptually prior to negation, suggesting a meaning-order.
Example: 'This apparition is either human or non-human.' Affirmation of humanity is prior to its negation.
5
Apply the resolution: hasr iḍāfī
The restriction is relative (iḍāfī) to opposition; the order is only in mention, not a necessary meaning-order, preserving the original claim that munfasila lacks muqaddam and tālī in the technical sense.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (جُزْآهُمَا ... إلخ) الصَّمِيرُ عَائِدٌ لِلشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُنْفَصِلَةِ، فَصَرِيحُ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ أَنَّ جُزْأَي المُنْفَصِلَةِ يُقَالُ لَهُمَا: «مُقَدَّمٌ ، وَتَالٍ» ، وَهُوَ مَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ السَّيِّدُ الشَّرِيفُ فِي شَرْحِ الخَوْنَجِي، وَبَعْضُ شُرَّاحِ إِيْسَاغُوجِي، وَالقُطْبُ، لَكِنْ ظَاهِرُ كَلَامِ السَّنُوسِيِّ فِي شَرْحِ مُخْتَصَرِهِ خِلَافَهُ ، بَلْ صَرَّحَ بِذَلِكَ فِي شَرْحِ إِيْسَاغُوجِي. وَقَدْ صَرَّحَ بِهِ أَيْضاً ابْنُ يَعْقُوبَ حَيْثُ قَالَ: المَشْهُورُ فِي الاِصْطِلَاحِ أَنَّ المُقَدَّمَ هُوَ مَدْخُولُ أَدَاةِ الشَّرْطِ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ، وَالتَّالِي مَا عُلِّقَ عَلَى مَدْخُولِهَا ، وَأَمَّا المُنْفَصِلَةُ فَلَا مُقَدَّمَ لَهَا وَلَا تَالِي؛ لأَنَّ المَعْنَى لَا يَخْتَلِفُ فِيهَا بِالتَّقْدِيمِ وَالتَّأخِيرِ . اهـ . قوله : (لِأَنَّ المَعْنَى لَا يَخْتَلِفُ ... إلخ) أي: فلا ترتيب بين جزأيها في المعنى، بل في الذكر فقط وقد يُقالُ : قد يكون بينهما ترتيب معنوي، كما إذا كان الحكم في أحدهما إثباتاً لشيء، وفي الآخر نفياً له، فإنَّ رتبة إثباته مقدَّمة على رتبة نفيه ؛ إذ لا يعقل سلب شيء إلا بعد تعقُله كما تقدَّم مراراً ؛ نحو: «هَذَا الشَّبَحُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ إِنْسَاناً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ غَيْرَ إِنْسَانِ». ويمكن أن يجاب بأنَّ الحصر إضافي؛ أي: بالنسبة للعِناد؛ أي: أنَّ التَّرتيب ليس إلا في الذكر، لا في العناد أو المنفي الترتيب المعنوي اللازم في كلِّ منفصلة، فافهم، أفاده الصَّبَّان
His statement: 'their two parts... etc.' The pronoun refers to the connected conditional and the disconnected conditional. So the explicit meaning of the author's words is that the two parts of the disconnected are called 'muqaddam' and 'tālī'. This is what al-Sayyid al-Sharīf stated in his commentary on al-Khūnajī, and some commentators on Īsāghūjī, and al-Quṭb. However, the apparent meaning of al-Sanūsī's words in his commentary on his Epitome is contrary to that; rather, he stated that in his commentary on Īsāghūjī. And Ibn Yaʿqūb also stated it where he said: 'The well-known in the technical terminology is that the muqaddam is what is entered by the conditional particle in the connected [conditional], and the tālī is what is suspended on what is entered by it. As for the disconnected [conditional], it has no muqaddam and no tālī, because the meaning does not differ in it by advancing and delaying.' End. His statement: 'because the meaning does not differ... etc.' i.e., there is no order between its two parts in meaning, but only in mention. And it may be said: occasionally there is between them a meaning-order, as when the judgment in one of them is an affirmation of something, and in the other a negation of it; for the rank of affirming it is prior to the rank of negating it, since the negation of something is not conceivable except after its conception, as has been mentioned repeatedly; for example: 'This apparition: either it is a human or it is a non-human.' And it can be answered that the restriction is relative (iḍāfī), i.e., with respect to opposition; i.e., the order is only in mention, not in opposition, or the meaning-order necessary in every disconnected conditional is denied. So understand. This is what al-Ṣabbān conveyed.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage addresses the parts of conditional propositions. The matn states that both muttasila and munfasila have two parts: muqaddam and tālī. The commentary first confirms that the pronoun 'their' refers to both types, so the author explicitly claims that the two parts of a munfasila are also called muqaddam and tālī. It then notes that this view is supported by al-Sayyid al-Sharīf and some commentators, but al-Sanūsī's opinion in his commentary appears opposite, though later he clarifies. Ibn Yaʿqūb is cited as stating the well-known terminological convention: muqaddam is the part entered by the conditional particle in muttasila, and tālī is the part suspended; for munfasila, there is no muqaddam or tālī because the meaning does not change with order. Al-Anbābī then discusses this: the lack of meaning-order means only mention-order, but a counterexample is given where a meaning-order seems to exist (affirmation before negation). The response is that the restriction is 'iḍāfī' (relative to opposition), meaning the order is only verbal, not in opposition or necessary meaning-order. The passage thus presents a debate about whether muqaddam/tālī apply to munfasila and offers a resolution through the concept of relative exclusivity.

Key Terms
مُقَدَّم
تَالِي
جُزْء
حَصْر إِضَافِيّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it has no parts.
B) Because the meaning does not change by advancing or delaying its parts.
C) Because it is not a conditional.
D) Because it has only one part.

Key Takeaway
The terms muqaddam and tālī technically apply only when the order of parts affects meaning, which is not the case for disconnected conditionals unless a relative restriction is introduced.

Al-'Attar's Critique of Meaning-Order in Munfasila
Critique of the claim that parts of a munfasila can have a meaning-order, arguing they are not independent propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the parts of a disjunctive conditional cannot be complete propositions according to al-'Attar, and identify the logical reasoning about judgment linkage.

Core Idea
A complete proposition with a fixed judgment cannot be linked; conditional parts are stripped of judgment.

In this passage, al-'Attar challenges a claim that the two sides of a disjunctive conditional (munfasila) can have a meaning-order, implying each side is a complete proposition. He argues this is incoherent because the disjunctive particle merges them into one unit. Understanding this critique sharpens your grasp of what constitutes a complete proposition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being critiqued
Find the claim that there can be a meaning-order between the parts of a munfasila. This claim assumes each part is an independent proposition.
Look for the phrase 'قد يكون بينهما ترتيب معنوي'.
2
Understand al-'Attar's first objection
Al-'Attar says the disjunctive particle makes the two parts into one proposition. So each part cannot have its own judgment of affirmation or negation; the only judgment is the opposition.
Recognize the key idea: linking precludes independent judgment.
3
Analyze the supporting quote from al-Sayyid
Al-Sayyid explains that a complete proposition (qadiyya) requires a fixed judgment, either by positing (īqāʿ) or derivation (intizāʿ). Once a judgment is fixed, it cannot be linked to anything else. Therefore, to become part of a conditional, a proposition must be stripped of its judgment.
This is the core logical argument: a linked part cannot retain its own judgment.
4
Evaluate the consequence
If each part were a separate proposition, the conditional would contain three judgments (two from the parts plus the opposition), which is absurd. Hence, the parts are not propositions.
This shows the necessity of the critique for consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وناقشه العطّار : بأن قوله : «قد يكون بينهما ترتيب معنوي ... إلخ» لا يصح، بل لا يعقل؛ إذ مِنَ المعلوم أنَّ أداة الانفصال ربطت القضيَّتين وصيَّرتهما واحدة، كما اعترف به هو في غير هذا المحل، وحينئذ فكيف يعقل أنَّ في كلِّ مِنَ القضيَّتين حكماً بإثبات أو نفي؟ وإِنَّما الحكم بالتّنافي بين الطرفين، ولأنَّا لو نظرنا لِمَا اشتمل عليه الطَّرفان قبل الرَّبط ، ولاحظناه ،بعد ، لزم أنَّ كلَّ قضيَّةٍ شرطيَّةٍ مشتملةٌ على أحكام ثلاثة، ولم يقل بذلك أحد. قال السيد في حواشي القطب : إنَّ أطراف الشَّرطيَّة ليست قضايا ؛ لأنَّ القضيَّة لا تتم إلَّا إذا اعتبر فيها الحكم إيقاعاً أو انتزاعاً، وما اعتبر فيه ذلك لا يرتبط بغيره ضرورةً، فإنَّك إذا قلت : «الشَّمس طالعة» وأوقعت النِّسْبَة بين طرفيه، لم يتصور ربطه بشيءٍ آخر ؛ بأن يصيّر محكوماً عليه أو به، فما لم تتجرَّد القضيَّة عن الحكم، لم يمكن جعلها جزء قضيَّةٍ أخرى [اهـ]، وإذا تأمَّلت هذا حقَّ التَّأمُّل ظهر لك مصداق ما قلنا، وفي المحشي من أمثال هذا أشياء كثيرة، ولولا خوف ملل النَّاظر وتشويش الخاطر، لاستقصيناه. [اهـ كلام العطار] ، ولا يخفى على النّبيه رده .
And al-‘Aṭṭār criticized him: that his statement: 'occasionally there is between them a meaning-order... etc.' is not correct, rather it is inconceivable; because it is known that the disjunctive particle linked the two propositions and made them one, as he himself acknowledged in another place. Then how can it be conceived that each of the two propositions has a judgment by affirmation or negation? Rather, the judgment is about the opposition between the two sides. And because if we considered what the two sides contained before the linking, and then observed it after, it would necessitate that every conditional proposition contains three judgments, and no one has said that. Al-Sayyid said in the glosses on al-Quṭb: 'The sides of a conditional are not propositions, because a proposition is not complete unless judgment is considered in it, either by positing (īqāʿ) or derivation (intizāʿ). And that which is considered as such cannot be linked to another necessarily; for if you say: “The sun is rising” and you posit the relation between its two sides, it is not conceivable to link it with anything else, by making it something judged upon or by which a judgment is made. So unless the proposition is stripped of judgment, it cannot be made a part of another proposition.' [End]. And if you contemplate this properly, the truth of what I say will appear to you. And in the marginal annotations there are many such things, and were it not for fear of the reader's boredom and disturbance of mind, we would have exhausted it. [End of al-‘Aṭṭār's words.] And the discerning person will not fail to see his refutation.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents al-‘Aṭṭār's critique of the idea that a meaning-order exists within a disconnected conditional. He argues that the disjunctive connective combines the two propositions into a single unit, so it is incoherent to claim that each part individually carries a judgment of affirmation or negation. The only judgment is the opposition between the two sides. If we were to treat the parts as independent propositions, then a conditional would contain three judgments (the two separate judgments plus the opposition), which no one accepts. He supports this by quoting al-Sayyid al-Sharīf, who states that the sides of a conditional cannot be full propositions because a complete proposition, once its judgment is fixed, cannot be linked to another. To become part of a conditional, a proposition must be stripped of its judgment. The passage concludes with a note that the discerning person can see the refutation of the opposing view. This is a critical refinement (hāshiya) aiming to correct the previous claim about meaning-order.

Key Terms
قَضِيَّة
حُكْم
إِيقَاع
اِنْتِزَاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-‘Aṭṭār, why can the parts of a conditional not be complete propositions?
A) Because they are not meaningful sentences.
B) Because a complete proposition with a judgment cannot be linked to another.
C) Because conditionals are not real propositions.
D) Because the disjunctive particle cancels the judgments.

Key Takeaway
A conditional's parts are not complete propositions because they lack independent judgment; they are merely sides (aṭrāf) unified by the connective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the disjunctive conditional: 'The world is either created or eternal.' According to al-'Attār, are 'The world is created' and 'The world is eternal' complete propositions? Explain your reasoning using the concept of judgment (ḥukm).
?
Analyze the conditional: 'If Zayd comes, then we will go to the mosque.' Is the antecedent 'Zayd comes' a full proposition? Why or why not? (Hint: Think about whether it has its own independent judgment.)

Defining the Conjunctive Conditional: Talazum and Its Scope
Author defines muttasila and distinguishes luzumiyya from ittifaqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya conjunctive conditionals and identify the correct scope of the definition of muttasila.

Core Idea
A conjunctive conditional requires a real, not coincidental, entailment from antecedent to consequent.

This passage defines the conjunctive conditional (muttasila) as a statement that expresses entailment (talazum) between its two parts. However, the commentator clarifies that talazum here means one-way necessity, not mutual implication, and that the definition properly applies only to conditionals where the entailment is based on a real relationship like causality, not mere coincidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the definiendum: 'muttasila' (conjunctive conditional). Note that the matn defines it as 'that which necessitates talazum of the two parts'.
2
Analyze the meaning of talazum
The commentary explains that talazum here does not mean mutual entailment but one-way necessity (luzum) from antecedent to consequent. The proposition only asserts the consequent follows the antecedent, not vice versa.
Even if in some subject matters the converse is also true, the definition does not require reciprocity.
3
Distinguish luzumiyya from ittifaqiyya
Luzumiyya conditionals have a real relationship (e.g., causality) between parts, like 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists'. Ittifaqiyya conditionals lack such relationship, e.g., 'If a donkey brays, then a human speaks' – a mere coincidence. The definition applies only to luzumiyya.
The commentator al-Shirshimi stresses that the relationship must be explicitly considered.
4
Test with examples
Apply the definition: 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists' is a valid luzumiyya because of causal link. 'If the day exists, then the world is illuminated' is problematic because the relationship is not one of cause but both depend on sunrise.
The logicians focus on the manifest relationship, not hidden causes like divine will.

Chapter on propositions and their judgments. (59) Its two parts: antecedent and consequent. As for the explanation of the nature of the conditional: (60) 'That which necessitates the talazum of the two parts, and the nature of the disjunctive without bias.' Al-Bajuri: And an example like your saying: 'The daytime exists if the sun is rising' is not an objection, because the first mentioned in that is not a consequent, but rather its evidence; because the established position of the investigators in Arabic grammar is that the answer to a condition is always delayed, and the first mentioned is its evidence. Thus it is taken from al-Qutb, and Ibn Marzuq explicitly stated it in his commentary on al-Jumal. However, al-Sa'd mentioned that the first mentioned in that is the very consequent, and even if it is advanced in mention, it is a consequent in rank. He said: 'The claim of omitting the apodosis in such cases is merely the terminology of the grammarians.' End. And it is the definite view that must be adopted if one knows it from the terminology of the logicians, and some justified it by saying that the logicians only care about meanings, so there is no need to posit something without which the meaning is complete. So reflect. His statement: (As for the explanation of the nature of the conditional) i.e., the possessor of connection, which is the conjunctive conditional. His statement: (That which necessitates the talazum of the two parts) i.e., it requires and entails that. And talazum here is not from both sides; because the proposition only indicates the necessity of the consequent from the antecedent and not the converse, even if it occurs in some matters. So it has the meaning of entailment (luzum), and its attribution to the two parts is due to its relation with them because it is a relation between them. And an objection was raised against the author that this is apparent only in the luzumiyya muttasila, which is: 'that in which it is judged that a proposition is true on the condition of the truth of another due to a relationship between them that necessitates that, like causality; e.g., your saying: Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists' — not the ittifaqiyya. Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (due to a relationship between them) i.e., by taking a relationship into account, so it is not an objection that there is a relationship between the hee-haw of a donkey and the speech of a human, namely the will of God Almighty for them, because that relationship is not considered. So it is not like your saying: 'Whenever daytime exists, then the world is illuminated' — in terms of the ordering of everything upon another. For this example has the ordering of the two parts upon a second thing, namely sunrise, just as the hee-haw and the donkeyness are ordered upon a third thing, namely the will. Because you know that their ordering upon the will is not considered, unlike the mentioned example, where the relationship is considered, and that is due to the obviousness of sunrise and its cause to them, while here the will and its causality to them [is not obvious].
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage provides the formal definition of the conjunctive conditional (muttasila) and the disjunctive conditional (munfasila) as found in the matn. The matn states: 'That which necessitates the talazum of the two parts, and the nature of the disjunctive without bias.' The commentary, primarily by al-Bajuri, explains that 'talazum' here means entailment (luzum) from the antecedent to the consequent, not necessarily reciprocally. It is not a two-way implication; the proposition only asserts that if the antecedent holds, the consequent holds, not vice versa, even if in some subject matters the converse is also true. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: the definition seems to cover only the 'luzumiyya' type of muttasila (where there is a real causal or other necessary relationship between the parts), and not the 'ittifaqiyya' type (where the connection is merely coincidental). For example, 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists' is luzumiyya because there is a causal relation. In contrast, a statement like 'If daytime exists, then the world is illuminated' might be considered ittifaqiyya if the relationship is not one of cause. Al-Shirshimi elaborates: the relationship must be explicitly considered; otherwise, accidental connections like 'If a donkey brays, then a human speaks' (both effects of divine will) are not considered genuine conditional relationships because the underlying cause is not apparent. The passage thus refines the definition of muttasila to specifically refer to the luzumiyya type, where the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent due to a manifest relationship. This guards against overly broad interpretations of conditional propositions.

Key Terms
تَلَازُم
مُتَّصِلَة لُزُومِيَّة
مُتَّصِلَة اتِّفَاقِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, the definition 'mā awjabat talāzuma al-juz'ain' applies primarily to which type of conjunctive conditional?
A) The ittifaqiyya (coincidental) type.
B) The luzumiyya (causal) type.
C) Both types equally.
D) Only the disjunctive conditional.

Key Takeaway
The conjunctive conditional is defined by real entailment from antecedent to consequent, which excludes coincidental connections.

Definitions of Conditional and Disjunctive Propositions: Essential and Accidental Types
Author defines conditional and disjunctive propositions and responds to scope objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the essential and accidental subtypes of conditional (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) propositions and explain how the definitions in the matn accommodate both.

Core Idea
Conditional and disjunctive definitions are broad: they cover both essential and accidental cases.

In this lesson, we analyze the definitions of the conditional and disjunctive hypothetical propositions as given by the author and expanded by the commentator. We will see how the logicians handle an objection: the definitions seem to fit only the essential types, but they are interpreted to also include accidental cases. This reveals the conceptual breadth of the logical terms 'talazum' and 'tanafur'.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of the conditional
Find the matn's definition: 'What necessitates the mutual entailment of the two parts.' Note that 'talazum' here is clarified by the commentator to mean co-occurrence, not two-way entailment.
The definition is for the conditional (mutasila) proposition.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objector argues that this definition only covers the essential conditional (luzumiyya), where there is a real relationship like causality, and not the accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) where parts merely coincide.
Example of essential: 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists.' Accidental: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.'
3
Examine the commentator's defense
The commentator broadens 'talazum' to mean 'tasahub' (co-occurrence), which includes both necessary and accidental co-occurrence. Alternatively, some scholars say the accidental type is disregarded because it is useless in syllogisms.
This shows that definitions in logic can be interpreted broadly to maintain generality.
4
Analyze the disjunctive definition similarly
The matn defines the disjunctive as 'what necessitates mutual opposition without falsehood.' An objection arises that this only fits the oppositional disjunctive (inadiyya), like 'the number is either even or odd,' not the accidental (ittifaqiyya) like 'this is either white or a writer.'
The answer (implied) is that 'tanafur' is also interpreted broadly.
5
Assess the role of these distinctions in logical reasoning
Recognize that essential propositions are informative and used in syllogisms, while accidental ones may be true but are not useful for inference. This distinction helps evaluate the strength of arguments.
The commentator notes that al-Malawi suggested the accidental type is treated as non-existent in syllogisms.

Source Text

لِعَلَاقَةٍ بَيْنَهُمَا تُوجِبُ ذَلِكَ، كَالسَّبَبِيَّةِ؛ نَحْوُ قَوْلِكَ : كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ، فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ ، دُونَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِذَلِكَ لَا لِعَلَاقَةٍ تُوجِبُهُ ، بَلْ لِاِتِّفَاقِ أَنَّ جُزأيهَا وُجِدَا مَعاً»؛ نَحْوُ قَوْلِكَ : إِنْ كَانَ الإِنْسَانُ نَاطِقاً، فَالحِمَارُ نَاهِقٌ إِذْ لَا عَلَاقَةَ بَيْنَ نَاطِقِيَّةِ الإِنْسَانِ وَنَاهِقِيَّةِ الحِمَارِ، حَتَّى يَسْتَلْزِمَ أَحَدُهُمَا الآخَرَ، بَلِ اتَّفَقَ أَنَّهُمَا وُجِدًا مَعاً . وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ تَلَازُمِ الجُزْأَيْنِ : تَصَاحُبُهُمَا ) ؛ سَوَاءٌ كَانَ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ كَمَا اللُّزُومِيَّةِ، أَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ الاتِّفَاقِ كَمَا فِي الاِتِّفَاقِيَّةِ ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ - كَمَا قَالَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ - أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ نَزَّلَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةَ مَنْزِلَةَ العَدَمِ؛ لِعَدَمِ إِنْتَاجِهَا فِي الأَقْيسَةِ، فَيَكُونُ التَّلَازُمُ بِمَعْنَى عَدَمِ صِحَّةِ الِانْفِكَاكِ عَقْلاً؛ أَفَادَهُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِه . قَوْلُهُ : (وَذَاتُ الانْفِصَالِ) أَيْ : صَاحِبَةُ الاِنْفِصَالِ، وَهِيَ المُنْفَصِلَةُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (دُونَ مَيْنِ) أَيْ : دُونَ كَذِبٍ، وَهُوَ مُقَدَّمٌ مِنْ تَأخِيرٍ ، وَالأَصْلُ : وَذَاتُ الِانْفِصَالِ مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَهُمَا دُونَ مَيْنِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَهُمَا ) أَيْ: مَا اقْتَضَتْ وَاسْتَلْزَمَتْ تَنَافِياً وَعِنَاداً بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ . وَاعْتُرِضَ عَلَى المُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ ظَاهِرُ فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ العِنَادِيَةِ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالمُعَانَدَةِ بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ لِذَاتِهِمَا »؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : «العَدَدُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ ، أَوْ فَرْدٌ»، دُونَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ، وَهِيَ : : الَّتِي حُكمَ فِيهَا بِتِلْكَ المُعَانَدَةِ لِمُجَرَّدِ الاتِّفَاقِ»؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ فِي شَخْصِ أَسْودَ كَاتِبِ»: «هَذَا : إِمَّا أَبْيَضُ، أَوْ كَاتِبٌ . الأنبابي قوله : (لَا لِعَلَاقَةِ تُوجِبُهُ يرد عليه أنَّ مِن أنواع العلاقة أن يكون المقدَّم والتّالي مُسبَّبين عن سببٍ ،واحدٍ، كما هنا، ولا شكّ أنَّ ناطقيَّة الإنسان وناهقيَّة الحمار مسبَّبان عن سبب واحد، وهو تعلق القدرة والإرادة عندنا، فيكون هذا المثال من قبيل اللُّزوميّة. ويجاب : بأنَّ المراد لملاحظة علاقة، كما في «الصَّبَّان» نقلاً عن بعضهم ، أو يقال : المراد علاقةٌ خاصة.
Chapter on propositions and their rulings. (59) Their two parts: antecedent and consequent. As for explanation of the conditional: (60) 'What necessitates the mutual entailment of the two parts, and the disjunctive without falsehood.' al-Bajuri: And it is not refuted by something like your saying: 'Daytime exists if the sun has risen' because what is mentioned first in that is not the consequent, rather it is its evidence; because the doctrine of the people of verification in the Arabic language is that the answer to the conditional is always delayed, and what is mentioned first is its evidence. Thus it is taken from al-Qutb, and Ibn Marzuq stated it clearly in his commentary on al-Jumal. However, al-Sa'd mentioned that what is mentioned first in that is exactly the consequent, and even if it comes first in mention, it is consequent in order. He said: The claim of deleting the result in such cases is just the convention of grammarians. End. And it is determinate and must be adopted if he has known it from the convention of logicians. And some of them explained it: that the intention of logicians is meanings, so there is no need to assume something without which the meaning is complete. Let it be considered. His saying: (As for explanation of the conditional) i.e., the one possessing connection, and it is the conditional. And his saying: (What necessitates the mutual entailment of the two parts) i.e., it is what implies and necessitates that, and entailment here is not from both sides; because the proposition only indicates the necessity of the consequent from the antecedent without the converse, even if it is realized in some materials, so it is in the sense of necessity, and its addition to the two parts is due to its connection with them because it is a relation between them. And an objection was raised against the author: that that is apparent in the essential conditional, which is: 'that in which it is judged that a proposition is true on the condition of the truth of another due to a relationship between them that necessitates that, like causality; e.g., your saying: Whenever the sun has risen, daytime exists,' not the accidental conditional, which is: 'that in which it is judged that due to no relationship that necessitates it, but because it coincidentally happens that its two parts occur together'; e.g., your saying: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying' since there is no relationship between the rationality of man and the braying of the donkey such that one necessitates the other, but rather they coincidentally occur together. And it was answered: that by 'entailment of the two parts' is meant their co-occurrence, whether in the way of necessity as in the essential conditional, or in the way of coincidence as in the accidental conditional. And it is possible - as some said - that the author treated the accidental conditional as non-existent because it does not produce results in syllogisms, so entailment would be in the sense of the impossibility of separation rationally. This was conveyed by al-Malawi in his large commentary. His saying: (And the disjunctive) i.e., the one possessing disjunction, and it is the disjunctive. And his saying: (without falsehood) i.e., without lying, and it is a forward displacement, and the original is: 'And the disjunctive is what necessitates mutual opposition between them without falsehood.' And his saying: (What necessitates mutual opposition between them) i.e., what implies and necessitates mutual repulsion and opposition between the two parts. And an objection was raised against the author: that that is apparent in the oppositional disjunctive, which is: 'that in which it is judged that there is opposition between the two sides due to their essence'; e.g., your saying: 'The number is either even or odd,' not the accidental disjunctive, which is: 'that in which it is judged that there is such opposition merely due to coincidence'; e.g., your saying about a black writing person: 'This is either white or a writer.' al-Anbabi: His saying: (not due to a relationship that necessitates it) it is objected that among the types of relationship is that the antecedent and consequent are caused by a single cause, as here, and there is no doubt that the rationality of man and the braying of the donkey are caused by a single cause, which is the attachment of power and will with us, so this example falls under the essential type. And it is answered: that what is intended is the observation of a relationship, as in al-Sabban quoting from some, or it is said: what is intended is a specific relationship.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary (sharh) on a matn defining conditional and disjunctive hypothetical propositions. The author first addresses a preliminary grammatical point: in 'if... then' statements, the first mentioned part is usually the antecedent, but in Arabic grammar the result (jawab al-shart) is actually the delayed part. He cites a disagreement between al-Qutb and Ibn Marzuq versus al-Sa'd, but settles on the logicians' perspective that the intended meaning is paramount, so no deletion is needed. Then he explains the matn's definition of the conditional: 'what necessitates the mutual entailment of the two parts'. He clarifies that 'talazum' here means one-way implication (luzum), not two-way, because the conditional asserts that the consequent follows from the antecedent, not vice versa. An objection is raised: this definition seems to cover only the essential conditional (luzumiyya), where there is a real relationship like causality, and not the accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya), where the two parts merely coincide without a connecting relationship. An example of the essential conditional is 'if the sun has risen, then daytime exists', and of the accidental conditional is 'if man is rational, then the donkey is braying'. The defense is that 'talazum' here is interpreted broadly as 'co-occurrence' (tasahub), covering both essential and accidental. Alternatively, some scholars say the author disregarded the accidental type because it is useless in syllogisms, so 'talazum' then means 'impossibility of separation' rationally. The commentary then turns to the disjunctive proposition, defined as 'what necessitates mutual opposition without falsehood'. He explains 'without falsehood' as a forward phrase (taqdim wa ta'khir), so the full definition is 'the disjunctive is what necessitates mutual opposition between them without falsehood'. An objection similar to the previous one is raised: this definition fits only the oppositional disjunctive (inadiyya), where the two sides are mutually exclusive by nature, like 'the number is either even or odd', not the accidental disjunctive (ittifaqiyya), where two things are mutually exclusive merely by accident, like 'this person is either white or a writer' (said of a black writer). The answer is not explicitly given, but the al-Anbabi gloss appended adds a further refinement: in the accidental example, there actually is a relationship (both caused by divine will), so it is not a pure coincidence. The answer is that the intended relationship is a specific one that is observed in the context. Overall, the passage carefully distinguishes between essential and accidental types within both conditional and disjunctive propositions, showing how the definitions must accommodate both.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an 'accidental conditional' (ittifaqiyya)?
A) If the sun rises, then daytime exists.
B) If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.
C) The number is either even or odd.
D) This person is either white or a writer.

Key Takeaway
The definitions of conditional and disjunctive propositions are intentionally broad to cover both essential and accidental subtypes, with 'talazum' interpreted as co-occurrence and 'tanafur' as opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following conditional statements as essential (luzumiyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya) and justify your answer: (a) 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists.' (b) 'If a person is a scholar, then he knows grammar.' (c) 'If the sky is clear, then the birds are singing.'
?
For the disjunctive 'Either this number is even or it is odd,' identify whether it is oppositional or accidental. Then find a real-world example of an accidental disjunctive from daily life (e.g., 'Either it is raining or I am carrying an umbrella') and explain why it is accidental.

Propositions
intermediate
8 min
The Definition of the Disjunctive Proposition and Its Two Types
Commentary defines the disjunctive proposition and defends its definition against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the oppositional (inadiyya) and coincidental (ittifaqiyya) disjunctive propositions based on the nature of opposition.

Core Idea
A disjunctive proposition is defined by non-coexistence, whether intrinsic or coincidental.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the definition of the disjunctive proposition and defends it against an objection. The objection claimed the definition only covers the oppositional type, not the coincidental. The reply shows that 'opposition' here means mere non-coexistence. This distinction is crucial for understanding how disjunctives function in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the definition of the disjunctive proposition in the passage: it is that which necessitates opposition (tanafur) between its two parts without falsehood. The commentator explains 'opposition' as mutual exclusion and contradiction.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that this definition only fits the oppositional disjunctive (inadiyya), where parts are inherently opposed like even and odd. For example, 'The number is either even or odd' – the parts cannot coexist by their nature.
3
Analyze the response
The commentator gives two answers: (1) 'opposition' is interpreted broadly as non-coexistence, even if merely coincidental; (2) the author treats the coincidental type as effectively non-existent. So both types satisfy the definition.
4
Apply the distinction
Test the coincidental example: 'This (a black writer) is either white or a writer.' Here the two parts cannot both be true, but they can both be false. The opposition is not essential but arises from the particular subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَذَاتُ الانْفِصَالِ) أَيْ : صَاحِبَةُ الاِنْفِصَالِ، وَهِيَ المُنْفَصِلَةُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (دُونَ مَيْنِ) أَيْ : دُونَ كَذِبٍ، وَهُوَ مُقَدَّمٌ مِنْ تَأخِيرٍ ، وَالأَصْلُ : وَذَاتُ الِانْفِصَالِ مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَهُمَا دُونَ مَيْنِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَهُمَا ) أَيْ: مَا اقْتَضَتْ وَاسْتَلْزَمَتْ تَنَافِياً وَعِنَاداً بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ . وَاعْتُرِضَ عَلَى المُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ ظَاهِرُ فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ العِنَادِيَةِ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالمُعَانَدَةِ بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ لِذَاتِهِمَا »؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : «العَدَدُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ ، أَوْ فَرْدٌ»، دُونَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ، وَهِيَ : : الَّتِي حُكمَ فِيهَا بِتِلْكَ المُعَانَدَةِ لِمُجَرَّدِ الاتِّفَاقِ»؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ فِي شَخْصِ أَسْودَ كَاتِبِ»: «هَذَا : إِمَّا أَبْيَضُ، أَوْ كَاتِبٌ . وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ التَّنَافُرِ» بَيْنَهُمَا : عَدَمُ تَصَاحُبِهِمَا ، وَلَوْ لِمُجَرَّدِ الإِنْفَاقِ، أَوْ أَنَّ المُصَنِّف نَزَّلَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةَ مَنْزِلَةَ العَدَمِ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ. قَوْلُهُ : (أَقْسَامُهَا) أَيْ : أَقْسَامُ ذَاتِ الانْفِصَالِ. قَوْلُهُ : (مَانِعُ جَمْعِ ...) كَانَ مُقْتَضَى الظَّاهِرِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «مَانِعَةُ جَمْعٍ، لَكِنَّ الْمُصَنِّفَ ذَكَّرَ بِاعْتِبَارِ كَوْنِ القَضِيَّةِ خَبَراً، وَلَا حَاجَةَ لِقَوْلِ بَعْضِهِمْ : حَذَفَ النَّاءَ لِلضَّرُورَةِ». وَاخْتُلِفَ فِي تَفْسِيرِ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ : - فَقِيلَ - وَهُوَ المَشْهُورُ - : هِيَ : «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزأينِ صِدْقاً وَكَذِباً ، أَوْ صِدْقاً فَقَطْ» فَالأَوَّلُ : إِذَا كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْوُ قَوْلِكَ : «العَدَدُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ ، أَوْ لَا زَوْجٌ ، وَقَوْلِكَ : العَدَدُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ ، أَوْ فَرْدٌ». وَالثَّانِي : إِذَا كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَخَصُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : هَذَا : إِمَّا أَبْيَضُ، أَوْ أَسْودُ»، فَإِنَّ أَسْودَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيض أَبْيَضَ وَهُوَ لَا أَبْيَضُ ؛ لِشُمُولِهِ الأَسْوَدَ، وَالأَحْمَرَ، وَغَيْرِهِمَا . - وَقِيلَ : هِيَ : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزْئِينِ صِدْقاً فَقَط (۱) ؛ بِأَنْ كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَخَصُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ، نَحْرُ مَا ذُكِرَ .
('And the one of disjunction') means: the possessor of disjunction, and it is the disjunctive proposition. And his statement 'without lying' means: without falsehood, and it is advanced from a delay, and the original is: the disjunctive proposition is that which necessitates opposition between its two parts without falsehood. And his statement 'that which necessitates opposition between them' means: that which requires and entails mutual exclusion and contradiction between the two parts. And an objection was raised against the author: that this is apparent in the oppositional disjunctive, which is that in which opposition between the two sides is judged due to their essence, like your statement: 'The number: either even or odd', not the coincidental one, which is that in which that opposition is judged merely due to coincidence, like your statement about a black writing person: 'This: either white or a writer'. And it was answered: that the intended meaning of 'opposition' between them is non-coexistence, even if merely due to coincidence, or that the author treated the coincidental as non-existent, as was mentioned in the conditional. His statement: 'Its divisions' means: divisions of the disjunctive. His statement: 'Preventer of conjunction...' The apparent requirement was to say 'preventer of conjunction' feminine, but the author made it masculine considering the proposition as a report, and there is no need for some saying 'he omitted the feminine for necessity'. And there is disagreement in the interpretation of 'preventer of conjunction'. It was said—and it is the well-known—it is: 'that in which opposition is judged between the two parts in truth and falsity, or in truth only.' The first: when it is composed of a thing and its contradictory, or the equivalent of its contradictory; like your statement: 'The number: either even or not even', and your statement: 'The number: either even or odd'. The second: when it is composed of a thing and the more specific than its contradictory; like your statement: 'This: either white or black', for black is more specific than the contradictory of white, which is 'not white', because it includes black, red, and others. And it was said: it is: that in which opposition is judged between the two parts in truth only, by being composed of a thing and the more specific than its contradictory, like what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition and classification of the disjunctive proposition (al-munfasila). It begins by clarifying the author's (al-Sullam) statement 'and the one of disjunction' meaning the disjunctive proposition. The phrase 'without lying' is explained as 'without falsehood' and the order is inverted for the sake of meaning: the disjunctive proposition is that which necessitates opposition between its two parts truthfully. The commentator then defines 'opposition' as mutual exclusion and contradiction. An objection is raised: this definition of opposition fits only the oppositional disjunctive (al-‘inādiyya), where the parts are inherently opposed (e.g., 'The number is either even or odd'), not the coincidental disjunctive (al-ittifāqiyya), where opposition is merely coincidental (e.g., about a black writer: 'This is either white or a writer'—there is no real opposition). The reply is that the intended meaning of opposition is simply that the two parts do not coexist, even if only by coincidence, or that the author considers the coincidental type as effectively nonexistent for logical purposes. Next, the three divisions of the disjunctive are introduced: (1) preventer of conjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘), (2) preventer of emptiness (māni‘at al-khulū), and (3) both (the true exclusive). The commentator notes the masculine form 'māni‘' instead of 'māni‘a' and justifies it. Then it defines 'preventer of conjunction' in two interpretations. The famous (mashhūr) view: it is a proposition that judges opposition between the two parts in truth and falsity, or in truth only. The first case occurs when the parts are contradictory or equivalent to a contradictory (e.g., even vs. not even). The second case occurs when one part is more specific than the contradictory of the other (e.g., white vs. black, where black is a species of 'not white'). Another view restricts 'preventer of conjunction' to only the truth-only case (the second type). The passage thus thoroughly defines the disjunctive proposition and its sub-types, using examples from numbers and colors—standard logical illustratons in the Islamic tradition.

According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a coincidental disjunctive?
A) The number is either even or odd
B) This is either white or black
C) This (a black writer) is either white or a writer
D) The person is either alive or dead

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive proposition asserts the non-coexistence of its parts, whether due to their essence or due to coincidence.
