The Divisions of the Disjunctive: Preventer of Conjunction
Commentary explains the division of maniat al-jam' and its two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two interpretations of 'preventer of conjunction' (maniat al-jam') and classify examples accordingly.

Core Idea
Maniat al-jam' can mean opposition in both truth and falsity or only in truth, depending on the relationship of the parts.

After defining the disjunctive, the commentator discusses its divisions, focusing on the first division: preventer of conjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘). This is a disjunctive that forbids the combination of its two parts. The passage presents two interpretations: the famous view includes both truth-falsity opposition and truth-only opposition, while another view restricts it to truth-only. Understanding this is essential for correctly evaluating disjunctive syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'preventer of conjunction'
It is a disjunctive that judges opposition between the two parts. Note that there is disagreement on its interpretation.
2
Identify the first (famous) interpretation
It judges opposition in truth and falsity, or in truth only. The first occurs when parts are contradictory (e.g., even vs. not even) or equivalent to a contradictory (e.g., even vs. odd). The second occurs when one part is more specific than the other's contradictory (e.g., white vs. black).
3
Identify the second interpretation
It restricts 'preventer of conjunction' to only the truth-only case, i.e., when the parts are of the second type.
4
Test with examples
'The number is either even or odd' – opposition in truth and falsity (contradictory). 'This is either white or black' – opposition in truth only (sub-contrary).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَذَاتُ الانْفِصَالِ) أَيْ : صَاحِبَةُ الاِنْفِصَالِ، وَهِيَ المُنْفَصِلَةُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (دُونَ مَيْنِ) أَيْ : دُونَ كَذِبٍ، وَهُوَ مُقَدَّمٌ مِنْ تَأخِيرٍ ، وَالأَصْلُ : وَذَاتُ الِانْفِصَالِ مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَهُمَا دُونَ مَيْنِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَهُمَا ) أَيْ: مَا اقْتَضَتْ وَاسْتَلْزَمَتْ تَنَافِياً وَعِنَاداً بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ . وَاعْتُرِضَ عَلَى المُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ ظَاهِرُ فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ العِنَادِيَةِ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالمُعَانَدَةِ بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ لِذَاتِهِمَا »؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : «العَدَدُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ ، أَوْ فَرْدٌ»، دُونَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ، وَهِيَ : : الَّتِي حُكمَ فِيهَا بِتِلْكَ المُعَانَدَةِ لِمُجَرَّدِ الاتِّفَاقِ»؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ فِي شَخْصِ أَسْودَ كَاتِبِ»: «هَذَا : إِمَّا أَبْيَضُ، أَوْ كَاتِبٌ . وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ التَّنَافُرِ» بَيْنَهُمَا : عَدَمُ تَصَاحُبِهِمَا ، وَلَوْ لِمُجَرَّدِ الإِنْفَاقِ، أَوْ أَنَّ المُصَنِّف نَزَّلَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةَ مَنْزِلَةَ العَدَمِ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ. قَوْلُهُ : (أَقْسَامُهَا) أَيْ : أَقْسَامُ ذَاتِ الانْفِصَالِ. قَوْلُهُ : (مَانِعُ جَمْعِ ...) كَانَ مُقْتَضَى الظَّاهِرِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «مَانِعَةُ جَمْعٍ، لَكِنَّ الْمُصَنِّفَ ذَكَّرَ بِاعْتِبَارِ كَوْنِ القَضِيَّةِ خَبَراً، وَلَا حَاجَةَ لِقَوْلِ بَعْضِهِمْ : حَذَفَ النَّاءَ لِلضَّرُورَةِ». وَاخْتُلِفَ فِي تَفْسِيرِ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ : - فَقِيلَ - وَهُوَ المَشْهُورُ - : هِيَ : «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزأينِ صِدْقاً وَكَذِباً ، أَوْ صِدْقاً فَقَطْ» فَالأَوَّلُ : إِذَا كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْوُ قَوْلِكَ : «العَدَدُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ ، أَوْ لَا زَوْجٌ ، وَقَوْلِكَ : العَدَدُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ ، أَوْ فَرْدٌ». وَالثَّانِي : إِذَا كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَخَصُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : هَذَا : إِمَّا أَبْيَضُ، أَوْ أَسْودُ»، فَإِنَّ أَسْودَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيض أَبْيَضَ وَهُوَ لَا أَبْيَضُ ؛ لِشُمُولِهِ الأَسْوَدَ، وَالأَحْمَرَ، وَغَيْرِهِمَا . - وَقِيلَ : هِيَ : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزْئِينِ صِدْقاً فَقَط (۱) ؛ بِأَنْ كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَخَصُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ، نَحْرُ مَا ذُكِرَ .
('And the one of disjunction') means: the possessor of disjunction, and it is the disjunctive proposition. And his statement 'without lying' means: without falsehood, and it is advanced from a delay, and the original is: the disjunctive proposition is that which necessitates opposition between its two parts without falsehood. And his statement 'that which necessitates opposition between them' means: that which requires and entails mutual exclusion and contradiction between the two parts. And an objection was raised against the author: that this is apparent in the oppositional disjunctive, which is that in which opposition between the two sides is judged due to their essence, like your statement: 'The number: either even or odd', not the coincidental one, which is that in which that opposition is judged merely due to coincidence, like your statement about a black writing person: 'This: either white or a writer'. And it was answered: that the intended meaning of 'opposition' between them is non-coexistence, even if merely due to coincidence, or that the author treated the coincidental as non-existent, as was mentioned in the conditional. His statement: 'Its divisions' means: divisions of the disjunctive. His statement: 'Preventer of conjunction...' The apparent requirement was to say 'preventer of conjunction' feminine, but the author made it masculine considering the proposition as a report, and there is no need for some saying 'he omitted the feminine for necessity'. And there is disagreement in the interpretation of 'preventer of conjunction'. It was said—and it is the well-known—it is: 'that in which opposition is judged between the two parts in truth and falsity, or in truth only.' The first: when it is composed of a thing and its contradictory, or the equivalent of its contradictory; like your statement: 'The number: either even or not even', and your statement: 'The number: either even or odd'. The second: when it is composed of a thing and the more specific than its contradictory; like your statement: 'This: either white or black', for black is more specific than the contradictory of white, which is 'not white', because it includes black, red, and others. And it was said: it is: that in which opposition is judged between the two parts in truth only, by being composed of a thing and the more specific than its contradictory, like what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition and classification of the disjunctive proposition (al-munfasila). It begins by clarifying the author's (al-Sullam) statement 'and the one of disjunction' meaning the disjunctive proposition. The phrase 'without lying' is explained as 'without falsehood' and the order is inverted for the sake of meaning: the disjunctive proposition is that which necessitates opposition between its two parts truthfully. The commentator then defines 'opposition' as mutual exclusion and contradiction. An objection is raised: this definition of opposition fits only the oppositional disjunctive (al-‘inādiyya), where the parts are inherently opposed (e.g., 'The number is either even or odd'), not the coincidental disjunctive (al-ittifāqiyya), where opposition is merely coincidental (e.g., about a black writer: 'This is either white or a writer'—there is no real opposition). The reply is that the intended meaning of opposition is simply that the two parts do not coexist, even if only by coincidence, or that the author considers the coincidental type as effectively nonexistent for logical purposes. Next, the three divisions of the disjunctive are introduced: (1) preventer of conjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘), (2) preventer of emptiness (māni‘at al-khulū), and (3) both (the true exclusive). The commentator notes the masculine form 'māni‘' instead of 'māni‘a' and justifies it. Then it defines 'preventer of conjunction' in two interpretations. The famous (mashhūr) view: it is a proposition that judges opposition between the two parts in truth and falsity, or in truth only. The first case occurs when the parts are contradictory or equivalent to a contradictory (e.g., even vs. not even). The second case occurs when one part is more specific than the contradictory of the other (e.g., white vs. black, where black is a species of 'not white'). Another view restricts 'preventer of conjunction' to only the truth-only case (the second type). The passage thus thoroughly defines the disjunctive proposition and its sub-types, using examples from numbers and colors—standard logical illustratons in the Islamic tradition.

صِدْقاً وَكَذِباً
صِدْقاً فَقَط
نقيض
الأخص

Key Takeaway
Maniat al-jam' can forbid the combination of its parts in both truth and falsity or only in truth, depending on whether the parts are contradictory or merely sub-contrary.

Types of Disjunctive Propositions (Mani' Jam' and Mani' Khuluww)
Author defines mani' jam', mani' khuluww, and mani' jam' wa khuluww and their relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of disjunctive propositions (mani' jam', mani' khuluww, and both) and identify their logical relations using examples from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Disjunctive propositions in logic mirror exclusive or, inclusive or, and both, defined by truth and falsehood conditions.

Disjunctive propositions (munfasila) are crucial in Islamic reasoning, especially in usul al-fiqh and kalam, where they frame exclusive or inclusive options. This lesson unpacks the three types defined by classical logicians: preventing combination (both true), preventing emptiness (both false), or both. Understanding their relations sharpens your ability to parse legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the Arabic and translation. Mani' al-jam' prevents both disjuncts being true. Mani' al-khuluww prevents both being false. The third prevents both. Notice the matn's phrasing: 'preventer of combination, or of emptiness, or both'.
Think of these as exclusive OR, inclusive OR, and exclusive AND? Actually, exclusive OR is like mani' jam' wa khuluww.
2
Analyze the truth conditions
For mani' jam': at most one true. For mani' khuluww: at least one true. For both: exactly one true. These correspond to logical operators: XOR (exclusive or), OR (inclusive), and a biconditional? Actually, both is an exclusive disjunction.
In Islamic logic, the term 'haqiqi' (real) is used for the third type as the most specific.
3
Exemplify each type with Islamic legal scenarios
Mani' jam': 'Either this prayer is valid or invalid' – cannot be both true (a prayer cannot be simultaneously valid and invalid). Mani' khuluww: 'Either the defendant is guilty or innocent' – at least one must be true (no third state). Both: 'Either the ruling is wajib or haram' – exactly one is true (assuming no middle in this binary).
These examples illustrate the logical form used in fiqh debates.
4
Work through the relations according to the first opinion
The commentary explains that both types share a common area: when the disjuncts are a thing and its exact contrary or equal. Mani' jam' is exclusive to composites with a more specific contrary; mani' khuluww to composites with a more general contrary. Their relation is 'generality and specificity in some respect' (عموم وخصوص من وجه).
Use the Venn diagram or set logic to visualize: overlapping circles with each having an exclusive part.
5
Contrast with the second opinion
According to the second opinion, the two types are completely different (تباين) – no overlap. The matn advises to ponder this nuance.
This shows interpretive flexibility in the logical tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَانِعُ جَمْعٍ، أَوْ خُلُوِّ ، أَوْهُمَا وَهُوَ الحَقِيقِيُّ الأَخَصُّ فَاعْلَمَا
وَانْفِرَادِ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَخَصُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ. وَكَذَلِكَ النِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَ مَانِعَتِهِمَا وَمَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ :
- لاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ.
- وَانْفِرَادِ مَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالْأَعَمِّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ. وَأَمَّا النِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ وَمَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ فَالعُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ :
- لِاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضه.
- وَانْفِرَادِ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالْأَخَصُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ.
- وَانْفِرَادِ مَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَعَمُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ.
هَذَا كُلُّهُ عَلَى القَوْلِ الأَوَّلِ فِي كُلِّ مِنْ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ وَمَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى القَوْلِ الثَّانِي فِي ذَلِكَ فَالنِّسْبَةُ فِي ذَلِكَ كُلِّهِ التَّبَايُنُ ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
A preventer of combination, or of emptiness, or both, and it is the real [disjunction] i.e. the most specific, so know [this]. And the exclusivity of mani' al-jam' in the composite of a thing and the more specific of its contrary. And likewise the relation between the two types and mani' al-khuluww: because of their commonality in the composite of a thing and its contrary, or the equal to its contrary. And the exclusivity of mani' al-khuluww in the composite of a thing and the more general of its contrary. As for the relation between mani' al-jam' and mani' al-khuluww, it is generality and specificity in some respect: because of their commonality in the composite of a thing and its contrary, or the equal to its contrary; and the exclusivity of mani' al-jam' in the composite of a thing and the more specific of its contrary; and the exclusivity of mani' al-khuluww in the composite of a thing and the more general of its contrary. All this is according to the first opinion concerning each of mani' al-jam' and mani' al-khuluww. But according to the second opinion concerning that, the relation in all that is complete difference, so let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is definitional. It defines the three types of disjunctive conditional propositions (munfasila). The matn states: 'Mani' jam' (preventer of combination), or mani' khuluww (preventer of emptiness), or both, and that is the real (exclusive) disjunction, i.e., the most specific, so know this.' The commentary by al-Bajuri then explains the logical relations between these types. First, it says that mani' jam' is unique in a composite of a thing and something more specific than its contrary. Next, it describes the relation between the two types: they share in a composite of a thing and its contrary or something equal to its contrary, but each has its exclusive area. Then it specifies the relation between mani' jam' and mani' khuluww as general and specific in some respect (عموم وخصوص من وجه), because they share a common area (composite of thing and its contrary) but differ in others: mani' jam' is exclusive to composite with more specific contrary, mani' khuluww is exclusive to composite with more general contrary. Finally, it notes that this is according to one opinion; on another opinion, the two are completely different (تباين).

Key Terms
مانع جمع
مانع خلو
مانع جمع وخلو

B) They are general and specific in some respect.
C) They are identical.
D) They are completely different.

Key Takeaway
Mani' jam' and mani' khuluww are distinct logical types with overlapping and exclusive domains, as explained through composites of a thing and its contrary.

?

Definition of Contradiction and Its Role in Reductio Proof
Defines contradiction and shows its application in reductio proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define logical contradiction according to the Mantiq tradition and apply it in a qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) proof using an Islamic example.

Core Idea
Contradiction is the difference in quality between two propositions; one must be true.

In this passage, the author defines contradiction (tanāquḍ) both linguistically and technically, and al-Anbabi explains its crucial role in demonstrative proof. You will learn the precise definition and see how contradiction is used in qiyas al-khalf, a powerful method for establishing truths in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Find the linguistic and technical definitions of contradiction in the text. The linguistic meaning is 'affirmation and negation of a thing'; the technical meaning is 'difference of two propositions in quality' with the necessity that one is true.
2
Extract genus and differentia
The genus is 'khulf' (difference), and the differentia is the specification to two propositions differing in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This is a real definition (hadd haqiqi).
3
Understand the necessity of truth
The definition implies that one of the two contradictories must be true and the other false. This is a necessary logical relation underlying all contradiction-based proofs.
4
See contradiction in qiyas al-khalf
Al-Anbabi gives an example: to prove something is an animal, assume its contradictory (not animal), derive a contradiction with a known truth (it is human), so the assumption is false and the original is true. This is a conditional syllogism of modus tollens.
The desired conclusion is established indirectly by refuting its contradictory.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Construct a qiyas al-khalf on an Islamic topic, e.g., proving nabidh is forbidden by assuming it is not forbidden and deriving a contradiction with a known ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ
تَنَاقُضٌ : خُلْفُ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ فِي كَيْفِ، وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ أَمْرٌ قُفِيْ
قَوْلُهُ : (فَصْلٌ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ) أَيْ : فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ وَأَحْكَامِهِ ، وَقَدْ أَشَارَ لِلأَوَّلِ بِالبَيْتِ الأَوَّلِ، وَلِلثَّانِي بِمَا بَعْدَهُ.
وَمَعْنَى التَّنَاقُضِ لُغَةٌ: «إِنْبَاتُ الشَّيْءِ وَرَفْعُهُ»
وَاصْطِلاحاً مَا ذَكَرَهُ المُصَنِّفُ
قَوْلُهُ : («تَنَاقُضٌ مُبْتَدَأ ، وَالمُسَوّعُ : إِرَادَةُ الجِنْسِ، أَوْ وُقُوعُهُ فِي مَعْرِضِ التَّفْصِيلِ الآتِي.
كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ المُصَنِّفُ .
وَقَوْلُهُ : (خُلْفُ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ ... إلخ) (الخُلْفُ»: اسْمُ مَصْدَرٍ ؛ بِمَعْنَى : الِاخْتِلَافِ، وَهُوَ جِنْسٌ
دَخَلَ فِيهِ جَمِيعُ الاخْتِلَافَاتِ.
وَخَرَجَ بِإِضَافَتِهِ إِلَى القَضِيَّتَيْنِ : خُلْفُ غَيْرِهِمَا :
الأنبابي
[ قوله : ] (فَصْلٌ فِي التَّناقُضِ وجه الحاجة إلى التناقض والعكس : أنَّ إقامة الدليل في بعض المواضع قد يقوم على إبطال النقيض، والمطلوب نقيض هذا النقيض، أو على صدق المعكوس، والمطلوب عكسه، فإن بطل أحد النقيضين كان الآخر حقًا، وإذا صدق المعكوس صدق العكس؛ إذ يلزم من صدق الملزوم صدق اللازم.
فمِنَ الأوَّل قولك في قياس الخلف: «لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ هَذَا حَيَوَاناً لَمْ يَكُنْ إِنْسَانَا، لَكِنَّهُ إِنْسَانٌ فـ : «هُوَ حَيَوَانٌ»، فهذا المطلوب لم يقم الدليل ابتداءً عليه، بل على إبطال نقيضه بنفي لازمه، فلزم صدقه. ومِنَ الثاني ما ذكروه في الأشكال الثلاثة غير الأوّل من ردّها للأوّل بالعكس، ومثالُهُ في الثَّاني: لَا شَيْء مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِحَيَوَانِ وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ»، فإذا ردَّ إلى الأوَّل بعكس الصغرى وجعلها كبرى أنتج : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ»، والمطلوب عكسه، وهو لازم صدقه منه.
وَإِنَّما قدَّم التناقض ; لأنَّه يجري في جميع القضايا، بخلاف العكس كما يعلم من كلام المصنّف .
Chapter on Contradiction. Contradiction: the difference of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is a matter followed [i.e., established]. His statement: 'Chapter on contradiction' means concerning its definition and its rules, and he indicated the former by the first verse and the latter by what follows. And the meaning of contradiction linguistically: affirmation and negation of a thing; terminologically as the author mentioned. His statement: 'Contradiction' is a subject, and the musawwa' [term permitting it to be indefinite] indicates the genus or its occurrence in the context of the coming detail, as the author mentioned. And his statement: 'the difference of the two propositions ... etc.' 'al-khulf' is a noun of source meaning difference, and it is a genus encompassing all differences. And by adding it to 'the two propositions', it excludes difference of others. Al-Anbabi: [His statement:] 'Chapter on contradiction' — the need for contradiction and conversion: that the establishment of proof in some places may be based on refuting the contradictory, and the desired is the contradictory of that contradictory, or on the truth of the converse, and the desired is its conversion. For if one of the two contradictories is false, the other is true, and if the converse is true, the conversion is true; because the truth of the necessary implies the truth of the necessary. An example of the first is your statement in qiyas al-khalf: 'If this were not an animal, then it would not be a human, but it is a human, therefore it is an animal.' So this desired [conclusion] was not proven initially, but by refuting its contradictory through negating its necessary, so its truth follows. An example of the second is what they mentioned in the three figures other than the first of reducing them to the first by conversion, and its example in the second: 'No stone is an animal; every human is an animal.' So when reduced to the first by converting the minor premise and making it the major, it yields: 'No human is a stone.' And the desired is its conversion, and it is a necessary [consequence] of its truth. And he presented contradiction first because it applies to all propositions, unlike conversion, as is known from the author's words.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is definitional, but also includes epistemological justification. It defines logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) and explains its importance. First, the matn gives the definition: contradiction is the difference (khulf) between two propositions in quality (i.e., one affirmative, one negative), and it is necessarily that one of them is true (and the other false). The commentary expands: linguistically, it is affirmation and negation of the same thing. Technically, it is as the matn says. The term 'khulf' is a genus for all differences, and adding it to 'the two propositions' excludes differences between other things. Then al-Anbabi explains why contradiction is needed: in demonstrative reasoning, sometimes we prove a conclusion by disproving its contradictory (qiyas al-khalf), and other times we need conversion ('aks) to reduce syllogisms to the first figure. The example of qiyas al-khalf: to prove that something is an animal, we assume the opposite (it is not an animal) and derive a contradiction (it would not be human, but it is human), so the negation is false, hence the original is true. For conversion, the example from the second figure: from 'No stone is animal' and 'Every human is animal' we convert the minor premise to 'No animal is stone' (though the text shows conversion differently), then the syllogism becomes in the first figure, yielding the converse of the original conclusion. The final reason given for presenting contradiction before conversion is that contradiction applies to all propositions (universal, particular, etc.) while conversion only applies to some.

خلف
قياس الخلف
عكس

Key Takeaway
Contradiction is the logical backbone of qiyas al-khalf, enabling proofs by showing the negation of a claim leads to an impossible self-contradiction.

Understanding Contradiction: Definition, Objection, and Refinement
Definition of contradiction with objection and refinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given pair of propositions constitutes a genuine logical contradiction by verifying the need for difference in quality, necessary truth of one, and the required unities.

Core Idea
Contradiction is not just opposite quality—it demands necessary truth of one under strict unities.

In this lesson, we examine the classical definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ) from the Islamic logical tradition. The definition requires two propositions that differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and are such that one is necessarily true and the other false. However, this definition faced an objection: it is not exclusive enough, as it would include many pairs that do not truly contradict. The response refines the definition by emphasizing that the truth of one must be a necessary consequence, which only holds when all relevant unities—subject, predicate, time, place, etc.—are preserved. By working through this passage, you will learn to spot genuine contradictions in theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Isolate the two conditions in the definition
The author states that contradiction is 'difference of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is a necessary consequence.' Write down these two conditions: (1) difference in quality (one affirmative, one negative), (2) truth of one is necessary (i.e., it cannot be that both are true or both false).
The second condition is expressed by the phrase 'amrun qufiya' (a necessary consequence), which is key to the refinement.
2
Understand the objection: definition is not exclusive
The objector gives four categories of counterexamples where two propositions differ in quality but do not satisfy the second condition: (a) both can be true or both false, (b) both must be true, (c) both must be false, (d) one true one false coincidentally (not necessarily). For each category, the pair differs in quality but the truth values are not necessary as required.
For example, 'Zaid is standing, 'Amr is not standing' can both be false (if both are sitting), so it's not a contradiction.
3
See how 'necessary consequence' excludes the counterexamples
The response argues that the second condition (truth of one is a necessary consequence) rules out all these cases. In genuine contradiction, it must be impossible for both to be true or both false—only then is the consequence necessary. The objector's examples fail because they allow both same truth values or only accidental truth.
Focus on the concept of necessity: it must hold always, not just in some instances.
4
Identify the role of the unities (waḥdāt)
The necessary consequence only holds when the two propositions share the same subject, predicate, time, place, relation, condition, potentiality/actuality, and whole/part. Without these unities, the truth of one is not forced. For instance, 'Zaid is standing, 'Amr is not standing' lacks unity of subject.
The list of unities is standard; later texts may expand it. These ensure the propositions are about exactly the same state of affairs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٦٣) «تَنَاقُضٌ»: خُلْفُ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ فِي كَيْفِ، وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ أَمْرٌ قُفِي.
وَاعْتُرِضَ عَلَى المُصَنِّفِ بِأَنَّ هَذَا التَّعْرِيفَ غَيْرُ مَانِعِ؛ لِصِدْقِهِ بِخُلْفِ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ فِي الكَيْفِ: مَعَ جَوَازِ صِدْقِهِمَا وَكَذِبِهِمَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ، عَمْرُو لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ، وَقَوْلِكَ: زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِكَاتِبٍ، وَقَوْلِكَ: زَيْدٌ صَائِمُ الآنَ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِصَائِمِ أَمْسَ؛ إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ. وَمَعَ وُجُوبِ صِدْقِهِمَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ. وَمَعَ وُجُوبِ كَذِبِهِمَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانٍ. وَمَعَ صِدْقِ إِحْدَاهُمَا وَكَذِبِ الأُخْرَى اتِّفَاقاً لَا اطِّرَاداً؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَيَوَانٍ، وَقَوْلِكَ: بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانٍ.
وَأُجِيبَ: بِأَنَّ جَمِيعَ ذَلِكَ خَرَجَ بِقَوْلِهِ: وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ أَمْرٌ قُفِي، فَإِنَّهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلاسْتِثْنَافِ، جَعْلُهُ حَالاً أَوْلَى، فَيَكُونُ قَيْداً فِي التَّعْرِيفِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ.
قَوْلُهُ: (فِي كَيْفِ) أَيْ: فِي الإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ، وَكَذَا فِي الكَمْ؛ أَعْنِي: الكُلِّيَّةَ وَالجُزْيَّةَ إِنْ كَانَتِ القَضِيَّةُ الأَصْلِيَّةُ مُسَوَّرَةً، كَمَا سَيَذْكُرُهُ المُصَنِّفُ بِقَوْلِهِ: وَإِنْ تَكُنْ مَحْصُورَةٌ بِالسُّورِ... إلخ، وَإِنَّمَا اقْتَصَرَ عَلَى ذِكْرِ الكَيْفِ هُنَا؛ لِاطِّرَادِهِ فِي جَمِيعِ القَضَايَا حَتَّى الشَّخْصِيَّةَ وَالمُهْمَلَةَ، بِخِلَافِ الكَم، كَمَا هُوَ ظَاهِرٌ.
قَوْلُهُ: (وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ) أَيْ: وَكَذِبُ الآخَرَ، فَفِي كَلَامِهِ اكْتِفَاءُ. وَكَانَ مُقْتَضَى الظَّاهِرِ أَنْ يَقُولَ: «وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدَةٍ ... إلخ»، لَكِنَّهُ نَظَرَ إِلَى كَوْنِ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ بِمَعْنَى القَوْلَيْنِ.
وَقَوْلُهُ: (أَمْرٌ قُفِيْ) أَيْ: تُبِعَ، وَذَلِكَ كِنَايَةٌ عَنْ كَوْنِهِ مُطَّرِداً، وَلَا يَكُونُ كَذَلِكَ إِلَّا عِنْدَ ثُبُوتِ الوَحَدَاتِ المَشْهُورَةِ، وَهِيَ: وَحْدَةُ المَوْضُوعِ، وَوَحْدَةُ المَحْمُولِ، وَوَحْدَةُ الزَّمَانِ، وَوَحْدَةُ المَكَانِ، وَوَحْدَةُ الإِضَافَةِ، وَوَحْدَةُ الشَّرْطِ، وَوَحْدَةُ القُوَّةِ أَوِ الفِعْلِ، وَوَحْدَةُ الكُلِّ أَوِ الجُزْءِ، .. إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ؛ أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِنَا: زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ، عَمْرُو لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ.
(63) 'Contradiction' is the difference of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is a necessary consequence. And an objection was raised against the author that this definition is not exclusive, because it applies to the difference of two propositions in quality: with the possibility of both being true or both false; as in your saying: Zaid is standing, 'Amr is not standing, and your saying: Zaid is standing, Zaid is not a writer, and your saying: Zaid is fasting now, Zaid is not fasting yesterday; etc. And with the necessity of both being true; as in your saying: Some animal is human, Some animal is not human. And with the necessity of both being false; as in your saying: Every animal is human, and No animal is human. And with the truth of one and falsity of the other contingently, not universally; as in your saying: Every human is animal, and No human is animal, and your saying: Some human is animal, Some human is not animal. And it was answered: All that is excluded by his saying: 'and the truth of one is a necessary consequence', for although it is possible for it to be [to read it as] independent, making it a circumstantial accusative is better, so it becomes a restriction in the definition, so reflect. His saying: 'in quality', meaning: in affirmation and negation, and likewise in quantity; I mean universality and particularity if the original proposition is quantified, as the author will mention in his saying: 'And if it is quantified by a quantifier... etc.' And he only limited mention to quality here because it applies consistently to all propositions including singular and indefinite, unlike quantity, as is apparent. His saying: 'and the truth of one' means: and the falsity of the other, so in his speech there is conciseness. And the apparent requirement was that he say: 'and the truth of one (feminine)... etc.', but he considered that the two propositions are in the sense of two statements. And his saying: 'a necessary consequence' means: followed, and that is a metaphor for its being constant, and it is only like that upon the establishment of the well-known unities: unity of subject, unity of predicate, unity of time, unity of place, unity of relation, unity of condition, unity of potentiality or actuality, unity of whole or part, ... etc.; do you not see that it is not like that in the case of our saying: Zaid is standing, 'Amr is not standing.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage defines the logical concept of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and then refines it in response to an objection. The definition is: two propositions differ in quality (i.e., one affirmative, one negative), and it is necessarily the case that one is true and the other false. The objector argues that the definition is not exclusive (ghayru māni‘): it would include pairs that differ in quality but do not have the necessary truth/falsity relation. He gives four classes of counterexamples: (1) both could be true or false (e.g., 'Zaid is standing, 'Amr is not standing'—they can both be false if both are sitting); (2) both must be true (e.g., 'Some animal is human, some animal is not human'—both are true); (3) both must be false (e.g., 'Every animal is human, no animal is human'—both false); (4) one true, one false, but only coincidentally, not universally (e.g., 'Every human is animal, no human is animal'—the first is true, the second false, but if we substitute 'animal' and 'human' in other propositions this does not always hold). The answer to the objection is that the definition includes the phrase 'the truth of one is a necessary consequence' (wa-ṣidqu wāḥidin amrun qufiya). This phrase is interpreted as a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) restricting the definition: contradiction requires that the truth of one necessarily follows from the laws of logic, not just any difference in quality. The explanation then details that 'difference in quality' means one affirmative, one negative; also if the propositions are quantified, they must differ in quantity (one universal, one particular). The author focused on quality because it applies to all propositions including singular and indefinite, whereas quantity does not. The phrase 'and the truth of one' implicitly includes the falsity of the other. Finally, the 'necessary consequence' is only realized when certain unities (waḥdāt) are maintained: unity of subject, predicate, time, place, relation, condition, potentiality/actuality, whole/part, and others. For example, 'Zaid is standing, 'Amr is not standing' lacks unity of subject, so it is not a contradiction. The definition thus ensures that contradictory pairs are exactly those that cannot both be true and cannot both be false under the specified unities.

Key Terms
تناقض
خلف
كيف
صدق واحد أمر قفي
وحدات

A) Zaid is standing and Zaid is not standing at the same time and place.
B) Zaid is standing and 'Amr is not standing.

Key Takeaway
A genuine contradiction requires two propositions differing in quality with the truth of one being a necessary consequence, which is only achieved when all relevant unities (subject, predicate, time, place, etc.) are present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1) Consider the two propositions: 'All wine is forbidden' and 'Some wine is not forbidden.' Determine whether they form a genuine contradiction according to the definition, checking quality, necessity, and unities. Explain your reasoning.

Contradiction of Particular and Indefinite Propositions
Rule for contradicting particular and indefinite propositions with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of any particular or indefinite proposition by changing its quality while preserving the eight unities, and identify the disagreement regarding indefinite contradictories.

Core Idea
The contradictory flips quality but keeps all other unities intact.

This lesson explains how to form the correct contradictory (naqīḍ) of particular (juz'iyya) and indefinite (muhmala) propositions. The rule is simple – change the quality from affirmative to negative or vice versa – but it must be applied while respecting the eight unities (e.g., subject, time, place). Al-Bajuri's commentary illustrates each unity with everyday Arabic sentences. The passage also reveals a subtle difference of opinion: some logicians insist that the contradictory of an indefinite proposition must be a universal, not another indefinite.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the rule
The base text states: 'If it is particular or indefinite, its contradictory is by quality: that you change it.' This means you only flip the affirmation/negation; you do not change modality or the quantifier (for particular).
2
Examine the eight unities through examples
Al-Bajuri provides eight pairs showing that the contradictory must keep the same subject, predicate, and additional conditions: unity of subject, time, place, relation, condition, potentiality/actuality, whole/part, and instrument. For each, note what is preserved and what changes.
The unities are implicit in the definition of contradiction; the examples make them explicit.
3
Understand the disagreement on indefinite propositions
The author implies that the contradictory of an indefinite is another indefinite (e.g., 'Man is an animal' contradicts 'Man is not an animal'). However, other logicians argue that an indefinite has the force of a particular, so its true contradictory is a universal negative: 'No human is an animal'.
This disagreement shows the importance of clarifying the quantifier force.
4
Apply the rule to new examples
Take a particular affirmative like 'Some scholars are just.' Its contradictory is 'Some scholars are not just.' For an indefinite like 'Water is pure,' state both possible contradictories according to the two views.
Practice ensures you can handle both types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٦٤) فإن تكن شخصية أو مهملة فنقضها بالكيف أن تبدله.
الباجوري: وقولنا: زيد قائم، زيد ليس بكاتب. وقولنا: زيد صائم اليوم، زيد ليس بصائم أمس. وقولنا: زيد جالس في المسجد، زيد ليس بجالس في السوق. وقولنا: زيد أب لعمرو، زيد ليس بأب لبكر. وقولنا: الزكاة واجبة في مال الصبي إذا بلغ نصاباً، الزكاة ليست واجبة في مال الصبي إذا لم يبلغ نصاباً. وقولنا: الخمر في الدن مسكر بالقوة، الخمر في الدن ليس بمسكر بالفعل. وقولنا: الزنجي أسود أي: جزؤه، الزنجي ليس بأسود أي: كله. وقولنا: زيد كاتب بالقلم الحديد، زيد ليس بكاتب بغير القلم الحديد. وبذلك علم أن قول المصنف: وصدق واحد... إلخ مغن عن اشتراط الوحدات المذكورة، فليتأمل. قوله: (فإن تكن... إلخ) الفاء: إما تفريعية، أو فصيحية، والضمير يرجع للقضية من حيث هي، وقوله: «فنقضها» مبتدأ، وقوله: أن تبدله خبر، واحترز بقوله: بالكيف عن نقضها بالجهة، فإن له أحكاماً مذكورة في المطولات. ويحتمل أن قوله: (بالكيف هو الخبر، وقوله: أن تبدله بدل منه على حد البدل في نحو: نفعني زيد علمه. ومقتضى كلام المصنف: أن نقيض الشخصية: شخصية مخالفة لها في الكيف، ونقيض المهملة: مهملة كذلك، وهو عند غير المصنف مسلم في الأولى دون الثانية؛ لأن نقيض المهملة عند غير المصنف إنما هو كلية تخالفها في الكيف؛ لكونها في قوة الجزئية: فنقيض المهملة الموجبة نحو: الإنسان حيوان: سالبة كلية نحو: لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان.
(64) And if it is particular or indefinite, then its contradictory is by quality: that you change it. Al-Bajuri: And our saying: 'Zayd is standing, Zayd is not writing.' And our saying: 'Zayd is fasting today, Zayd is not fasting yesterday.' And our saying: 'Zayd is sitting in the mosque, Zayd is not sitting in the market.' And our saying: 'Zayd is the father of 'Amr, Zayd is not the father of Bakr.' And our saying: 'Zakat is obligatory on the property of a minor if it reaches the nisab, zakat is not obligatory on the property of a minor if it does not reach the nisab.' And our saying: 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating potentially, wine in the jar is not intoxicating actually.' And our saying: 'The black person is black' meaning: part of him, 'the black person is not black' meaning: all of him. And our saying: 'Zayd is a writer with the iron pen, Zayd is not a writer with other than the iron pen.' And by that it is known that the author's saying: 'and truth of one... etc.' suffices for stipulating the mentioned unities, so reflect. His saying: 'if it is... etc.', the fa' is either consequential or eloquent, and the pronoun refers to the proposition as such, and his saying: 'its contradictory' is the mubtada', and his saying: 'that you change it' is the khabar, and he cautioned by his saying: 'by quality' against contradicting it by modality, for it has rulings mentioned in the longer works. And it is possible that his saying: 'by quality' is the khabar, and his saying: 'that you change it' is a substitute from it, like the substitute in 'Zayd benefited me, his knowledge.' And the implication of the author's speech is that the contradictory of a particular is a particular differing from it in quality, and the contradictory of an indefinite is an indefinite likewise, and this is conceded by others for the first but not the second; because the contradictory of an indefinite according to others is only a universal differing in quality, due to its being in the power of a particular: so the contradictory of affirmative indefinite like 'Man is an animal' is a universal negative like 'No human is an animal'.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage gives the rule for forming the contradictory of particular (juz'iyya) and indefinite (muhmala) propositions. The author states that the contradictory is formed by changing the quality (from affirmative to negative or vice versa). Al-Bajuri then illustrates each of the eight unities with concrete examples from everyday Arabic sentences. For example, unity of subject: 'Zayd is standing, Zayd is not writing' (same subject). Unity of time: 'Zayd is fasting today, Zayd is not fasting yesterday' (same subject but different time). Unity of place: 'Zayd is sitting in the mosque, Zayd is not sitting in the market'. Unity of relation: 'Zayd is father of 'Amr, Zayd is not father of Bakr'. Unity of condition: 'Zakat is obligatory if nisab is reached, not obligatory if not'. Unity of potentiality/actuality: 'Wine is intoxicating potentially, not actually'. Unity of whole/part: 'The black person is black (part), not black (whole)'. Unity of instrument: 'Zayd writes with iron pen, not with others'. These examples show that the condition of unities is inherent in the definition of contradiction. The commentator then notes that some scholars differ: for indefinite propositions, the contradictory should be a universal, not another indefinite, because an indefinite has the force of a particular (juz'iyya). Thus 'Man is an animal' (indefinite affirmative) is contradictory to 'No human is an animal' (universal negative).

Key Terms
شخصية
مهملة
نقيض
جهة

B) 'No human is an animal' (universal negative).
C) 'Some man is not an animal' (particular negative).
D) 'Man is not an animal' (indefinite negative) with same subject/predicate.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a particular or indefinite proposition is obtained by inverting its quality while preserving the unities; the indefinite case has a disputed contradictory form (indefinite vs universal).

Awwaliyyat: Self-evident Propositions and Their Etymology
Author defines awwaliyyat and explains the correct pronunciation of the term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define awwaliyyat (self-evident propositions) by identifying its genus and differentia, and explain the two possible pronunciations of the term.

Core Idea
Awwaliyyat are propositions known immediately by the intellect without any proof.

In this passage, the author defines awwaliyyat, one of the six types of certain premises. He gives examples to illustrate their self-evident nature, and then discusses a subtle point of Arabic morphology concerning the spelling and pronunciation of the term. This lesson will walk you through the logical definition and the linguistic nuance.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of awwaliyyat
Find the sentence in the Arabic: 'والأوليات هي: القضايا التي يدركها العقل بمجرد تصور الطرفين'. This is the core definition.
2
Break down the definition into genus and differentia
Identify the genus (jins) as 'قضايا' (propositions) and the differentia (fasl) as 'يدركها العقل بمجرد تصور الطرفين' (perceived by the intellect merely by conceptualizing the two terms).
3
Examine the examples
Apply the definition to the examples given: 'الواحد نصف الاثنين' (one is half of two) and 'الكل أعظم من الجزء' (the whole is greater than the part). Confirm that they fit the definition: as soon as you understand 'one' and 'two', you know the relation.
4
Understand the two pronunciations
Note that some scholars pronounce it as 'أوليات' (with lightened ya, as plural of 'أولى'), but the common and preferred pronunciation is 'أوليّات' (with shadda on waw and ya, as a relative adjective to 'الأول'). The author favors the latter because it matches the meter of the matn and reflects the meaning (judged from the first glance).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَهُوَ مُتَرَكْبٌ مِنْ هَذِهِ الضَّرُورِيَّاتِ السِّتْ إِمَّا حَقِيقَةً وَإِمَّا حُكْمًا. وَالأَوَّلِيَّاتُ هِيَ: الْقَضَايَا الَّتِي يُدْرِكُهَا الْعَقْلُ بِمُجَرَّدِ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ: «الْوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الاثْنَيْنِ» وَ «الْكُلُّ أَعْظَمُ مِنَ الْجُزْءِ». وَقَدْ ضَبَطَ بَعْضُ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ «الأَوْلَيَاتِ»: بِضَمِّ الْهَمْزَةِ وَسُكُونِ الْوَاوِ وَفَتْحِ اللَّامِ وَتَخْفِيفِ الْيَاءِ؛ عَلَى أَنَّهُ جَمْعُ «أَوْلَى». لَكِنَّ الَّذِي جَرَى عَلَى الأَلْسِنَةِ: «أَوَّلِيَّاتٌ» بِفَتْحِ الْهَمْزَةِ وَتَشْدِيدِ الْوَاوِ وَكَسْرِ اللَّامِ وَتَشْدِيدِ الْيَاءِ، وَهُوَ صَحِيحٌ أَيْضًا؛ عَلَى أَنَّهَا مَنْسُوبَةٌ إِلَى «الأَوَّلِ»، لِحُكْمِ الْعَقْلِ بِهَا مِنْ أَوَّلِ وَهْلَةٍ، إِذْ لَا تَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى شَيْءٍ بَعْدَ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ. بَلْ هُوَ الْمُتَعَيِّنُ فِي الْمَتْنِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ الْمُوَافِقُ لِلْوَزْنِ.
Therefore it is composed of these six necessary premises either in reality or in judgment. And the self-evident propositions are: the propositions that the intellect perceives by merely conceptualizing the two terms; like your saying: 'One is half of two' and 'The whole is greater than the part.' And some verifying scholars have transcribed 'الأوليات' with a damma on the hamza, sukun on the waw, fatha on the lam, and a lightened ya, considering it a plural of 'أولى'. But what is prevalent on tongues is 'أوليّات' with a fatha on the hamza, shadda on the waw, kasra on the lam, shadda on the ya, and that is also correct; considering it is relative to 'الأول' (first), because the intellect judges them from the first glance, as they do not depend on anything after conceptualizing the two terms. Rather, it is the determined in the text because it agrees with the meter.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a definition of awwaliyyat (self-evident propositions). The author begins by stating that the composition of theoretical premises ultimately ends in necessary premises, and that these necessary premises are six in number, either in reality or in judgment. Then he defines awwaliyyat as propositions that the intellect grasps immediately upon understanding the subject and predicate terms, without needing any further proof. He gives two examples: 'One is half of two' and 'The whole is greater than the part.' These are known to be true as soon as the terms are understood. Then he discusses a linguistic issue regarding the pronunciation of the term. Some scholars prefer the pronunciation 'أوليات' (with lightened ya), treating it as a plural of 'أولى' (more fitting). However, the common pronunciation is 'أوليّات' (with shadda on waw and ya), which is also correct, treating it as a relative adjective from 'أول' (first), because these propositions are known from the very first moment the terms are conceived. He concludes that this latter form is the one that should be used in the matn because it conforms to the meter (weight) of the poem (likely referring to the matn text that is in verse).

Key Takeaway
Awwaliyyat are self-evident propositions that require no proof beyond conceptualizing their terms, and the term itself is derived from 'first' (awwal) because they are known at first glance.

Understanding Mushahadat, Mahsusat, and Propositions with Inherent Proofs
Author defines inner and outer perception and introduces a new category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mushahadat, mahsusat, and qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha, and explain why the latter is considered theoretically necessary.

Core Idea
Some propositions appear self-evident because their proof is always present in the mind, yet they are actually theoretical.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the difference between inner and outer perception, and introduces a third category that seems necessary but is not. This classification is essential for understanding how the intellect gains certainty in different types of knowledge, especially in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of perception
Find the definitions of mushahadat (inner sense) and mahsusat (outer sense) in the Arabic. Note how they are contrasted: one is through the inner sense (e.g., feeling hunger as painful), the other through external senses (e.g., seeing the sun as bright).
Both are perceived by the intellect, but the sense modality differs.
2
Understand the conventional naming
The commentator states that the specific naming is only a convention by the author and Ibn al-Hajib. Otherwise, the terms are interchangeable. This shows that categories in logic can be relative to a particular system.
3
Recognize the alternative classification
Some scholars combine mushahadat and mahsusat into one category, and replace the sixth category with 'propositions whose proofs accompany them' (qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha). Identify why they do this: because the proof is always present in the mind.
This alternative reflects a different way of slicing the epistemic landscape.
4
Analyze the example of 'four is even'
The example given is 'Four is even'. The middle term 'divisible into two equal parts' is immediately present when you think of four. See how this makes the proposition seem necessary, even though it technically requires a syllogism.
The middle term never leaves the mind, giving an illusion of self-evidence.
5
Determine why it is actually theoretical
The commentator clarifies that 'propositions whose proofs accompany them' are in reality theoretical because they depend on a syllogism. They are counted among necessary premises only because the syllogism is always present, not because they are self-evident.
This nuance is important to avoid conflating apparent necessity with genuine axiomatic truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (مُشَاهَدَاتِ) هِيَ : الْقَضَايَا الَّتِي يُدْرِكُهَا الْعَقْلُ بِسَبَبِ الْمُشَاهَدَةِ بِالْحِسِّ الْبَاطِنِي، كَقَوْلِكَ : الْجُوعُ مُؤْلِمٌ. وَأَمَّا الْقَضَايَا الَّتِي يُدْرِكُهَا الْعَقْلُ بِسَبَبِ الْمُشَاهَدَةِ بِالْحِسِّ الظَّاهِرِ فَهِيَ الْمَحْسُوسَاتُ، وَهِيَ السَّادِسَةُ فِي كَلَامِ الْمُصَنِّفِ. لَكِنْ تَسْمِيَةُ الْأُولَى بِالْمُشَاهَدَاتِ وَالثَّانِيَةِ بِالْمَحْسُوسَاتِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ اصْطِلَاحٌ لِلْمُصَنِّفِ وَابْنِ الْحَاجِبِ وَمَنْ وَافَقَهُمَا، وَإِلَّا فَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا يُسَمَّى بِاسْمِ الْآخَرِ. وَلِذَلِكَ جَعَلَهُمَا بَعْضُ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ قِسْمًا وَاحِدًا، وَجَعَلَ الْقِسْمَ السَّادِسَ : «الْقَضَايَا الَّتِي قِيَاسَاتُهَا مَعَهَا» ، وَهِيَ : مَا يُدْرِكُهَا الْعَقْلُ بِوَاسِطَةٍ لَا تَغِيبُ عَنِ الذِّهْنِ عِنْدَ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : الْأَرْبَعَةُ زَوْجٌ، فَإِنَّ الْعَقْلَ يُدْرِكُ ذَلِكَ بِوَاسِطَةٍ لَا تَغِيبُ عَنِ الذِّهْنِ عِنْدَ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، وَتِلْكَ الْوَاسِطَةُ : أَنَّ الْأَرْبَعَةَ تَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى مُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ، وَكُلُّ مُنْقَسِمٍ إِلَى مُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ زَوْجٌ. وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَذْكُرِ الْمُصَنِّفُ هَذَا الْقِسْمَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا تَكَلَّمَ عَنِ الضَّرُورِيَّاتِ، وَهُوَ فِي الْحَقِيقَةِ مِنَ النَّظَرِيَّاتِ، وَإِنَّمَا عَدَّهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الضَّرُورِيَّاتِ لِأَنَّ قَضَايَاهُ لَمَّا كَانَتْ قِيَاسَاتُهَا لَا تَغِيبُ عَنِ الذِّهْنِ عِنْدَ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ صَارَتْ كَأَنَّهَا ضَرُورِيَّةٌ. وَعُلِمَ مِنْ هَذَا أَنَّ الْعَدَدَ عَلَى كُلِّ سِتَّةٌ، فَتَفَطَنْ.
His statement: (Mushahadat) are the propositions that the intellect perceives due to inner observation, like your saying: Hunger is painful. As for the propositions that the intellect perceives due to observation by the outer sense, they are the mahsusat, and they are the sixth in the author's speech. However, naming the first as mushahadat and the second as mahsusat is only a convention of the author and Ibn al-Hajib and those who agree with them; otherwise each of them is called by the name of the other. Therefore some verifying scholars made them one category, and made the sixth category 'the propositions whose proofs are present with them', i.e., what the intellect perceives by means of a middle that does not disappear from the mind when conceptualizing the two terms; like your saying: Four is even. For the intellect perceives that by means of a middle that does not disappear from the mind when conceptualizing the two terms, and that middle is: Four divides into two equal parts, and everything that divides into two equal parts is even. And the author only mentioned this category because he only spoke about necessary premises, while in reality it is from the theoretical premises, but many counted it among the necessary premises because its propositions have proofs that do not disappear from the mind when conceptualizing the two terms, so they become as if necessary. And it is known from this that the number is six in all, so be aware.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the enumeration of necessary premises. It defines mushahadat (inner perception) as propositions perceived by the intellect through inner sense, such as the feeling that hunger is painful. It distinguishes these from mahsusat (outer perception), which are perceived through the five external senses, and notes that in the author's (al-Akhdari's) classification, mahsusat are the sixth type. However, the author of this commentary explains that the naming is merely conventional; some scholars use the terms interchangeably. In fact, some scholars combine both into one category and replace the sixth category with a different type: 'propositions whose proofs accompany them' (qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha). These are propositions that, although technically requiring a syllogism, have a middle term that is immediately present to the mind once the terms are understood. The classic example is 'Four is even', which is proven by the middle term 'divisible into two equal parts'. Because the mind grasps this middle instantly, such propositions appear necessary even though they are actually theoretical. The author (of the matn) did not mention this category because he was focusing on true necessary premises. The commentator ends by reiterating that the total number of necessary premise types is six, whether one combines categories or not.

Key Terms
مُشَاهَدَات
مَحْسُوسَات
قَضَايَا قِيَاسَاتُهَا مَعَهَا

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Their proof is always present in the mind.
C) They are false.
D) They are only known by experts.

Key Takeaway
The classification of necessary premises involves nuance; some are only apparently necessary due to the immediate presence of their proof, yet they remain theoretical.

Defining Mujarrabat, Mutawatirat, and Hadasiyyat
Author defines experiential, recurrent, and intuitive propositions and notes scholarly differences on their status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mujarrabat, mutawatirat, and hadasiyyat and identify the main epistemological dispute over their classification as necessary or theoretical.

Core Idea
Certainty comes through experience, transmission, and intuition—but scholars disagree on their epistemic status.

This passage defines three of the six types of certain premises used in Islamic logic. Each has a distinct source: repeated experience, widespread transmission, or clear intuition. The commentator not only gives examples but also reports that while the author considers them necessary (badihi), many scholars view them as theoretical (nazari) or even probable. Understanding these distinctions sharpens your ability to evaluate the strength of premises in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining method for mujarrabat
Find the Arabic phrase 'ما يدركها العقل بواسطة تكراري يفيد اليقين' (what the intellect perceives by means of repetition that yields certainty). The example is that scammony purges bile.
Note that the definition emphasizes repetition as the cause of certainty.
2
Map the scholarly positions on mujarrabat
The passage lists four views: necessary (the author's view), theoretical (due to a hidden syllogism), intermediate, and probable (the view of many scholars).
This shows that even 'certain' premises can be disputed.
3
Define mutawatirat and its condition
Find the definition: 'ما يدركها العقل بواسطة السماع عن جمع يؤمن تواطؤهم على الكذب' and the condition that no specific number is required; impossibility of collusion is the criterion.
Example: the Prophet's miracles.
4
Analyze the dispute on mutawatirat
Again, note the three views: necessary, theoretical, intermediate. The commentator does not take sides, only reports.
This mirrors the structure of the previous dispute.
5
Define hadasiyyat
Find the definition: 'القضايا التي يدركها العقل بواسطة حدس بين يفيد العلم' and the example: the moon's light from the sun.
Intuition here is a clear, immediate inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (مُجَرَّبَاتٍ) هِيَ : مَا يُدْرِكُهَا الْعَقْلُ بِوَاسِطَةِ تَكْرَارٍ يُفِيدُ الْيَقِينَ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : السَّقَمُونِيَا مُسْهَلَةٌ لِلصَّفْرَاءِ. وَكَلَامُ الْمُصَنِّفِ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى أَنَّ الْمُجَرَّبَاتِ مِنَ الضَّرُورِيَّاتِ، وَجَعَلَهَا بَعْضُهُمْ مِنَ النَّظَرِيَّاتِ لِمُلَاحَظَةِ قِيَاسٍ خَفِيٍّ، وَجَعَلَهَا بَعْضُهُمْ وَاسِطَةً بَيْنَ الضَّرُورِيَّاتِ وَالنَّظَرِيَّاتِ. هَذَا وَالْمُتَّجِهُ الَّذِي دَرَجَ عَلَيْهِ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الْعُلَمَاءِ - كَمَا قَالَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ - أَنَّهَا مِنَ الظَّنِّيَّاتِ. قَوْلُهُ : (مُتَوَاتِرَاتِ) هِيَ : مَا يُدْرِكُهَا الْعَقْلُ بِوَاسِطَةِ السَّمَاعِ عَنْ جَمْعٍ يُؤْمَنُ تَوَاطُؤُهُمْ عَلَى الْكَذِبِ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : سَيِّدُنَا مُحَمَّدٌ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ ظَهَرَتِ الْمُعْجِزَةُ عَلَى يَدَيْهِ. وَالصَّحِيحُ : أَنَّهُ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ عَدَدٌ مَخْصُوصٌ، بَلِ الْمَدَارُ عَلَى كَوْنِ الْمُخْبِرِينَ يَمْتَنِعُ تَوَاطُؤُهُمْ عَلَى الْكَذِبِ، وَيَخْتَلِفُ ذَلِكَ بِاخْتِلَافِ الْوَقَائِعِ وَالْأَحْوَالِ. وَكَلَامُ الْمُصَنِّفِ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى أَنَّ الْمُتَوَاتِرَاتِ مِنَ الضَّرُورِيَّاتِ، وَجَعَلَهَا بَعْضُهُمْ مِنَ النَّظَرِيَّاتِ، وَجَعَلَهَا بَعْضُهُمْ وَاسِطَةً بَيْنَ الضَّرُورِيَّاتِ وَالنَّظَرِيَّاتِ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَحَدَسِيَّاتٍ) هِيَ : الْقَضَايَا الَّتِي يُدْرِكُهَا الْعَقْلُ بِوَاسِطَةِ حَدَسٍ بَيِّنٍ يُفِيدُ الْعِلْمَ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : نُورُ الْقَمَرِ مُسْتَفَادٌ مِنْ نُورِ الشَّمْسِ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ
His statement: (Mujarrabat) are what the intellect perceives by means of repetition that yields certainty; like your saying: Scammony is a laxative for yellow bile. And the author's statement is based on the view that mujarrabat are from the necessary premises, but some of them made them from the theoretical premises due to observing a hidden syllogism, and some made them intermediate between the necessary and theoretical. However, the correct view that many scholars have adopted - as some said - is that they are from the probable premises. His statement: (Mutawatirat) are what the intellect perceives by means of hearing from a group whose collusion on lying is safe; like your saying: Our master Muhammad (peace be upon him) miracles appeared at his hands. And the correct view is that no specific number is required; rather the criterion is that the informants are such that their collusion on lying is prevented, and that varies according to the events and conditions. And the author's statement is based on the view that mutawatirat are from the necessary premises, but some of them made them from the theoretical premises, and some made them intermediate between the necessary and theoretical. His statement: (Hadasiyyat) are the propositions that the intellect perceives by means of an evident intuition that yields knowledge; like your saying: The light of the moon is derived from the light of the sun, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage defines three more types of certain premises. First, mujarrabat (experiential) are propositions known through repeated experience that gives certainty, e.g., that scammony (a medicinal herb) purges yellow bile. The commentator notes that the author (al-Akhdari) considers these necessary, but some scholars argue they are actually theoretical because they involve a hidden syllogism (the generalization from repeated instances), and others see them as intermediate. The commentator also mentions that many scholars regard them as merely probable (zanni), not certain. Second, mutawatirat (recurrent transmission) are propositions known through hearing from a group that cannot collude on a lie, e.g., the well-known fact that miracles appeared at the hands of Prophet Muhammad. The key condition is not a specific number but the impossibility of collusion, which depends on context. Again, there is disagreement whether they are necessary or theoretical. Third, hadasiyyat (intuitive) are propositions known by a clear intuition, such as that the moon's light comes from the sun. This completes the six types of certain premises. The commentator refrains from taking a definitive stance, simply reporting the scholarly positions.

Key Terms
مُجَرَّبَات
مُتَوَاتِرَات
حَدَسِيَّات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the correct condition for accepting a mutawatir report?
A) There must be at least four witnesses.
B) There must be at least ten witnesses.
C) The informants must be so numerous that collusion on a lie is impossible.
D) The informants must all be companions of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Mujarrabat, mutawatirat, and hadasiyyat are three types of premises whose certainty is debated; while the author considers them necessary, many scholars classify them as theoretical or probable.

Are Certainties Only Necessary? Resolving an Objection
Bajuri answers an objection about theoretical certainties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how theoretical certainties reduce to necessary premises and defend the enumeration of certainties in logic texts.

Core Idea
Theoretical certainties rest on necessary premises, so they are 'as if' necessary.

In this passage, Bajuri confronts an important objection: if certainties can be derived theoretically, how can the matn limit the six types to necessary premises? He provides a crucial epistemological clarification that theoretical knowledge ultimately depends on necessary foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate the phrase 'يرد عليه' and note the objector's challenge: that certainties may be theoretical (نظرية), yet the author confined them to necessary (ضرورية).
The objection assumes theoretical certainties are distinct from necessary ones.
2
Trace the reduction argument
Find the response starting with 'ويجاب'. Bajuri argues that theoretical certainties must ultimately end in necessary premises, so they become 'as if necessary' (كأنها ضرورية). Understand the logical flow: every theoretical chain terminates in axioms.
This is not denying theoretical certainty but showing its dependence.
3
Apply the principle
Consider a typical Kalam proof: 'The world is changing; whatever changes is created; therefore the world is created.' Identify the necessary premises (e.g., 'whatever changes is created') and see how the conclusion becomes 'as if necessary' through reduction.
The matn's enumeration remains valid because it lists directly evident premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (فَتِلْكَ جُمْلَةُ اليَقِيْنِيَّاتِ) يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ : أَنَّ اليَقِينِيَّاتِ قَدْ تَكُونُ نَظَرِيَّةٌ، فَكَيْفَ يَحْصُرُهَا فِي الضَّرُورِيَّاتِ؟ وَيُجَابُ : بِأَنَّهُ (۱) لَمَّا كَانَتِ النَّظَرِيَّاتُ لَا بُدَّ وَأَنْ تَنْتَهِي لِلضَّرُورِيَّاتِ ، صَارَتْ كَأَنَّهَا ضَرُورِيَّةٌ كَمَا مَرَّ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَفِي دَلالَةِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ عَلَى النَّتَيْجَةِ ... إلخ) أَيْ: وَفِي إِفَادَةِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ لِلشَّيجَةِ ... إلخ، : كَذَا قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ، وَهُوَ الأَنْسَبُ (۲) بِكَلامِ المَتْنِ (۳) مِمَّا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ المَلوِيُّ) مِنْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ بـ «الدَّلَالَةِ»: الارْتِبَاطُ، وَفِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ حَذْفٌ؛ وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : وَفِي دَلَالَةِ العِلْمِ أَوِ الظَّنِّ بِالمُقَدِّمَاتِ عَلَى العِلْمِ أَوِ الظَّنِّ بِالنَّتِيجَةِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ.
His statement: ('That is the totality of certainties') It is objected to him: That certainties may be theoretical, so how does he confine them to the necessary? And it is answered: That since the theoretical must ultimately lead to the necessary, they become as if they are necessary, as passed. His statement: ('And in the indication of the premises to the conclusion ... etc.') i.e., and in the making known [ifada] of the premises of the conclusion ... etc., thus said some, and it is more appropriate to the wording of the matn than what al-Mallawi indicated, that the intended meaning of 'indication' is relation [irtibat], and in the author's statement there is ellipsis; and the implied [meaning] is: and in the indication of knowledge or probability by the premises to knowledge or probability by the conclusion, so reflect.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage deals with two related points. First, an objection is raised against the matn's characterization of the six types as 'the totality of certainties'. The objector notes that certainties can also be theoretical (nazari), i.e., derived through reasoning, so how can the author limit them to necessary (daruri) premises? The answer is that theoretical certainties must ultimately rest on necessary premises; they are so obvious in their syllogistic structure that they become 'as if' necessary. Thus, the enumeration is justified. This is a key epistemological clarification: the division is not exhaustive of all certainties in a strict sense, but of those that are immediate or quasi-immediate. Second, the text turns to the phrase from the matn: 'And in the indication of the premises to the conclusion there is a disagreement coming'. Here Bajuri interprets 'indication' (dalala) as 'making known' (ifada), i.e., the premises make the conclusion known. He notes that this interpretation is more fitting to the matn than the view of al-Mallawi, who took 'indication' as mere 'relation' (irtibat). Furthermore, there is an ellipsis in the author's statement; the complete meaning is: 'And in the indication of knowledge or probability by the premises to knowledge or probability by the conclusion.' This means that the premises do not directly entail the conclusion, but rather our knowledge of the premises leads to knowledge of the conclusion. Bajuri ends with 'fata'ammal' (so reflect), indicating that the reader should ponder the precision of this formulation.

Key Terms
يقينيات
ضروريات
نظريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the objection that certainties can be theoretical answered?
A) Theoretical certainties are not real certainties.
B) Theoretical certainties are reducible to necessary ones and thus appear necessary.
C) The author intended only necessary certainties, excluding theoretical.
D) The objection is dismissed as irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Theoretical certainties are not an independent category—they depend on necessary premises and are thus 'as if' necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following statement: 'The world is created because it changes; change is a property of created things.' Identify the necessary premises and the theoretical conclusion.
?
In a fiqh context, the ruling that 'alcohol is haram' is based on the necessary premise 'khamr is haram' and the theoretical link that 'all intoxicants are khamr by analogy.' How does the reduction principle apply?

Interpreting Indication (Dalala): Making Known vs. Mere Relation
Bajuri explains the meaning of 'dalala' and an ellipsis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two interpretations of 'dalala' (indication) in logical premises and identify the ellipsis in the matn.

Core Idea
'Indication' means the premises make the conclusion known, not just a relation.

Bajuri refines the term 'dalala' in the matn. While al-Mallawi understood it as mere relation (irtibat), Bajuri argues that the more fitting interpretation is 'making known' (ifada). He also notes an ellipsis: the complete idea is that knowledge (or probability) of premises indicates knowledge (or probability) of the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Find the phrase 'مِمَّا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ المَلوِيُّ' and note that al-Mallawi interpreted 'dalala' as mere relation (irtibat). Then locate Bajuri's preferred interpretation: 'ifada' (making known).
Bajuri explicitly says 'huwa al-anasab' (it is more appropriate).
2
Evaluate which fits the matn
Bajuri argues that 'ifada' is more appropriate to the wording of the matn. Consider why: the matn discusses premises leading to a conclusion, which is an epistemic process, not just a static relation. Reflect on the difference.
The matn is about how premises generate knowledge of the conclusion.
3
Reconstruct the ellipsis
Bajuri notes an ellipsis: 'وفي دلالة العلم أو الظن بالمقدمات على العلم أو الظن بالنتيجة'. This means that the indication is from our knowledge (or probability) of the premises to our knowledge (or probability) of the conclusion. Understand why this ellipsis matters for precision.
The ellipsis clarifies that premises themselves do not indicate; it is our epistemic state.

Source Text

The Basmala: Declarative or Performative?
Author presents an objection by al-Safawi on the basmala sentence type and gives an answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the logical distinction between declarative (khabariyya) and performative (insha'iyya) sentences and apply it to the basmala debate.

Core Idea
The basmala is not a report but a performance creating a relation with Allah's name.

This passage presents a dialectical problem regarding the basmala ('Bismillah'). The objection forces us to decide whether it is a declarative (true/false) or performative (enacting) sentence. Each choice initially seems problematic. The answer by Abu Hafs al-Fasi clarifies that the basmala is performative, but its function is to create the ta'alluq (relation) of seeking help or accompaniment, not to perform the entire action. This lesson will walk you through the logical structure of the objection and answer, sharpening your understanding of how sentence types function in Islamic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two options
Recognize that every sentence is either khabariyya (declarative) or insha'iyya (performative). The basmala must be one.
2
Examine the problem with khabariyya
If it is declarative, its truth requires that the action (e.g., eating) is already being done with the name, independent of the utterance. But the basmala is meant to accompany the action.
3
Examine the problem with insha'iyya
If it is performative, uttering it should immediately bring about its meaning. But saying 'Bismillah' does not actually perform the action (e.g., it does not slaughter).
4
Study the answer
The answer chooses insha'iyya but refines it: the basmala creates the ta'alluq (seeking help/accompaniment) which is the meaning of the preposition 'bi'. The action itself is not the direct meaning; the basmala is meant to accompany the action, not to enact it.
5
Apply to examples
Consider the difference between saying 'I begin in the name of Allah' (general, no problem) versus 'I slaughter in the name of Allah' (specific, problematic if ta'alluq is specific). The answer resolves by making the ta'alluq the primary intent.

الكَلَامُ عَلَى مُقَدِّمَةِ النَّاظِمِ بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ (1) الحَمْدُ للهِ الَّذِي قَدْ أَخْرَجَا نَتَائِجَ الفِكْرِ لِأَرْبَابِ الحِجَا قوله: (بِسْمِ اللَّهِ الكلامُ في البسملة بحر زاخر، غاص في لجج بحره الأوائل والأواخر، واستخرجوا منه جواهر تزري بالنُّجوم الزَّوَاهر، فلنقتصر على إشكال أورده الفاضل الشيخ عيسى الصَّفوي شيخ العبادي رحمهما الله على تقدير المتعلق خاصا مناسباً لما جعلتِ التّسمية مبدأ له، كما ذهب إليه الزَّمخشري ومَن وافقه. حاصله: أنَّ جملة البسملة لا بدَّ أن تكون خبريَّةٌ أو إنشائيَّةٌ؛ ضرورة انحصار الكلام في الخبر والإنشاء: - فإن كانت خبريَّةً توجّه عليه: أنَّ من لازم الخبر الصَّادق أن يتحقق مدلوله في نفس الأمر بدونه، ويكون هو حكاية عنه، كما صرح بذلك العلامة التفتازاني وغيره، وجملة البسملة ليست بهذه الصفة؛ لأنَّ مِن تتمَّة مدلولها مصاحبة الاسم والاستعانة به، ولا يقع ذلك إلَّا بالتلفظ به. - وإن كانت إنشائيَّةً توجّه عليه أنَّ مِن لازم الإنشاء أن يقارن وجوده حصول مدلوله، وهذه الجملة ليست كذلك؛ لأنَّ مدلولها وهو حصول التأليف مصحوباً بالاسم الشَّريف لا يقع بمجرَّد النُّطق بها؛ لأنَّ نحو: «الأكل، والسفر والذَّبح ممَّا ليس بقول لا يحصل بالبسملة، فكيف يقدر مثلاً: «أَذْبَحُ» أو: «أُسَافِرُ بِاسْمِ اللهِ» بقصد الإنشاء. وإنَّما أورد الإشكال على تقدير المتعلق خاصا؛ لأنَّه لا إشكال في الإنشائية على تقديره عاما؛ لحصول الابتداء بالبسملة بمجرد النُّطق بها. وأجاب عن هذا الإشكال شيخ بعض شيوخنا أبو حفص الفاسي عملته بما نصه: أقول: يجاب باختيار أنَّها إنشائيَّةٌ، وأنَّها لإنشاء متعلّقها الذي هو الاستعانة أو المصاحبة؛ يعني: اللَّذين هما مدلول «الباء»، ولا يلزم أن يكون الأصل الذي هو الجملة غير مقصود أصلاً، بل يكون مقصوداً تبعاً، وإذا كانتِ الجملُ الخبريَّةُ مع بقائها على حالها يكون المقصود الأصلي منها المتعلّق الذي قيد به مضمونها
Discussion on the Introduction of the Poet. In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. (1) Praise be to Allah who brought forth the products of thought for the people of intelligence. His saying: 'In the name of Allah' – the discussion on the basmala is a vast ocean; earlier and later scholars have dived into its depths and extracted jewels that surpass the shining stars. Let us restrict ourselves to an objection raised by the virtuous Shaykh Isa al-Safawi, teacher of al-Abbadi, may Allah have mercy on them, regarding the estimation of the ta'alluq as specific, as appropriate to that for which the naming (basmala) was made a beginning, as al-Zamakhshari and those who agreed with him held. Its summary: The basmala sentence must be either khabariyya (declarative) or insha'iyya (performative), due to the necessary division of speech into declarative and performative. If it is khabariyya, then it is objected against it that one of the implications of a true declarative is that its meaning is realized in actual fact without it, and it is a narration about it, as al-Taftazani and others stated. But the basmala sentence is not of this description, because part of its complete meaning is the accompanying of the name and seeking help by it, and that does not occur except by uttering it. If it is insha'iyya, then it is objected against it that one of the implications of a performative is that its existence accompanies the occurrence of its meaning, but this sentence is not so, because its meaning – which is the occurrence of the composition accompanied by the noble name – does not occur by merely pronouncing it, because things like eating, traveling, and slaughtering, which are not speech, are not achieved by the basmala. So how can one estimate, for example, 'I slaughter' or 'I travel in the name of Allah' with the intent of performative? He only raised the objection on the estimation of the ta'alluq as specific, because there is no problem with the insha'iyya on the estimation of it as general, since the beginning with the basmala occurs by merely pronouncing it. And a teacher of some of our teachers, Abu Hafs al-Fasi (may Allah have mercy on him), answered this objection with his text: I say: It is answered by choosing that it is insha'iyya, and that it is for the creation of its ta'alluq, which is seeking help or accompaniment, i.e., those two are the meaning of the letter 'ba'. And it is not necessary that the original sentence is not intended at all; rather, it is intended secondarily. And when declarative sentences, while remaining as they are, have their primary intended meaning as the ta'alluq that restricts their content.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical problem regarding the basmala ('Bismillah'). The author introduces an objection by Shaykh Isa al-Safawi. The objection rests on the fact that any sentence must be either khabariyya (declarative) or insha'iyya (performative). If we take the basmala as declarative, then its truth would require that its meaning (the act of accompanying or seeking help by the name) actually occurs without the sentence itself being the cause, but that meaning only occurs upon uttering the sentence. If we take it as performative, then its occurrence should immediately bring about its meaning, but the meaning (e.g., the act of slaughtering) does not happen by merely saying the basmala. The objection is specifically about the ta'alluq being 'specific', meaning the action is specified (e.g., 'I slaughter in the name of Allah'). If the ta'alluq were 'general', i.e., the basmala simply means 'I begin in the name of Allah', then the performative reading would work. The answer by Abu Hafs al-Fasi resolves this by choosing the performative interpretation and clarifying that the basmala creates its ta'alluq (the seeking of help or accompaniment), which is the meaning of the 'ba'. The original sentence (e.g., 'I slaughter in the name of Allah') is not the primary intent; the basmala itself creates the relation. The discussion also notes that in declarative sentences, the main intent can be the ta'alluq that restricts the content, similar to how 'I am doing an action in the name of Allah' focuses on the relation rather than the action itself.

Key Terms
بسملة (basmala): The phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' (In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful). [Note: Stable term.]
خبرية (khabariyya): A declarative sentence that can be true or false, reporting a state of affairs. [Note: Standard Arabic grammar term; no significant shift.]
إنشائية (insha'iyya): A performative sentence that enacts its content by being uttered, e.g., commands, requests, contracts. [Note: Standard Arabic grammar term; no significant shift.]
متعلق (ta'alluq): The attachment or relation indicated by the preposition 'bi' in 'bismillah', which can be isti'ana (seeking help) or musahaba (accompaniment). [Note: In logic, ta'alluq often means the relation of a predicate to its subject; here it is specifically the grammatical attachment of the basmala's preposition.]

A) Because it requires the action to be already performed without the utterance causing it.

Key Takeaway
The basmala is a performative sentence that creates the relation of seeking help or accompaniment with the name of Allah, not a report about an action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
